Wudu in Weak-Flowing Water and Small Basins
Stating rulings for wudu with weak-flowing water and small basins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi ruling for wudu in weak-flowing water and small basins, and identify the condition that governs its validity.

Core Idea
Wudu is valid as long as used water exits immediately – basin size is irrelevant.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah' clarifies two practical scenarios for wudu: using water with a slow current and performing wudu in a small basin with inflow and outflow. The key legal principle is the status of used water (ghusalah): according to the mufta bihi, it is pure (tahir) but not purifying (tahur). Consequently, wudu remains valid unless used water dominates. The author resolves an intra-Hanafi disagreement on basin size, affirming that the operative ruling does not require specific dimensions as long as the water flows continuously.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers (1) weak-flowing water (e.g., a stream) and (2) a small basin (hawd) with water entering and exiting. Understand each case separately.
2
Analyze the ruling on used water (ghusalah)
Ghusalah is the water that drips from limbs during wudu. The passage explains that the absolute ruling (based on a narration) treats it as impure, but the mufta bihi holds it as pure but not purifying. This nuance affects when precaution is needed.
The mufta bihi is the operative position for fatwa.
3
Determine conditions for weak-flowing water
For weak-flowing water, one must sit upstream (so that used water flows away) or pause between washes to allow used water to dissipate. The condition is that used water should not be used again in the wudu.
If used water dominates, wudu becomes invalid.
4
Examine the small basin ruling
For a basin where water enters and exits continuously, wudu is permissible in all parts. The passage notes that some earlier scholars required a size limit (4×4 cubits), but the mufta bihi rejects this estimation because the continuous flow ensures used water does not stagnate.
Al-Tatarkhaniyyah declares the size estimation unnecessary.
5
Apply the principle to practical scenarios
Walking through the reasoning: what matters is immediate removal of used water, not dimensions. Thus a modern sink with running water would be treated like a basin with inflow/outflow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل ماءٍ ضعيف الجريان إذا توضأ به يجب أن يجلس بحيث لا يستعمل غُسالته ، أو يمكث بين الغرفتين مقدار ما يذهب . غُسالته. وإذا كمان الحوض صغيراً يدخلُ فيه الماء من جانب، ويخرج من جانب يجوز الوضوء في جميع جوانبه، وعليه الفتوى من غير تفصيل" بين أن يكون أربعاً في أربع أو أقل، فيجوز، أو أكثر فلا يجوز. [١] قوله : يجب ؛ هذا الحكم بإطلاقِهِ مبني على رواية نجاسة الماء المستعمل، وأمّا على المفتى به من أنه طاهر غير طهور، فهو مقيد بما إذا ظن غلبة المستعمل، فإنّهم صرحوا أنّ المستعمل على المفتى به إذا اختلط بالماء الطهور، يجوز بـه الوضوء ما لم يغلب المستعمل عليه. كذا في «فتح القدير [٢]قوله : بحيث ؛ أي يجلس في مكان لا يستعمل فيه غُسالته بالضم – ما غسلت به الشيء - أي ما تقاطر من أعضائه واختلط بالماء الضعيف الجريان، بأن يجلس على مورد الماء ومنبعه، فإنّه إذا جلس إلى جهة سيل الماء وجريانه يلتزم استعمال المتقاطر، وحينئذ يجب عليه أن يمكث بين الغرفتين مقدار ما يذهب المتقاطر مع الماء. [٣] قوله : من غير تفصيل ... الخ ؛ ذكر في «مجموع النوازل عن الفقيه أبي الحسن ) ه أنه إن كان أربعاً في أربع فما دونه يجوز التوضؤ به، وإن كان أكثر منه لا يجوز إلا في موضع دخول الماء وخروجه ؛ لأنه في الوجه الأول ما يقع فيه من المستعمل يخرج من ساعته ، ولا يستقر فيه ولا كذلك الوجه الثاني، والأصح أن هذا التقدير غير لازم. كذا في «التاتارخانية».
And every weak-flowing water, if one performs ablution with it, he must sit such that he does not use his used water, or he pauses between the two washes for the amount of time it takes for the used water to go away. And if the basin is small, water enters from one side and exits from another, it is permissible to perform ablution in all its sides, and upon this is the fatwa without distinction of whether it is four by four or less, then it is permissible, or more then it is not permissible. [1] His saying: 'must'; this ruling in its absolute form is based on the narration that used water is impure. But according to the fatwa that it is pure but not purifying, it is restricted to when one assumes the used water is dominant. For they explicitly stated that used water, according to the mufta bihi, when mixed with purifying water, ablution is permissible with it as long as the used water does not dominate. Thus in 'Fath al-Qadir'. [2] His saying: 'such that'; i.e., he sits in a place where he does not use his used water—with dammah on ghayn—that with which something is washed—i.e., what drips from his limbs and mixes with the weak-flowing water. By sitting at the source and origin of the water, because if he sits towards the direction of the flow and current, he will inevitably use the dripping water. And then he must pause between the two washes for the amount of time it takes for the dripping water to go with the flow. [3] His saying: 'without distinction...'; It is mentioned in 'Majmu' al-Nawazil' from al-Faqih Abu al-Hasan that if it is four by four or less, ablution with it is permissible, and if it is more than that, it is not permissible except at the entry and exit points of the water; because in the first case, what falls into it of used water exits immediately and does not settle, unlike the second case. And the most correct is that this estimation is not necessary. Thus in 'al-Tatarkhaniyyah'.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage gives practical rulings for using weak-flowing water for wudu. The author first states that one must sit upstream or pause to avoid using water that has already been used (ghusalah). The justification is to prevent using impure water according to one narration, but the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that used water is pure but not purifying; thus the restriction applies only when used water is dominant. The second ruling addresses a small basin (hawd) where water flows in and out continuously. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) allows wudu anywhere around the basin without considering size. However, some earlier scholars specified a size limit of 4x4 cubits; if larger, only at entry/exit. The commentary from 'al-Tatarkhaniyyah' declares the estimation non-binding, affirming the general permissibility as long as used water exits immediately.

Key Terms
غسالة (ghusalah): Used water that drips from limbs during wudu; it is considered pure but not purifying in the mufta bihi view.
حوض (hawd): A basin or tank; in this context, a small water container where water flows in and out.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) in the passage, what is the main condition for valid wudu in a small basin (hawd)?
A) The basin must be exactly 4×4 cubits
B) The basin must have water entering from one side and exiting from another so used water flows out
C) The basin must be completely drained after each use
D) The basin must be made of stone

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi position allows wudu in any small basin with continuous flow, regardless of size, as long as used water exits immediately.

Ruling on Avoiding Used Water (Ghusālah) in Weak-Flowing Water
First ruling: weak-flowing water and ghusālah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on wuḍūʾ in weak-flowing water and identify when the obligation to avoid ghusālah applies according to the muftā bihi.

Core Idea
Avoiding used water is only wajib if it dominates; otherwise mixing is permissible.

This passage addresses a common scenario: performing wuḍūʾ in a stream or weak-flowing water. The base text imposes an obligation to sit upstream or wait, but the commentator explains that this is based on a weaker view. The relied-upon position allows more flexibility, as we will see.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its basic ruling
The act is wuḍūʾ in weak-flowing water. The base text says 'must' (yajib) to avoid using ghusālah, meaning the water that drips from limbs after washing.
The ruling is presented as a wajib.
2
Locate the underlying dispute
The commentator states this absolute obligation is based on the narration that used water (musta'mal) is impure (najis). But the muftā bihi is that musta'mal is pure but not purifying.
The later view makes the obligation conditional.
3
Apply the muftā bihi condition
According to the relied-upon view, the obligation only applies if one fears the used water will dominate the fresh water. Otherwise, mixing is permissible.
One must assess the proportion of used water.
4
Summarize the practical guidance
In weak-flowing water, it is recommended to sit at the source or wait between rinses to ensure ghusālah flows away, but it is not strictly required unless used water is likely to dominate.
This reflects the tension between precaution and facilitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل ماءٍ ضعيف الجريان إذا توضأ به يجب أن يجلس بحيث لا يستعمل غُسالته ، أو يمكث بين الغرفتين مقدار ما يذهب غُسالته.
وإذا كان الحوض صغيراً يدخل فيه الماء من جانب، ويخرج من جانب يجوز الوضوء في جميع جوانبه، وعليه الفتوى من غير تفصيل بين أن يكون أربعاً في أربع أو أقل، فيجوز، أو أكثر فلا يجوز.
[١] قوله : يجب ؛ هذا الحكم بإطلاقِهِ مبني على رواية نجاسة الماء المستعمل، وأمّا على المفتى به من أنه طاهر غير طهور، فهو مقيد بما إذا ظن غلبة المستعمل، فإنّهم صرحوا أنّ المستعمل على المفتى به إذا اختلط بالماء الطهور، يجوز بـه الوضوء ما لم يغلب المستعمل عليه. كذا في «فتح القدير [٢]قوله : بحيث ؛ أي يجلس في مكان لا يستعمل فيه غُسالته بالضم – ما غسلت به الشيء - أي ما تقاطر من أعضائه واختلط بالماء الضعيف الجريان، بأن يجلس على مورد الماء ومنبعه، فإنّه إذا جلس إلى جهة سيل الماء وجريانه يلتزم استعمال المتقاطر، وحينئذ يجب عليه أن يمكث بين الغرفتين مقدار ما يذهب المتقاطر مع الماء. [٣] قوله : من غير تفصيل ... الخ ؛ ذكر في «مجموع النوازل عن الفقيه أبي الحسن ) ه أنه إن كان أربعاً في أربع فما دونه يجوز التوضؤ به، وإن كان أكثر منه لا يجوز إلا في موضع دخول الماء وخروجه ؛ لأنه في الوجه الأول ما يقع فيه من المستعمل يخرج من ساعته ، ولا يستقر فيه ولا كذلك الوجه الثاني، والأصح أن هذا التقدير غير لازم. كذا في «التاتارخانية».
Every weak-flowing water, if one performs ablution with it, one must sit so that he does not use his used water, or pause between the two rinses for the duration that his used water goes away. And if the basin is small, water enters from one side and exits from another, it is permissible to perform ablution in all its sides, and upon this is the fatwa, without detail between it being four by four or less, then permissible, or more then not permissible. [1] His saying: 'must': This ruling in its absoluteness is based on the narration of impurity of used water. But according to the relied-upon (position) that it is pure but not purifying, it is restricted to when one thinks the used water is dominant. For they have explicitly stated that used water according to the relied-upon view, when mixed with purifying water, ablution is permissible with it as long as the used water does not dominate over it. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. [2] His saying: 'such that': i.e., he sits in a place where he does not use his used water (with damm – what one washed something with), i.e., what drips from his limbs and mixes with the weak-flowing water, by sitting at the source of the water. For if he sits towards the direction of the flow, he will necessarily use the dripping water. Then he must pause between the two rinses for the duration that the dripping water goes away with the water. [3] His saying: 'without detail...etc.': It is mentioned in Majmu' al-Nawazil from al-Faqih Abu al-Hasan [؟] that if it is four by four or less, ablution with it is permissible, and if more, it is not permissible except at the place of entry and exit of water; because in the first case, what falls into it of used water comes out immediately and does not settle, and not so in the second case. And the most correct is that this specification is not necessary. Thus in al-Tatarkhaniyya.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is an expansion of the base text (matn) of al-Wiqayah. It discusses two related rulings: first, the proper method for performing wuḍūʾ in weak-flowing water to avoid reusing the water that has already touched the limbs (ghusālah). The base text states it is incumbent (yajib) to sit upstream or wait between rinses. However, the commentator clarifies that this ruling is based on a weaker transmission (riwayah) that considers used water (musta'mal) as impure (najis). The relied-upon position (muftā bihi) is that used water is pure (tāhir) but not purifying (ghayr tāhur). Therefore, the obligation only applies if one fears the used water will dominate; otherwise, it is permissible. The second rule concerns a small basin (hawd) with continuous flow. The base text says it is permissible in all parts without specification of size. The commentator notes an opinion from Majmu' al-Nawazil limiting it to 4x4 or less, but declares the more correct view (asahh) that size is not a condition; rather, the criterion is whether used water exits immediately. Thus the fatwa is on the general permissibility.

Key Terms
مستعمل (musta'mal): Water that has been used for wuḍūʾ or ghusl, i.e., water that has touched a limb with the intention of purification.
غسالة (ghusālah): The water that drips from the limbs during wuḍūʾ or ghusl after washing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the muftā bihi in the Ḥanafī school, what triggers the obligation to avoid ghusālah in weak-flowing water?
A) Any amount of ghusālah mixing.
B) When the used water is likely to dominate the fresh water.
C) When the basin is smaller than 4×4 cubits.
D) When the water is still, not flowing.

Key Takeaway
In the relied-upon Hanafī view, mixing used water with flowing water is permissible unless the used water dominates.

Ruling on the Small Basin (Ḥawḍ) with Inlet and Outlet
Second ruling: small basin with flow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Ḥanafī ruling on wuḍūʾ in a basin with continuous flow, and evaluate the earlier size restriction of 4×4 cubits.

Core Idea
Continuous flow removes used water; size of basin is irrelevant if water exits immediately.

The second part of the passage deals with a common fixture in mosques: a small basin for wuḍūʾ where water enters from one side and exits from another. The base text declares it permissible in all sides without specifying dimensions. The commentator notes an earlier view that limited it to 4×4, but declares it non-binding. This lesson unpacks that development.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and the issue
The object is a small basin (ḥawḍ) with continuous inflow and outflow. The issue is whether wuḍūʾ is permissible in all parts or only near the inlet/outlet.
The basin is designed to circulate water.
2
State the base ruling
The base text says: if the basin is small and water flows in and out, it is permissible to perform wuḍūʾ in all its sides. This is the operative fatwa.
No size limit is mentioned in the base text.
3
Examine the earlier restriction
An opinion from Majmūʿ al-Nawāzil limited permissibility to basins 4×4 cubits or smaller; larger basins only at the inlet/outlet. The reason is that in larger basins, used water settles and does not exit immediately.
This opinion is based on a practical observation about water circulation.
4
Apply the asahh (correct) view
The commentator declares that this specific size (4×4) is not necessary (ghayr lāzim). The correct criterion is whether the flow carries away the used water immediately, regardless of size.
Thus, a large basin with adequate flow is also permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل ماءٍ ضعيف الجريان إذا توضأ به يجب أن يجلس بحيث لا يستعمل غُسالته ، أو يمكث بين الغرفتين مقدار ما يذهب غُسالته.
وإذا كان الحوض صغيراً يدخل فيه الماء من جانب، ويخرج من جانب يجوز الوضوء في جميع جوانبه، وعليه الفتوى من غير تفصيل بين أن يكون أربعاً في أربع أو أقل، فيجوز، أو أكثر فلا يجوز.
[١] قوله : يجب ؛ هذا الحكم بإطلاقِهِ مبني على رواية نجاسة الماء المستعمل، وأمّا على المفتى به من أنه طاهر غير طهور، فهو مقيد بما إذا ظن غلبة المستعمل، فإنّهم صرحوا أنّ المستعمل على المفتى به إذا اختلط بالماء الطهور، يجوز بـه الوضوء ما لم يغلب المستعمل عليه. كذا في «فتح القدير [٢]قوله : بحيث ؛ أي يجلس في مكان لا يستعمل فيه غُسالته بالضم – ما غسلت به الشيء - أي ما تقاطر من أعضائه واختلط بالماء الضعيف الجريان، بأن يجلس على مورد الماء ومنبعه، فإنّه إذا جلس إلى جهة سيل الماء وجريانه يلتزم استعمال المتقاطر، وحينئذ يجب عليه أن يمكث بين الغرفتين مقدار ما يذهب المتقاطر مع الماء. [٣] قوله : من غير تفصيل ... الخ ؛ ذكر في «مجموع النوازل عن الفقيه أبي الحسن ) ه أنه إن كان أربعاً في أربع فما دونه يجوز التوضؤ به، وإن كان أكثر منه لا يجوز إلا في موضع دخول الماء وخروجه ؛ لأنه في الوجه الأول ما يقع فيه من المستعمل يخرج من ساعته ، ولا يستقر فيه ولا كذلك الوجه الثاني، والأصح أن هذا التقدير غير لازم. كذا في «التاتارخانية».
Every weak-flowing water, if one performs ablution with it, one must sit so that he does not use his used water, or pause between the two rinses for the duration that his used water goes away. And if the basin is small, water enters from one side and exits from another, it is permissible to perform ablution in all its sides, and upon this is the fatwa, without detail between it being four by four or less, then permissible, or more then not permissible. Key Terms

Checkpoint
According to the aṣaḥḥ view in the Ḥanafī school, what is the condition for a basin to be permissible in all its sides?
A) The basin must be 4×4 cubits or smaller.
B) The water must enter from one side and exit from another such that used water exits immediately.
C) The basin must be made of stone.
D) The water must be weak-flowing.

Key Takeaway
In a basin with continuous flow, wuḍūʾ is permissible in all parts regardless of size, as long as used water exits promptly.

Ruling on Foul Water and a Dead Dog Blocking a River
Rulings on foul water and dead dog blocking a river

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for foul water and for water flowing over a dead dog in a river, and distinguish between the majority position and Abu Yusuf's view.

Core Idea
Purity of water is presumed: foulness without known impurity is permissible; a dead dog's effect depends on relative volume.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah clarifies two common scenarios: water that smells bad from standing, and a river blocked by a dead dog. The rulings hinge on two principles: preserving the original state (istishab) and consideration of the majority (al-ghālib). An important intra-Hanafi difference appears between Imām Abū Yūsuf and the majority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues and the core rule for each
The passage presents two separate rulings: (1) water that becomes foul (intana), and (2) a dead dog blocking a river. For each, note the condition that preserves or negates purity.
The overall principle is 'preserving what was' (ibqāʾ mā kān).
2
Analyze foul water: condition for permissibility
If the foulness is known to be from impurity (najāsah), the water is not permissible. If the source is unknown, it is presumed from prolonged standing and remains pure. This relies on the report of a just Muslim or clear signs.
Certainty of impurity overrides the presumption.
3
Analyze dead dog: quantity comparison
When a dead dog blocks a river, the water that contacts it is compared to the rest. If the contacted water is less than the rest, wuḍūʾ downstream is permissible; if more, not; if equal, permissible but cautious.
This applies the principle of al-ghālib (majority).
4
Recognize the intra-madhhab dispute
Abū Yūsuf holds that as long as the water's color, taste, or smell remain unchanged, it is pure regardless of the dog's presence. The commentator notes that Ibn al-Humām preferred Abū Yūsuf's view, making it the muftā bihī.
The hadith 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure' is applied to flowing water.
5
Apply the principles to the rulings
Both rulings demonstrate how Hanafi fiqh uses istishab (presumption of continuity) and al-ghālib (consideration of majority) to determine purity. The dispute between the majority (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf shows a difference in how strictly to apply these principles.
The practical difference (thamarah) is whether one must measure volumes or simply check properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه إذا أنتن الماء فإن علم أن نتنَهُ للنَّجاسة لا يجوز، وإلا يجوز حملاً على أنَّ نتنه لطول المكث. وإذا سد كلب عرض النهر، ويجري الماء فوقه ، إن كان ما يلاقي الكلب أقل مما لا يلاقيه يجوز الوضوء في الأسفل، وإلا لا ، قال الفقيه أبو جعفر : على هذا أدركت مشايخي الله ، و عن أبي يوسف : لا بأس بالوضوء به إذا لم يتغير أحد أوصافه.
[١]قوله : واعلم ؛ هذه المسألة من فروع قاعدة إبقاء ما كان على ما كان، أوردها توضيحاً لقول المصنّف لم ير أثره.
[٢]قوله : فإن علم ؛ بإخبارِ رجل مسلم عدل أو بعلامات دالة عليه تورث اليقين أو الظنّ، فإنّه في حكم اليقين لكونه للنجاسة. [٣] قوله : وإذا سدّ كلب ؛ يشير إلى أن قوله : «لم يرَ أثره» إنما هو في غير الجيفة ونحوها، وأما فيها فالمعتبر هو عين النجاسة، فإذا وقع كلب في النهر عرضاً وسد جريان الماء - أي كلب ميت - لكونه نجساً، وحيّ أيضاً على رواية كونه نجس العين، فإن كان ما يلاقيه من الماء أقل مما لا يلاقيه جاز الوضوء به اعتباراً للغالب، وإن كان أكثر لا يجوز، وإن كان مساوياً يجوز ، لكن الأحوط أن لا يتوضأ، كذا نقله ابن كمال لله في «الإيضاح» عن «التحفة» و«البدائع»، وعرّض به على الشارحه بأن عبارته قاصرة. [٤]قوله : وعن أبي يوسف ... الخ ؛ هذا هو الذي رجحه ابن الهمام في «فتح القدير» بأنّ الحديث : وهو «الماء طهور لا ينجسه شيء» لَمَّا حُمِلَ على الماء الجاري.
Know that if water becomes foul, if it is known that its foulness is due to impurity, it is not permissible; otherwise it is permissible, based on the presumption that its foulness is due to prolonged standing. And if a dog blocks the width of the river, and water flows over it, if what contacts the dog is less than what does not contact it, wuḍūʾ downstream is permissible; otherwise not. Al-Faqīh Abū Jaʿfar said: 'Upon this I found my teachers [may Allāh?]'. And from Abū Yūsuf: 'There is no harm in performing wuḍūʾ with it if none of its properties have changed.'
[1] His saying: 'Know': This issue is from the branches of the principle of preserving what was as it was; he brings it to clarify the author's saying 'he did not see its effect.'
[2] His saying: 'if it is known': By the report of a just Muslim man, or by signs that indicate it, producing certainty or opinion; then it is in the ruling of certainty because it is for impurity.
[3] His saying: 'And if a dog blocks': It indicates that his saying 'he did not see its effect' applies only to other than a carcass and the like. But regarding them, the consideration is the very substance of impurity. So if a dog falls into the river crosswise and blocks the flow – i.e., a dead dog – because it is impure, and also a living one according to a narration of being impure in essence, then if the water that contacts it is less than what does not, wuḍūʾ is permissible considering the majority; if more, not permissible; if equal, it is permissible, but it is more cautious not to perform wuḍūʾ. Thus Ibn Kamāl [Allāh] narrated in al-Īḍāḥ from al-Tuḥfah and al-Badāʾiʿ, and he objected to the commentator that his expression is insufficient.
[4] His saying: 'And from Abū Yūsuf... etc.': This is what Ibn al-Humām preferred in Fatḥ al-Qadīr, because the hadith: 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure' was interpreted as applying to flowing water.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the topic of water purity. It first addresses water that develops a bad smell (intana). The original purity of water is presumed (istisḥāb al-ḥāl), so if the foulness is not known to be from najāsah, it is considered due to prolonged standing and remains permissible for wuḍūʾ. Certainty of najāsah can be established by a reliable report or clear signs. The second issue is more complex: a dead dog (or living dog on one view) blocks a river, and water flows over it. The commentator explains that the general rule of 'not seeing the effect' (lam yara atharahu) from the base text applies only to subtle impurities; here a visible carcass is a clear impurity. The permissibility of wuḍūʾ downstream depends on the relative amount of water contacting the dog versus the rest. If the contacted water is less than the rest, it is permissible; if more, not; if equal, it is permissible but recommended to avoid it. This is based on the principle of considering the majority (al-ghālib). Finally, the commentator notes that Imam Abū Yūsuf held a different view: as long as the water's color, taste, and smell are unchanged, it is pure regardless of the dog's presence. Ibn al-Humām in Fatḥ al-Qadīr preferred this view because the ḥadīth 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure' applies to flowing water. The commentary thus highlights a key intra-Ḥanafī dispute.

Key Terms
نتن (intana): To become foul or putrid, used for water that develops a bad smell from stagnation.
سد (sadda): To block or obstruct; here used for a dog that blocks the width of a river.
كلب (kalb): A dog, whether dead or alive; the text considers it a source of najāsah if it obstructs the water.
غالب (ghālib): The majority or predominant part; used to determine whether the water that contacted the najāsah is less than the pure water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Ḥanafī position in this text, what is the ruling if a dead dog blocks a river and the water downstream is exactly equal in volume to the water that contacts the dog?
A) Wuḍūʾ is not permissible because the water is equal.
B) Wuḍūʾ is permissible but it is recommended to avoid it.
C) Wuḍūʾ is permissible without any caution.
D) Wuḍūʾ is permissible only if the water’s properties have not changed.

Key Takeaway
The purity of water is presumed; for a dead dog, the later preferred view (Abū Yūsuf) allows wuḍūʾ as long as no property changes, but the majority requires a volume comparison.

Rulings on Putrid Water and a Dead Dog Blocking a River
Commentary on rulings for water with uncertain najasah and a dog obstructing a river

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water with uncertain najasah and apply the principle of istishab to determine the permissibility of wudu when a dead animal blocks a river.

Core Idea
Water remains pure unless change is certainly from najasah; presumption of continuity governs.

This lesson examines two practical scenarios about water purity. The first deals with water that has become putrid from an unknown cause. The second involves a dead dog blocking a river. In both cases, the Hanafi school relies on foundational principles: the presumption of continuity (istishab) and the consideration of the majority (al-ghalib). We will trace the reasoning of the classical scholars and identify the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct scenarios
The passage covers two cases: (1) water that becomes putrid without known cause, and (2) water flowing over a dead dog that blocks a river. Recognize that each scenario has its own ruling based on principles.
The first case uses istishab; the second uses ghalib and later Abu Yusuf's view.
2
Apply istishab to putrid water
If the cause of putridity is unknown, we presume the original state — that it is pure (tahir) — because najasah is not certain. This is the principle of 'ibqa' ma kana 'ala ma kana' (presumption of continuity).
If knowledge of najasah becomes certain (e.g., by reliable report or signs), the water becomes impure.
3
Determine the majority (ghalib) in the dog case
If a dead dog blocks the river, we compare the water that touches the dog (which is contaminated) with the water that does not. If the uncontaminated part is more, wudu is permissible; if equal, it is permissible but cautious to avoid; if less, it is not permissible.
This rule is based on the principle that the majority is given weight in doubt.
4
Consider Abu Yusuf's alternative ruling
Abu Yusuf holds that as long as none of the water's three properties (color, taste, smell) change, wudu is permissible regardless of the quantity. His evidence is the hadith 'Water is pure and cannot be made impure by anything', interpreted for flowing water.
This view is simpler and avoids the need to estimate quantities.
5
Identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon ruling)
Later scholars like Ibn al-Humam and Ibn Amir Haj favor Abu Yusuf's view, and the fatwa is upon it. The reasoning is that the hadith is strong and practical needs are served.
This is an example of tarjih (preference) within the Hanafi school.

Book of Purification / Categories of Water. Know that if water becomes putrid, if it is known that its putridity is due to najasah (impurity), it is not permissible (to use it for wudu); otherwise it is permissible, assuming that its putridity is due to long standing. And if a dog blocks the width of the river, and water flows over it, then if what meets the dog is less than what does not meet it, wudu is permissible in the lower part; otherwise it is not. Abu Ja‘far said: Upon this I found my teachers. And from Abu Yusuf: There is no harm in performing wudu with it if none of its three properties (color, taste, smell) have changed. [Commentary:] His statement: 'Know' – this issue is from the branches of the principle of leaving things as they were (istiṣḥāb). He mentioned it to clarify the author's statement 'he did not see its trace.' His statement: 'if it is known' – by the report of a just Muslim man or by signs that give certainty or strong probability, it is treated as certainty because it is about najasah. His statement: 'if a dog blocks' – indicates that the author's statement 'he did not see its trace' applies only to something other than a carcass, etc.; but regarding a carcass, the impurity itself is considered. So if a dog falls into the river sideways and blocks the flow – i.e., a dead dog, since it is impure, and also a living dog according to the narration that it is inherently impure – then if the water that meets it is less than what does not meet it, wudu is permissible, considering the majority; if it is more, it is not permissible; if it is equal, it is permissible, but it is cautious not to perform wudu. This was transmitted by Ibn Kamal from ‘al-Tuhfa’ and ‘al-Bada’i‘’, and he criticized the commentator (al-Sharih) for his deficient expression. His statement: 'and from Abu Yusuf...' – this is the view that Ibn al-Humam favored in Fath al-Qadir, because the hadith 'Water is pure and cannot be made impure by anything' when interpreted as flowing water, would necessitate the permissibility of wudu from its lower part, even if the carcass takes up most of the water and does not change it. And Ibn Amir Haj also chose this in al-Halba, and the author of al-Tariqa al-Muhammadiyya said: 'The fatwa is upon it.'
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage discusses two rulings. First, regarding water that has become putrid: if it is known that the putridity is from najasah (impurity), it is not permissible to use it for wudu. If the cause is unknown, we assume it is from long standing and the water remains pure. This is based on the principle of 'presumption of continuity' (istiṣḥāb). Second, if a dead dog (or living, as some narrate) blocks part of a river but water flows over it, the validity of wudu in the lower part depends on the majority of water that does not touch the dog. If the water touching the dog is less than the rest, wudu is valid; if more, not valid; if equal, it is valid but precaution is not to perform wudu. The commentator then mentions Abu Yusuf's opinion: wudu is permissible as long as no property has changed. This is supported by the hadith 'Water is pure and cannot be made impure by anything' (when interpreted as flowing water). The mufta bihi (relied-upon opinion) is that of Abu Yusuf, as preferred by later scholars such as Ibn al-Humam.

Key Terms
إبقاء ما كان على ما كان (ibqā' mā kāna 'alā mā kāna): The legal principle of presuming continuity of the previous state (istiṣḥāb).
الغالب (al-ghālib): The majority (of water) that is unaffected by the najasah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Never

Key Takeaway
Water is presumed pure unless najasah is certain or changes in properties are observed; in case of doubt, use the majority test or Abu Yusuf's simpler rule.

Why Water Remains Pure When an Aquatic Animal Dies in It
Commentary establishing purity of water when a water-born animal dies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water in which an aquatic animal dies and explain the rationale based on the absence of flowing blood.

Core Idea
Water stays pure when a water-born animal dies because it lacks flowing blood.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains that water in which an aquatic animal like a fish or frog dies remains pure and valid for wudu. The key reason is that such animals have no flowing blood, unlike land animals whose dead bodies spread impure blood. The commentator also refutes an alternative explanation that relies on the animal dying in its element.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State clearly: water in which a water-born animal dies is pure (tahir) and can be used for wudu.
2
Understand the principle of impurity from dead animals
Death itself is not impure; impurity arises from the flowing blood that mixes throughout the body after death, as stated in the text.
This is the foundational principle for distinguishing pure from impure carcasses.
3
Apply the principle to aquatic animals
Aquatic animals have no flowing blood (dam masfuh). The red moisture seen in fish is not real blood; it turns white in sunlight, unlike real blood which turns black.
This empirical observation supports the ruling.
4
Critique the alternative reasoning
Some argue that water remains pure because the animal dies in its element. The commentator rejects this because it would imply that if the animal died outside water and then fell in, the water would become impure – which is not the correct view.
This highlights the importance of consistent reasoning.
5
Note the author's separate mention
The author mentions this case separately even though the general rule of 'animals without flowing blood' covers it. This is for added benefit and to highlight the water-born aspect.
Shows attention to logical clarity in legal texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبماء مات فيه حيوان مائي المولد كالسمك والضفدع
[١]قوله : وبماء ؛ أي يجوز الوضوء بماء مات فيه حيوان موصوف بكونه مائي المولد ؛ لأنه طاهر لم ينجسه موته، فإنّ الموت ليس بنجس في نفسه، وإنما يحكمُ بنجاسة الميتة ؛ لأنَّ الدم النجس السائل في العروق يختلط بعد الموت في جميع البدن ، وينتشر في أجزائه.
والحيوان المائي المولد ليس له دم مسفوح ؛ إذ الدموي لا يسكن الماء لمنافاة بين طبع الماء والدم، وما يرى في السمك وغيره من رطوبة كلون الدم ليس بدم حقيقة ، بدليل أن الدم إذا ألقي في الشمس اسود، وهذه الرطوبة تبيض.
وهذا أولى مما علله بعضهم بأنّ ما يعيش في الماء إذا مات فيه مات في معدنه ، فلا يعطى له حكم النجاسة ؛ لأنه يقتضي أنه لو مات بحري خارج الماء ثم وقع فيه فسد الماء
مع أنه ليس كذلك على الصحيح ، صرّح به في «فتح القدير» و«البحر» وغيرهما. فإن قلت : إذا كان وجه عدم تنجس الماء بموتِ مائي المولد هو فقدان الدم السائل فيه، فلم ذكر المصنّف هذه الصورة على حدة، وهلا اكتفى على قوله : «وما ليس له دم سائل».
قلت : لما كان لهذه المسألة وجه آخر أيضاً أفردها بالذكر، مع أن في التعميم بعد التخصيص إفادة زائدة.
(۱) وقد صحح قول أبي يوسف اللكنوي في «السعاية ( ص ٣٤٨) ، وابن الهمام في «فتح القدير» (۱ : ۷۲ ، واختاره تلميذه ،قاسم، وقواه في «النهر»، وأقره صاحب «التنوير» (۱ : ١٠٦). وصحح الحلبي في غنية المستملي (۱) : (۸۱۹ الأول ، وهو ما اختاره صاحب «البدائع» (۱ : ۷۱ ، وأقره قاضي خان في فتاواه (۱) : (٤) ، وقال ابن عابدين في «رد المحتار» (١ : ١٠٦): والحاصل أنهما قولان مصححان، وأولهما أحوط. (۲) «فتح القدير» (۱) : ۸۳ - (٨٤) ، وينظر: «العناية»(۱ : ۲۰۸).
And (it is permissible to perform wudu with) water in which a water-born animal dies, such as fish and frog. [Commentary:] His statement: 'and (with) water' means it is permissible to perform wudu with water in which an animal described as water-born dies, because it is pure; its death does not make it impure. Indeed, death is not impure in itself; rather, a dead body is judged impure because the impure flowing blood in the veins mixes after death throughout the body and spreads in its parts. The water-born animal has no flowing blood; for a blood-bearing animal cannot live in water due to the incompatibility between the nature of water and blood. What is seen in fish and the like of moisture resembling blood color is not truly blood; evidence is that when blood is thrown in the sun it turns black, whereas this moisture turns white. This is preferable to the reasoning of some who say that what lives in water, when it dies in it, dies in its element, so it is not given the ruling of impurity; because that would imply that if it died outside water and then fell into it, it would spoil the water, while that is not the case according to the correct position, as stated in Fath al-Qadir and al-Bahr and others. If you say: If the reason water is not impurified by the death of a water-born animal is the lack of flowing blood, then why did the author mention this case separately, and not suffice with his general statement 'and what has no flowing blood'? I say: Because this case also has another aspect, he mentioned it separately, and generalization after specification gives additional benefit.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage explains that water remains pure when an aquatic animal dies in it. The key reason is that such animals have no flowing blood. In Hanafi fiqh, the impurity of a dead body is due to the mixing of impure blood. Since aquatic animals like fish and frogs do not have circulating blood, their death does not impart impurity. This is proven by empirical observation: what appears as blood in fish turns white in the sun, unlike real blood which turns black. The commentator prefers this reasoning over the alternative explanation that the animal dies in its element, because that would create inconsistency if the animal died outside water. The passage also notes a subtle point: the author mentioned this case separately despite already covering animals without flowing blood, because here the animal is specifically water-born, adding a specific ruling.

Key Terms
مائي المولد (mā'ī al-mawlid): Born in water; aquatic; an animal whose natural habitat is water.
دم سائل (dam sā'il): Flowing blood; blood that flows from a wound.
دم مسفوح (dam masfūḥ): Shed or flowing blood, specifically blood that flows from a wound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason water remains pure when an aquatic animal dies in it?
A) The animal dies in its element.
B) The animal has no flowing blood.
C) The animal is small.
D) Death is not impure in itself.

Key Takeaway
Water remains pure when a water-born animal dies because such animals lack flowing blood, which is the cause of impurity in dead bodies.

Ruling on Water with Dead Aquatic Animal
Explains ruling and rationale for aquatic animal death in water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water in which an aquatic-born animal has died and explain the rationale based on the absence of flowing blood.

Core Idea
Aquatic animals lack flowing blood, so their death does not render water impure.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary expands on a ruling from the base text regarding water in which an aquatic-born animal has died. The commentator clarifies that the water remains pure because the animal contains no flowing blood (dam masfuh). He also contrasts his preferred reasoning with an alternative explanation, deepening our understanding of the school's legal methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? (Performing wudu with water in which an aquatic-born animal died). What is the hukm? (Water remains pure, so wudu is valid.)
The ruling is not that the water becomes impure; death itself is not najis.
2
Locate the dalil and its type
The commentator uses qiyas: the effective cause (illa) for impurity is flowing blood. Aquatic animals lack flowing blood, so they are analogized to pure substances.
The proof is rational (aqli) based on observation and deductive reasoning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the absence of flowing blood prove purity? Impurity attaches to flowing blood, not to the dead body itself. Since aquatic animals have no such blood, their death does not cause impurity.
The commentator supports this with an empirical test: blood blackens in the sun, while fish moisture whitens.
4
Note the rejected reasoning
An alternative reasoning is that death in the element (water) prevents impurity. The commentator rejects this because it would wrongly imply impurity if the animal died outside water and then fell in.
The preferred reasoning is more consistent and covers all scenarios.
5
Understand the author's structure
Why mention this case separately despite the general rule about 'no flowing blood'? Because this case has an additional unique aspect, and mentioning it adds benefit of clarification.
This demonstrates the careful pedagogy of the Hanafi texts.

قوله : وبماء ; أي يجوز الوضوء بماء مات فيه حيوان موصوف بكونه مائي المولد ; لأنه طاهر لم ينجسه موته، فإنّ الموت ليس بنجس في نفسه، وإنما يحكمُ بنجاسة الميتة ; لأنَّ الدم النجس السائل في العروق يختلط بعد الموت في جميع البدن ، وينتشر في أجزائه. والحيوان المائي المولد ليس له دم مسفوح ; إذ الدموي لا يسكن الماء لمنافاة بين طبع الماء والدم، وما يرى في السمك وغيره من رطوبة كلون الدم ليس بدم حقيقة ، بدليل أن الدم إذا ألقي في الشمس اسود، وهذه الرطوبة تبيض. وهذا أولى مما علله بعضهم بأنّ ما يعيش في الماء إذا مات فيه مات في معدنه ، فلا يعطى له حكم النجاسة ; لأنه يقتضي أنه لو مات بحري خارج الماء ثم وقع فيه فسد الماء مع أنه ليس كذلك على الصحيح . فإن قلت : إذا كان وجه عدم تنجس الماء بموتِ مائي المولد هو فقدان الدم السائل فيه، فلم ذكر المصنّف هذه الصورة على حدة، وهلا اكتفى على قوله : «وما ليس له دم سائل». قلت : لما كان لهذه المسألة وجه آخر أيضاً أفردها بالذكر، مع أن في التعميم بعد التخصيص إفادة زائدة.
[Commentator] said: 'And with water' – meaning it is permissible to perform ablution with water in which an animal described as aquatic-born has died; because it is pure, its death does not make it impure, for death is not impure in itself. Impurity of dead animals is ruled only because the impure flowing blood in the veins mixes throughout the body after death and spreads in its parts. The aquatic-born animal does not have flowing blood; for a blooded creature does not dwell in water due to the incompatibility between the nature of water and blood. What is seen in fish and others of moisture resembling the color of blood is not actually blood, evidenced by the fact that blood turns black when exposed to the sun, while this moisture becomes white. This is better than what some reasoned: that whatever lives in water, if it dies in it, dies in its element, so it is not given the ruling of impurity – because that would imply that if a sea creature died outside water then fell into it, the water would become spoiled, whereas that is not the case according to the correct view. If you say: Since the reason water is not made impure by the death of an aquatic-born animal is the absence of flowing blood in it, why did the author mention this case separately and not suffice with his statement: 'and what has no flowing blood'? I say: Because this issue also has another aspect, so he mentioned it separately, and there is additional benefit in generalization after particularization.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This is an expansion on the ruling that ablution is valid with water in which an aquatic-born animal has died. The commentator explains the rationale: death itself is not impure; impurity arises from the mixing of flowing blood throughout the body. Since aquatic animals lack flowing blood (due to the incompatibility of blood with water), their death does not render water impure. The commentator prefers this reasoning over the alternative (that death in the element prevents impurity) because that alternative would entail that a creature dying outside water then falling in would spoil it, which is incorrect. The author's separate mention of aquatic animals despite the general rule of 'no flowing blood' is justified by the unique aspect of this case and the benefit of elaboration.

Key Terms
مائي المولد (mā'ī al-mawlid): An animal whose natural origin and habitat is water, such as fish and frogs.
دم مسفوح (dam masfūḥ): Flowing blood that is shed (i.e., that flows from a wound).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the death of an aquatic-born animal not render water impure according to this passage?
A) Because it died in its element
B) Because it lacks flowing blood
C) Because its body is naturally pure
D) Because water is a purifier

Key Takeaway
The death of an aquatic-born animal does not render water impure because it lacks flowing blood (dam masfuh), which is the source of impurity.

Ruling on Animals without Flowing Blood
States ruling and proof for animals without flowing blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that animals without flowing blood are pure when dead and derive it from the hadith of the fly.

Core Idea
Only flowing blood imparts impurity; a fly's body is pure even when dead.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a foundational principle of Hanafi tahara: impurity is limited to flowing blood (dam masfūḥ). Since creatures like flies, mosquitoes, and other insects lack such blood, their dead bodies are considered pure. The primary evidence is a hadith in which the Prophet instructed a person to fully dip a fly that fell into their food, implying that the fly does not render the food impure. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the important linguistic distinction regarding aquatic animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that the dead body of an animal without flowing blood is pure. This is the main legal conclusion.
2
Clarify the scope via the term 'aquatic-born'
The commentator prefers the term 'māʼī al-muwallad' (aquatic-born) over 'māʼī' (aquatic) to exclude animals like ducks that are born on land and have flowing blood, even if they live in water.
This precision ensures the ruling applies only to animals whose life cycle is entirely aquatic.
3
Locate the proof (dalīl)
The hadith: 'If a fly falls into a vessel of one of you, let him dip it completely...'
Narrated by Bukhari, Nasa'i, and others.
4
Understand the legal reasoning (wajh al-dalālah)
The command to dip the fly indicates that the fly does not render the food impure; otherwise, dipping would spread impurity. Thus, the fly's body is considered pure.
This reasoning is based on zannī dalālah but accepted by the Hanafi school.
5
Note the ikhtilāf with other schools
The Shafi'i school generally considers all dead animals impure except for the fly based on this hadith. The Hanafi school extends the ruling to all creatures without flowing blood.
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school, a dead mosquito in water does not make it impure; in the Shafi'i school, it might.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[Commentator] said: With a kasra on the dal – he pointed out that the correct and well-known is with a fatha, and also with a dammah on the dad and fatha on the dal; on the first two, the dad is with kasra. It is what is called in Persian 'Ghuk'; it has many types as in 'Hayat al-Hayawan' by al-Damiri. [Commentator] said: 'And he only said ... etc.' – indication of the reason for choosing this expression over 'what lives in water' as occurred in al-Hidayah, and over 'aquatic' as in expressions of some: because some animals are generated outside water and live in it, like ducks and geese; their falling into water dead spoils it due to the presence of flowing blood, and because they do not die in their element, even though 'what lives in water' and 'aquatic' apply to them; so he chose the phrase 'aquatic-born' to be specific to what is generated and has its abode only in water. [Commentator] said: 'Falling of the fly into food' – which is what was narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'If a fly falls into the vessel of one of you, let him dip it – i.e., immerse it – for in one of its wings is disease and in the other is cure, and it protects itself with the wing that has the disease.' Reported by al-Bukhari, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, Ibn Hibban, and others.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage expands on two rulings: first, the specification of 'aquatic-born' rather than just 'aquatic' or 'lives in water' to exclude animals like ducks that are born on land but live in water; these have flowing blood and thus spoil water if they die in it. Second, the general ruling that animals without flowing blood (like flies and bugs) are pure when dead. The proof is the hadith about the fly: the Prophet commanded to dip the fly entirely in food, implying its body is not impure. The commentator clarifies that the impurity is solely due to flowing blood, so any animal lacking it is pure.

Key Terms
ذباب (dhubāb): Common housefly, considered to have no flowing blood.
دم مسفوح (dam masfūḥ): Flowing blood that is shed.
مائي المولد (māʼī al-muwallad): Aquatic-born: an animal whose genesis and abode are entirely in water.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, impurity is limited to flowing blood; therefore, all animals without such blood are pure when dead, as confirmed by the hadith of the fly.

Ruling on Animals Without Flowing Blood: Pure When Dead in Water
Commentary on the ruling that animals without flowing blood are pure when dead in water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on animals without flowing blood dying in water and identify the proof from the hadith of the fly.

Core Idea
A dead fly does not spoil water — only flowing blood is impure.

Water is essential for purification, but when an animal dies in it, the ruling depends on the animal's characteristics. The Hanafi school distinguishes between animals with flowing blood and those without. This lesson explains that animals like bugs and flies, which lack flowing blood, do not render water impure, supported by the hadith of the fly. Understanding this ruling helps properly apply the rules of purity in everyday situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The case is: an animal without flowing blood (e.g., fly, bug) dies in water. The ruling is that the water remains pure (tahir) and the dead animal is not considered impure (najis).
This is a declaratory ruling (hukm wad'i) of purity, not an obligation.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author cites the hadith of the fly: the Prophet commanded to dip the fly if it falls into a vessel, indicating that its death does not make the liquid impure.
The hadith is reported by al-Bukhari, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and Ibn Hibban.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal implication)
The command to dip (فليمقله) implies that the fly's death does not spoil the liquid. If it did, the command would be to remove it, not immerse it further.
This is a secondary proof; the primary cause is the absence of flowing blood.
4
Understand the ta'lil (legal cause)
The commentary explains that impurity is only flowing blood (الدم المسفوح). Animals without flowing blood are therefore pure when dead, whether in water or not.
This principle was established earlier in the chapter on nullifiers of wudu.
5
Apply the definition of 'water-born'
The commentator explains why the author used 'water-born' (مائي المولد) instead of 'aquatic' or 'lives in water'. This excludes animals that originate on land but live in water (e.g., ducks), which have flowing blood and spoil water when they die in it.
The term ensures the ruling is specific to animals whose entire lifecycle is in water.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the ruling that animals without flowing blood (such as bugs and flies) do not render water impure when they die in it. The author first clarifies the pronunciation of 'بق' (bug), noting the most correct vowel is kasra on the daal. He then explains why the term 'water-born' (مائي المولد) was chosen over other expressions: it excludes animals that originate outside water but live in it (like ducks), which have flowing blood and would spoil water if they die in it. The core legal reason (ta'lil) is that impurity is only flowing blood (الدم المسفوح), as established in the discussion of nullifiers of wudu. Animals without flowing blood are therefore pure when dead. The hadith of the fly is cited as proof: the Prophet commanded dipping the fly into the liquid, indicating that its death does not make the liquid impure. This ruling applies to all similar animals without flowing blood.

Key Terms
دم سائل (dam sā'il): Blood that flows when an animal's vein is cut; used to distinguish animals whose death does not make water impure (those without such blood).
مائي المولد (mā'ī al-mawlid): An animal that is born and lives in water; its death in water does not make the water impure because it has no flowing blood.
النجس (al-najas): Impurity; here specifically refers to flowing blood (الدم المسفوح) as the only najas from animals.

Key Takeaway
Animals without flowing blood are pure when dead; only flowing blood is considered impurity from animals.

Ruling on Water Pressed from Trees or Fruit
Commentary on the ruling that water pressed from trees or fruit is not permissible for wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the Hanafi school holds that water pressed from trees or fruit is not valid for wudu and distinguish between pressed water and naturally dripping water.

Core Idea
Water pressed from trees or fruit is not absolute water — wudu with it is invalid.

This passage discusses a specific ruling: water that is extracted by pressing (i‘tiṣār) from a tree or fruit cannot be used for wudu. The commentator clarifies the correct reading of the text and explains the legal reasoning: such water is considered restricted (muqayyad) because it is associated with its source, unlike water that drips spontaneously, which remains absolute (muṭlaq). The disagreement with Imam al-Shafi‘i is also addressed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act of using water pressed from a tree or fruit for wudu. The ruling: it is not permissible, meaning wudu with such water is invalid.
The text explicitly states 'لا بما اعتصر' (not from what is pressed).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the definition of absolute water (mā’ muṭlaq): what the mind immediately turns to when 'water' is mentioned without qualification. Pressed water does not meet this definition.
The commentator defines absolute water as 'what the mind immediately turns to when water is mentioned absolutely'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The definition excludes pressed water because it is associated with a specific source (tree or fruit). This makes it restricted (muqayyad), and thus not valid for wudu.
The distinction between absolute (muṭlaq) and restricted (muqayyad) is central.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi‘i is reported to have disagreed on the first issue (animals without blood), but the commentator asserts that on the second issue (pressed water), there is no disagreement; or if there is, the Hanafi position is based on the clear definition.
The correct view is that al-Shafi‘i only disagreed on the first issue.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is that pressed water is not permissible for wudu. This is the relied-upon view in the school, chosen by the majority of authorities (Hidayah, Mujtaba, etc.).
The Hanafi school consistently maintains this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Not from something pressed from a tree or fruit. [1] And in it is ikhtilaf with al-Shafi'i. 'Not from something pressed' - the narration is with shortened 'ma', from tree or fruit. As for what drips from the tree, it is permissible to perform wudu with it. [1] His statement: 'and in it'; apparently it refers to the second issue, and it is possible that it refers to both issues, for the author of al-Hidayah mentioned ikhtilaf in both, and the correct view is that his ikhtilaf is only in the first, and in the second he agrees in meaning, as al-Ayni and others detailed. [2] His statement: 'not from something pressed'; i.e., it is not permissible to perform wudu with water pressed from a tree or fruit, because it is not absolute water; for absolute water is that to which the mind immediately turns when water is mentioned absolutely, and what drips and similar is not like that; rather it is restricted water. [3] His statement: 'al-riwayah'; i.e., what is transmitted and heard from the author and other jurists, is the shortened 'ma' (ما المقصورة الموصولة), not 'al-ma' (الماء), even though that is also correct; and it is supported that if the word 'water' were here, there would be no need to mention it in his statement 'nor with water that has changed'. [4] His statement: 'so it is permissible to perform wudu with it'; because it came out by itself without any treatment, and this is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah, al-Mujtaba, al-Mustasfa, and others. And the one preferred by the author of al-Bahr, al-Nahr, al-Hulba, al-Ghunya, and Nur...
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that water pressed (اعتصار) from trees or fruit is not permissible for wudu, as it is not absolute water (ماء مطلق). The matn states 'لا بما اعتصر' (not from what is pressed). The commentator first clarifies the correct reading: the word 'لا' is followed by 'ما' (shortened form), not 'ماء' (water). He then distinguishes between pressed water (which is impermissible) and water that drips spontaneously from a tree (which is permissible because it comes out without human intervention, making it still considered absolute water). The disagreement with al-Shafi'i (ikhtilaf) is discussed: the author of al-Hidayah mentions disagreement in both issues (animals without blood and pressed water), but the correct view is that al-Shafi'i only disagreed on the first issue. The legal reasoning (ta'lil) for the prohibition is that pressed water is restricted (مقيد) because it is specifically associated with its source (tree or fruit), unlike absolute water which is unrestricted and to which the mind immediately turns. The commentary also notes that the choice of the majority (including Hidayah, Mujtaba, Mustasfa) is that dripping water is permissible, while later authorities like al-Bahr prefer the opposite.

Key Terms
اعتصار (i‘tiṣār): Pressing or extracting liquid from a solid source, such as squeezing fruit or tapping a tree.
ماء مطلق (mā’ muṭlaq): Absolute water: water that remains in its original, unqualified state; the default water for purification.
ماء مقيد (mā’ muqayyad): Restricted water: water that is associated with a specific source or flavor, such as pressed juice; not valid for wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it is not absolute water (ماء مطلق) but restricted water.
C) Because pressing is considered a form of treatment that makes it impure.
D) Because it was not rainwater.

Key Takeaway
Only absolute water (mā’ muṭlaq) is valid for wudu; water pressed from trees or fruit is restricted and invalidates wudu.

Ruling on Water Extracted from Trees or Fruits for Wudu: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explaining the ruling on water extracted from trees/fruit and the disagreement with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wudu with extracted water, explain the distinction between naturally dripping and extracted water, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-position.

Core Idea
Water extracted by pressing is muqayyad; wudu with it is invalid in the Hanafi school.

In this passage, the commentator explains the ruling on using water that has been extracted from trees or fruits by pressing. The Hanafi school distinguishes between water that drips naturally (valid) and water obtained through treatment (invalid). The passage also notes that Imam al-Shafi'i considers such water absolute and permissible for wudu. Understanding this distinction is key to mastering the definition of absolute water (ma' mutlaq) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
The passage states that wudu is not permissible with water extracted from a tree or fruit. Determine the hukm level: wudu becomes invalid because the water is not ma' mutlaq.
The word 'la' indicates negation of permissibility.
2
Understand the distinction
Note that water that drips naturally without treatment is permissible; extracted water is not. The key difference is whether the water is obtained through human treatment (i'tisar) or natural flow.
Natural dripping is considered mutlaq; pressing changes its nature.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The fly-dipping hadith is cited to show that bloodless impurities do not spoil water. This supports the permissibility of naturally occurring water but does not extend to extracted water because extraction is a form of treatment that alters the water.
The hadith is about an impurity falling into water, not about the water itself being altered.
4
Explore the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., author of al-Hidayah) permit pressed water if it comes out by itself, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is non-permissibility, as supported by later scholars like al-Bahr and al-Nahr.
The tarjih (preference) is given to the view that extracted water is muqayyad.
5
Compare with Shafi'i view
Imam al-Shafi'i considers pressed water as mutlaq and valid for wudu. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafis cannot perform wudu with such water, while Shafi'is can.
The dispute arises from different definitions of ma' mutlaq.

Not with water extracted from a tree or fruit [1] and in it is the disagreement of al-Shafi'i. 'Not with water extracted' — the recitation (al-riwaya) with a shortened 'ma' from a tree or fruit. As for what drips from a tree, wudu with it is permissible. This hadith indicates the command to dip, and it is known that food may be hot and the fly dies in it; if its death spoiled it, he would not have commanded its dipping. When the ruling is established, it is established in its analogues among things without blood. [1] His saying: 'and in it' — it is apparent that it refers back to the second issue, and it is possible it refers to both issues, for the author of al-Hidayah mentioned his disagreement in both, but the correct view is that his disagreement is only in the first, and in the second he agrees in meaning (1), as al-'Ayni (2) and others explained it. [2] His saying: 'not with water extracted' — meaning wudu is not permissible with water extracted from a tree or fruit, for it is not absolute water (ma' mutlaq) because absolute is what the mind immediately turns to when water is mentioned, and what drips and the like is not like that, rather it is restricted water (ma' muqayyad). [3] His saying: 'al-riwaya' — meaning the transmitted and heard from the author and other jurists, it is the shortened ma (al-ma al-maqsura) not water (al-ma'), although it is also correct, and it is supported by the fact that if the word 'water' were here, he would not have needed to mention it in his statement: 'nor with water that has changed'. [4] His saying: 'so wudu with it is permissible' — because it came out by itself without treatment, and this is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah (3), al-Mujtaba, al-Mustasfa and others. And what the author of al-Bahr (4), al-Nahr, al-Hulba, al-Ghunya, Nur al-Idah, and Manh al-Ghaffar and others preferred is non-permissibility because it is restricted water, and the expansion is in al-Sa'aya.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary on a matn from al-Wiqaya. It addresses the ruling on water extracted by pressing from trees or fruits (ma' mu'tasar) and its permissibility for wudu. The passage begins by stating the ruling: wudu is not permissible with such water, and it notes that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees. The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'al-riwaya' refers to the transmitted text with a shortened 'ma' (meaning al-ma' al-maqsura, i.e., the word 'ma' meaning 'what' rather than 'water'), and this is supported by the fact that the author later uses 'ma' in 'wa la bima' zaal' (nor with water that has changed). The proof is the hadith about dipping a fly: the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded that if a fly falls into a drink, it should be dipped, because one wing carries disease and the other cure. The commentator argues that if the fly's death spoiled the food, the Prophet would not have commanded dipping. Since the ruling is established, it applies to analogous cases where there is no blood (i.e., the fly's death does not spoil the liquid). Therefore, water that naturally drips from a tree (without treatment) is permissible, but water extracted by pressing is not, because it is not absolute water; it has been treated and is considered muqayyad. The passage then reports a difference regarding which issue the phrase 'wa fihi khilaf al-Shafi'i' refers to: some say it refers to both issues (dripping and extracted), but the correct view is that it refers only to the first (dripping). Regarding the extracted water itself, the commentator cites two positions among Hanafi scholars: some (like the author of al-Hidayah) permit it because it comes out by itself (if it drips without pressing), but the stronger and relied-upon position (supported by al-Bahr, al-Nahr, etc.) is that it is not permissible because it is muqayyad. Thus, the Hanafi school holds that water extracted by pressing from trees or fruits is not valid for wudu, while naturally dripping water is valid. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees and considers such water mutlaq.

Key Terms
لا بما اعتصر (la bima' i'tusira): Water extracted by pressing or squeezing from a tree or fruit.
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): Absolute water, that which the mind immediately turns to when the word 'water' is used without qualification.
ماء مقيد (ma' muqayyad): Restricted water that has been altered by treatment or mixing, so it is no longer considered absolute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible
B) Impermissible
C) Makruh
D) Permissible only if heated

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, water for wudu must be ma' mutlaq; extracted water is muqayyad and invalidates wudu.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Dominance by Parts or Color?
Explaining the criterion for water losing its nature through domination by other substances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi criteria for water losing its mutlaq nature and explain which one is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Water loses its purifying power when its riqqa and silan are dominated — but is it by parts or by color? Abu Yusuf's view rules.

The passage from 'Umadat al-Ri'ayah tackles the question: when does water mixed with a pure substance cease to be mutlaq (absolute) and become muqayyad (restricted)? The Hanafi school agrees that if the water's essential properties — riqqa (fluidity) and silan (flowing) — are lost, then wudu with it is invalid. But they differ on what indicator of dominance (ghalaba) triggers that loss. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and the later preponderated view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of water in Hanafi law
Recognize that water's tab' consists of riqqa (being thin and able to be wrung from cloth) and silan (flowing over limbs). These define mutlaq water.
Riqqa and silan are the essence; if they are removed, water is no longer mutlaq.
2
Understand the issue of ghalaba
The question: what degree of mixture removes riqqa and silan? The text notes two opinions within the school.
Ghalaba means the added substance dominates the water.
3
Compare the two opinions
Abu Yusuf: dominance by parts (ajza') – the quantity of the mixture affects riqqa and silan. Muhammad al-Shaybani: dominance by color (lawn) – a visible sign that the water has changed.
Both are based on the idea that water's nature is altered, but they differ on the indicator.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The commentator declares Abu Yusuf's view as al-sahih (correct). Thus, as long as water remains fluid and flows, even if discolored, it is still mutlaq and valid for wudu.
The practical difference: if water turns yellow from saffron but still flows, per Abu Yusuf it is permissible; per Muhammad it is not.

ورقته : بأنه لا ينعصر عن الثوب وسيلانه : بأن لا يسيل على الأعضاء سيلان الماء. وأما إذا بقي على رقته وسيلانه فإنه لا يمنع جواز الوضوء به تغير أوصافه كلها بجامد خالطه بدون طبخ كزعفران وصابون وأشنان وفاكهة وورق شجر.

ثانياً : إن ظهر وصف واحد كلون أو طعم من مائع له وصفان كاللبن فيه وصفان اللون والطعم ولا رائحة له ، فإن لم ينتقل أحد الوصفين إلى الماء جاز الوضوء به، وإن وجد أحدهما لم يجز. ثالثاً : إن ظهر وصفين من مائع له أوصاف ثلاثة : كالخلّ له لون وطعم وريح، فأي وصفين منها ظهرا منعا صحة الوضوء، والواحد منها لا يضر لقلته. رابعاً : إن غلب الوزن من مائع لا وصف له يخالف الماء بلون أو طعم أو ريح : كالماء المستعمل فإنه بالاستعمال لم يتغير له طعم ولا لون ولا ريح، وهو طاهر كما سبق، وأيضاً ماء الورد المنقطع
Nor with water whose nature has changed by the domination of other parts — it is intended by it: that it takes it out of the nature of water, which is riqqa (fluidity) and silan (flowing). (1) His saying: 'parts' — he indicated by it that what is considered is the domination of the parts of the mixing substance (3) because they are what take it out of the requirement of the nature of water, which is flowing and fluidity. This is the madhhab of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, and it is correct. According to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, the domination is considered by color. (1) Nur al-Idah (p.65). (2) al-Sa'aya (1:359), and in it: al-Ramli said in his hashish on Manh al-Ghaffar: 'Whoever reviews the books of the madhhab finds most of them on non-permissibility, and it is more cautious.' (3) The later scholars mentioned a rule (dabit) for domination to remove the confusion of expressions in this chapter, and al-Shurnabalali mentioned it in al-Maraqi (pp.26-27), and I detailed it in al-Mishkat (pp.107-108) and summarized it in Nafahat al-Suluk (p.18). Its summary is as follows: The rule of domination has two aspects: First: if pure solids mix with water and take it out of its riqqa and silan, according to the correct view. Its riqqa: that it does not squeeze out of the garment. Its silan: that it does not flow over the limbs like water flows. But if it remains in its riqqa and silan, then even if all its descriptions change by a solid mixed without cooking, like saffron, soap, alkali, fruit, and tree leaves, it does not prevent the permissibility of wudu with it. Second: if liquids mix with water, and it has four states: First: if one description appears from a liquid that has one description, then wudu with it is permissible, like pumpkin and melon, for their water only differs in taste, and similarly rose water, for it only differs in smell. Second: if one description like color or taste appears from a liquid that has two descriptions (like milk has color and taste but no smell), then if neither of the two descriptions transfers to the water, wudu with it is permissible; if one of them appears, it is not permissible. Third: if two descriptions appear from a liquid that has three descriptions (like vinegar has color, taste, and smell), then whichever two descriptions appear prevent the validity of wudu, and one alone does not harm due to its scarcity. Fourth: if the weight dominates from a liquid that has no description differing from water in color, taste, or smell, like used water that by use has not changed in taste, color, or smell, and it is pure as before, and also rose water that has lost its scent.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn 'wa la bima' zaal tab'uhu bi ghalabati ghayrihi ajza', meaning water whose nature (tab') has been removed by the dominance of other parts. The commentator explains that the nature of water consists of riqqa (fluidity: not being thick or sticky) and silan (flowing: being able to run over the limbs). The key issue is what constitutes 'domination' (ghalaba) that takes water out of its natural state and makes it muqayyad. The passage reports an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: according to Abu Yusuf, the criterion is dominance of parts (ajza') — meaning the quantity of the mixed substance must overwhelm the water in terms of substance, causing loss of riqqa and silan. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, the criterion is dominance by color (lawn) — if the color of the water changes significantly. The commentator states that Abu Yusuf's view is the correct one (al-sahih). Then a detailed dabit (rule) from later scholars is presented, summarizing the conditions for water mixed with solids and liquids. For solids: if the mixture takes the water out of its riqqa and silan, wudu is invalid; if the water remains fluid and flowing, even if its colors change, wudu is valid (e.g., water mixed with saffron or soap that still flows). For liquids: the classification is based on the number of attributes (taste, color, smell) that appear. If a liquid has one attribute (e.g., pumpkin water has taste only) and that attribute appears, wudu is valid because it's still close to absolute water. If the liquid has two attributes (e.g., milk has color and taste) and one appears, wudu is invalid; if neither appears, it's valid. If the liquid has three attributes (e.g., vinegar) and two appear, wudu is invalid; one alone is not harmful. Additionally, if the weight of a liquid without attributes (like used water) dominates, it does not affect the ruling.

Key Terms
طبع الماء (tab' al-ma'): The nature of water, consisting of riqqa (fluidity) and silan (flowing).
غلبة الأجزاء (ghalabat al-ajza'): Dominance of parts; the added substance outweighs water in quantity, affecting its flow.
غلبة اللون (ghalabat al-lawn): Dominance by color; the water's color is clearly changed by the mixture.
رقة (riqqa): Fluidity; the water is not thick and can be wrung out of a cloth.
سيلان (silan): Flowing; water runs over the limbs when poured.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Change in color
B) Change in taste
C) Dominance of parts (ajza')
D) Change in smell

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's criterion (dominance by parts) is the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school for determining when water loses its mutlaq status.

Ruling on Water Whose Nature is Changed by Admixture
Explains when water loses its purifying quality due to admixture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water whose nature is changed by admixture (by parts or by cooking), explain the criteria of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and apply the rule to practical examples such as beverages, broth, and leaf-infused water.

Core Idea
Water loses its purifying quality when it loses its fluidity and runniness, not merely by color change.

In the Hanafi school, the purity of water for wudu depends on its retaining its original nature. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that when a foreign substance dominates water—either by parts (solids) or by cooking—the water can lose its purifying quality. There is a key difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the criterion, with Abu Yusuf's view being relied upon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the passage rules on water whose nature has changed due to admixture. The ruling: such water is not permissible for wudu (it is makruh tahrimi).
This is established in the Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Understand the nature of water
The inherent nature of water is defined as fluidity (al-riqqah) and runniness (al-sayalan). Loss of these properties indicates that the water's original nature has been overcome.
This definition is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf considers the loss of fluidity and runniness (ghalabat al-ajza') as the criterion. Muhammad considers predominance in color (ghalabat bi al-lawn) as the criterion.
The text states that Abu Yusuf's view is al-sahih (the correct one).
4
Apply to examples of cooking
Water changed by cooking (e.g., beverages, vinegar, broth) is analogous to water changed by solids. The loss of original nature may occur through cooking even if the water remains fluid.
The later scholars provide a detailed dabit (rule) for mixing with liquids.
5
Apply to leaf-infused water
Water that changes color due to leaves (like water of broad beans) is also invalid for wudu because it has lost its original nature.
This is a practical application of the same principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة/ أقسام المياه
ولا بماء زال طبعه بغلبة غيره أجزاء
ولا بماء زال طبعُهُ بغلبة غيره أجزاءً ) ، المراد به : أن يُخْرِجَهُ من طبع الماء ،
وهو الرقة والسيلان.
[1] قوله : أجزاء ؛ أشار به إلى أنّ المعتبر هو غلبة أجزاء المخالط ؛ لأنَّها المخرجة عن مقتضى طبع الماء وهو السيلان والرقة، وهو مذهب أبي يوسف له، وهو الصحيح، وعند محمد له تعتبر الغلبة بحسب اللون.

الأول : إن خالط الماء الجامدات الطاهرات بإخراجه عن رقته وسيلانه على الصحيح.
ورقته : بأنه لا ينعصر عن الثوب وسيلانه : بأن لا يسيل على الأعضاء سيلان الماء. وأما إذا بقي على رقته وسيلانه فإنه لا يمنع جواز الوضوء به تغير أوصافه كلها بجامد خالطه بدون طبخ كزعفران وصابون وأشنان وفاكهة وورق شجر.
الثاني : إن خالط الماء المائعات، وله أربع حالات : أولا : إن ظهر وصف واحد من مائع له وصف واحد فإنه يجوز الوضوء به كالقرع والبطيخ فإن ماءها لا يخالف إلا في الطعم، وكذلك ماء الورد فإنه لا يخالف إلا في الريح.

ثانياً : إن ظهر وصف واحد كلون أو طعم من مائع له وصفان كاللبن فيه وصفان اللون والطعم ولا رائحة له ، فإن لم ينتقل أحد الوصفين إلى الماء جاز الوضوء به، وإن وجد أحدهما لم يجز. ثالثاً : إن ظهر وصفين من مائع له أوصاف ثلاثة : كالخلّ له لون وطعم وريح، فأي وصفين منها ظهرا منعا صحة الوضوء، والواحد منها لا يضر لقلته. رابعاً : إن غلب الوزن من مائع لا وصف له يخالف الماء بلون أو طعم أو ريح : كالماء المستعمل فإنه بالاستعمال لم يتغير له طعم ولا لون ولا ريح، وهو طاهر كما سبق، وأيضاً ماء الورد المنقطع
الرائحة، فإن اختلط لتران من الماء المستعمل بلتر من الماء المطلق لم يجز الوضوء به ، وإن استويا
