The Book of Purification / Divisions of Water. Nor with water whose nature has changed due to the predominance of another substance in parts. Nor with water whose nature has changed due to the predominance of another substance in parts: what is meant by it is that it removes it from the nature of water, which is fluidity and runniness. [1] His statement: 'in parts'; he indicated by it that what is considered is the predominance of the parts of the admixed substance, because they are what remove it from the requirement of the nature of water which is runniness and fluidity, and this is the madhhab of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, and it is the correct one, and according to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, predominance is considered according to color. (Or by cooking, like beverages and vinegar, and water of broad beans, and broth) analogous to what is pressed from trees and fruits, so rhubarb drink is pressed from trees, and apple drink and the like is pressed from fruits. (And water of broad beans) analogous to what has been overcome by something else in parts. (And broth) analogous to what has been overcome by something else by cooking. As for water that has changed due to the abundance of leaves falling into it until when it is lifted in the palm the color of the leaves appears in it, then wudu with it is not permissible because it is like water of broad beans. Nor with stagnant water into which impurity has fallen unless it is... [1] His statement: 'or by cooking'; it apparently is conjoined to his statement: 'by predominance': i.e., wudu is not permissible with that whose nature has changed by cooking, and apparently the commentator's speech regarding his statement: 'the broth' requires that it is conjoined to his statement: 'in parts', so its meaning is by predominance of something else by parts or by cooking, and in it there is apparent difficulty, for there is no meaning for predominance of parts by cooking, for what is obtained by it is only change, not predominance, unless it is said that the 'ba' is in the meaning of 'with'. [2] His statement: 'like beverages' with fatha on the hamza, sukun on the shin, and kasr on the ra, plural of sharab meaning that which is drunk, and what is intended is beverages taken from trees and fruits: like pomegranate and apple drinks. [3] His statement: 'unless it is...' etc.; know that there has occurred much disagreement regarding the rendering of non-flowing water najis by the falling of impurity among the mujtahids of the ummah and their followers, with their agreement that flowing water does not become najis by the falling of impurity into it unless its effect appears in it: The Zahiriyyah went to the opinion of its not becoming najis at all, even if one or all of its attributes change by the falling of the impure, and this is a madhhab that reason and transmission reject. And the Shafi'iyyah went to the opinion that if it is the amount of two qullas, it does not become najis unless one of its attributes changes... (the translation continues with the footnote on ghayb example).
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on divisions of water in the book of Taharah. It presents the ruling that water becomes unsuitable for wudu if its inherent nature—characterized by fluidity (al-riqqah) and runniness (al-sayalan)—is removed due to the predominance of another substance. The author first explains the criterion for 'predominance of parts' (ghalabat al-ajza'): according to Imam Abu Yusuf, the decisive factor is the physical predominance of the admixed substance such that the water loses its fluidity and runniness. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, considers predominance in color as the criterion. The text notes that Abu Yusuf's view is the correct (al-sahih) one. Next, the passage extends the ruling to water whose nature is changed by cooking (bi al-tabh), giving examples: beverages extracted from fruits and trees (like rhubarb and apple drinks), vinegar, water of broad beans, and broth. It explains that these are analogous to water that has lost its original nature. A practical example: water mixed with many leaves until its color appears in the palm is like water of broad beans and thus invalid for wudu. The passage then begins a new section on stagnant water (maa' raakid) into which impurity falls, noting that there is much disagreement among schools. It mentions the Zahiriyyah's extreme position that such water never becomes najis, and the Shafi'i position that water of two qullas (approx. 270 liters) does not become najis unless its attributes change. The text ends abruptly mid-sentence. The accompanying footnote provides a detailed rule (dabit) for determining ghalabah (predominance) when mixing with solids or liquids, including a four-case analysis for liquids with one, two, or three attributes. The overall lesson is that the purity of water for wudu depends on its retaining its original fluid and flowing nature.

Key Terms
الرقة (al-riqqah): The fluidity of water; its ability to be absorbed and not remain stiff.
السيلان (al-sayalan): The runniness of water; its property of flowing easily on surfaces.
غلبة أجزاء (ghalabat al-ajza'): The predominance of the parts of a foreign substance in water, causing it to lose its inherent fluidity and runniness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the Hanafi madhhab, when is water considered to have its nature changed by a solid substance, such that wudu is not permissible?
A) When any one of the three attributes (color, taste, smell) changes.
B) When the water loses its fluidity and runniness.
C) When the water's color changes, regardless of fluidity.
D) When the water reaches the count of two qullas.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, water does not lose its purifying quality by mere attribute changes; rather, it must lose its essential fluidity and runniness (for solids) or specific attribute transfer (for liquids), with the view of Abu Yusuf being the relied-upon position.

Ruling on Water Changed by Cooking or Admixture for Wudu
States ruling and examples for water changed by cooking or admixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water changed by cooking or admixture and identify which types of water are impermissible for wudu.

Core Idea
Water that loses its original properties through cooking or admixture becomes impermissible for wudu.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary outlines a key ruling on water used for wudu: if water's original character is lost due to cooking or being dominated by other pure elements, it cannot be used for ritual purification. The examples provided help clarify the principle that water must remain in its original state (mutlaq) to be valid for wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Primary Ruling
Read the passage carefully to extract the main legal ruling: water that has been cooked or has had other substances dominate it is not permissible for wudu. The text uses 'أو بالطبخ' (or by cooking) to introduce this condition.
The ruling applies to water that changes due to non-impure sources.
2
Understand the Examples
List the examples given: drinks (ashriba) like apple or rhubarb juice, vinegar, water of broad beans, and broth. Each represents a scenario where water loses its original nature by either extraction or cooking.
These are all pure substances, not impurities.
3
Apply the Analogous Case
The passage extends the ruling to water that has changed color due to many leaves falling into it, stating it is like bean water and thus impermissible. Recognize the analogy: if leaves dominate the water, it loses its original quality.
The key is whether the change is substantial enough that water is no longer considered 'mutlaq'.
4
Note the Principle of Dominance
The phrase 'نظير ما غلب عليه غيره' (similar to what other components have dominated it) indicates the principle: if a foreign element overcomes the water in color, taste, or consistency, the water becomes unusable for wudu.
This is different from water that merely has a slight change from infusible leaves (where wudu is valid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بالطبخ، كالأشربة والخلّ، وماء الباقلاء، والمرق . (أو بالطبخ، كالأشربة والخلّ) نظير ما اعتصر من الشَّجرِ والثَّمر، فشرابُ الرِّيباس معتصر من الشجر، وشراب التفاح ونحوَهُ معتصر من الثَّمر. ( وماء الباقلاء) نظير ما غلب عليه غيره أجزاء. (والمرق) نظير ما غلب عليه غيرُهُ بالطَّبخ. وأما الماء الذي تغيّر بكثرة الأوراق الواقعة فيه حتى إذا رُفِعَ في الكف يظهرُ فيه لون الأوراق ، فلا يجوز به الوضوء لأنه كماء الباقلاء.
Or by cooking, like drinks and vinegar, and water of broad beans, and broth. (Or by cooking, like drinks and vinegar) similar to what is pressed from trees and fruits, so drink of rhubarb is pressed from trees, and drink of apple and the like is pressed from fruits. (And water of broad beans) similar to what other components have dominated it. (And broth) similar to what other than it has dominated by cooking. As for water that has changed due to the abundance of leaves falling into it, so that when lifted in the palm the color of the leaves appears in it, then wudu is not permissible with it because it is like water of broad beans.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from a commentary on a legal text. It explains that water which has lost its original character—either by being cooked or by having other substances dominate it—is not valid for wudu. The examples given are fruit drinks (like apple or rhubarb juice) which are extracted from trees or fruits, water of broad beans where other components have overwhelmed it, and broth where cooking has caused other substances to dominate. Additionally, water that has become discolored by many leaves is analogous to bean water and thus impermissible. This is the Hanafi position that such altered water is not pure (tahir) for wudu.

Key Terms
الأشربة (al-ashriba): Drinks made from pressing fruits or plants, like apple or rhubarb juice
الوضوء (al-wudu): Ritual washing of specific body parts for prayer
ماء الباقلاء (ma' al-baqila'): Water in which broad beans have been boiled, now thick and changed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Water that loses its original character due to cooking or being dominated by pure substances is not permissible for wudu.

When Does Stagnant Water Become Impure? The Hanafi Position in Context
Inter-madhhab dispute on impurity of stagnant water, with intra-Hanafi variation on defining 'flowing-like'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on when stagnant water becomes impure and compare it with the positions of the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Zahiri schools.

Core Idea
For the Hanafi school, only flowing water remains pure; stagnant water becomes impure regardless of change or quantity.

Purification with water is central to Islamic practice. But what happens when impurity falls into water? The four major schools differ markedly on this question. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents the Hanafi stance against three other madhhabs, and even shows internal Hanafi disagreement on how to define water that is 'like flowing'. By understanding this dispute, you'll grasp a key principle in Hanafi taharah: the emphasis on movement and connectivity over quantity or observable change.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the unique Hanafi ruling: only flowing water and water that is 'like flowing' (where impurity cannot cross) remains pure upon contact with najas. All other stagnant water becomes impure regardless of change or quantity.
This is a stricter view than the other schools.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their criteria
List the three other positions: Zahiris say water never becomes impure; Shafi'is set a quantity threshold (two qullas) and only consider impurity if change occurs below that; Malikis require a change in taste, color, or smell regardless of quantity.
Each school has a different threshold for impurity.
3
Locate the point of consensus
Note that all schools agree flowing water does not become impure unless the impurity's effect is visible.
This consensus is the starting point of the dispute.
4
Examine the Hanafi's unique criterion
Understand that the Hanafi criterion is not quantity or observable change but whether the impurity can reach the other side. This is based on the idea that non-flowing water is like a single body; if impurity enters one part, the whole becomes impure unless the water is in motion or so vast that the impurity cannot diffuse across.
This is derived from istihsan, not explicit text.
5
Explore the intra-Hanafi definitions
Review the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on what qualifies as 'flowing-like': some define by area (large size), others by practical tests like turbidity when bathing or dye dispersion when saffron is added.
This shows the Hanafi school's flexibility in applying the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بماء راكد وقع فيه نَجَسٌ إلا إذا كان . اعلم أنه قد وقع الاختلاف الكثير في تنجيس الماء الغير الجاري بوقوع النجاسة بين مجتهدي الأمة وأتباعهم مع اتفاقهم على أن الجاري لا يتنجس بوقوع النجاسة فيه ما لم يظهر أثرها فيه : فذهب الظاهرية إلى عدم تنجسه مطلقاً، وإن تغير أحد أوصافه أو كلّها بوقوع النجس، وهو مذهب يردّه العقل والنقل. وذهبت الشافعيّة إلى أنه إن كان مقدار القلتين لا ينجس ما لم يتغير أحد أوصافه، وإلا ينجس، وقدَّروا القُلّة - بضم القاف وتشديد اللام، يعني: الجرّة يسع قربتين ونصفاً. وذهبت المالكية إلى أنه لا ينجس مطلقاً إلا أن يتغير طعمه أو لونه أو ريحه بوقوع النجس، سواء كان أقل من القلتين أو أكثر. وأما أصحابنا فلم يقولوا بذاك ولا بهذا ، بل اختاروا بأن الجاري وما في حكم الجاري لا ينجس وما سواه ينجس، تغيّر أحد أوصافه أو لم يتغيّر، قلّة كان أو قلتين، أو أقل أو أكثر، وقالوا : ما في حكم الجاري هو ما لا يخلص فيه النجس بوقوعه فيه إلى الجانب الآخر، وقد وقع فيه اختلاف بينهم : فمنهم من حده بالمساحة. ومنهم من حده بغيرها. أما الذين حدوه بغير المساحة : فمنهم من حدَّه بالتكدّر، كما نقله صاحب «النهاية»: عن محمد بن سلام أنه إن كان بحال لو اغتسل فيه يتكدّر الجانب الآخر، فهو مما يخلص بعضه إلى بعض، وإن لم يصل فهو مما لا يخلص. ٢. ومنهم من حدَّه بالصبغ كما نقله في «النهاية» عن أبي حفص الكبير له أنه يلقى زعفران في جانب منه ، فإن أثر في الجانب الآخر كان مما يخلص بعضه إلى بعض ، وإلا فلا.
And not (by) stagnant water in which impurity has fallen, unless it is... Know that there occurred much disagreement regarding the impurity of non-flowing water by the occurrence of impurity among the mujtahids of the community and their followers, with their agreement that flowing water does not become impure by the occurrence of impurity in it unless its effect appears in it. The Zahiris held it is not impure absolutely, even if one or all of its attributes change by the impurity, a school that is rejected by reason and revelation. The Shafi'is held that if it is the quantity of two qullas, it does not become impure unless one of its attributes changes, otherwise it becomes impure; and they defined qulla (with damma on qaf and shadda on lam) meaning a jar which holds two and a half qirbas. The Malikis held that it does not become impure absolutely unless its taste or color or smell changes by the impurity, whether it is less than two qullas or more. As for our (Hanafi) scholars, they did not say this nor that, but they chose that flowing water and what is in the ruling of flowing water does not become impure, and other than that becomes impure, whether one of its attributes changes or not, whether it is a qulla or two qullas or less or more. And they said: What is in the ruling of flowing water is that in which the impurity, when it falls, does not reach the other side. And there occurred disagreement among them regarding it: some defined it by area measurement; some defined it by other than that. As for those who defined it by other than area measurement: some defined it by turbidity, as narrated by the author of al-Nihaya from Muhammad ibn Salam: that if it is such that if one bathes in it the other side becomes turbid, then some of it reaches some; and if not, then it does not. And some defined it by dyeing, as narrated in al-Nihaya from Abu Hafs al-Kabir: that saffron is thrown in one side; if it affects the other side, then some of it reaches some; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the impurity of stagnant water when najas (impurity) falls into it. It begins by noting consensus that flowing water is not impurified unless the impurity's effect is visible. The Zahiris hold water never becomes impure even if changed; the Shafi'is set a threshold of two qullas (approximately 270 liters) below which it is impure if unchanged; the Malikis require a change in taste, color, or smell. The Hanafis take a unique position: only flowing water and water that is 'like flowing' (i.e., where the impurity cannot reach the other side) remain pure; otherwise, any stagnant water becomes impure regardless of change or quantity. Within the Hanafi school, there is further ikhtilaf on how to determine if water is 'like flowing': some use area (likely a large size) and others use practical tests like turbidity or dye dispersion. This shows the Hanafi reliance on istihsan and practical reasoning, distinguishing their approach from other schools that focus on quantity or detectable change.

Key Terms
الراكد (al-rakid): Stagnant or still water that does not flow
القلتين (al-qullatayn): Two qullas, a volume measure: each qulla is a jar holding about two and a half qirbas (approx 135 liters each, total ~270 liters).
الجاري (al-jari): Flowing water (like rivers) that is pure unless impurity's effect is visible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in the passage, which type of water becomes impure upon the fall of impurity?
A) Water that changes in color, taste, or smell
B) Water that is less than two qullas
C) Stagnant water that is not like flowing water
D) Flowing water if the impurity is visible

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely holds that stagnant water becomes impure regardless of change or quantity; only flowing or 'like-flowing' water remains pure.

Taharah — Purification

The Hanafi Ruling on Flowing Water: Between Madhhabs and Within the School
Inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi exposition on water impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Maliki and Hanafi positions on water impurity, explain the Hanafi concept of 'flowing water' (jari), and identify the relied-upon method to determine if water is in its ruling.

Core Idea
Flowing water never becomes impure, even without change; stagnant water is impure even without change unless it is large — but defining 'flowing' is a matter of judgment.

Water purity is a fundamental issue in taharah. While the Maliki school holds that water is never impure unless its properties change, the Hanafi school distinguishes between flowing and stagnant water. However, Hanafi scholars themselves disagreed on how to determine whether water is 'flowing' or not. This passage presents that internal discussion and the preferred view that delegates the determination to the person's own judgment (ra'y al-mubtala).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions on water impurity
First, note the Maliki position: water is absolutely pure unless its taste, color, or smell change. Then note the Hanafi position: flowing water and what is in its ruling is pure, while other water becomes impure even without change (if less than two qullahs).
This is an inter-madhhab dispute on the threshold for impurity.
2
Understand the key concept: 'what is in the ruling of flowing water'
The Hanafi school defines this as water where impurity does not penetrate (khulūṣ) to the other side. The rest of the lesson explores various tests to determine penetration.
Penetration is the operative criterion.
3
Survey the intra-Hanafi views on testing penetration
Read through the different methods: area measurement, turbidity test (bathing), dye test (saffron), agitation test (tahrik). These are all attempts to operationalize the concept of 'no penetration'.
These are ziyadat (additional) from later scholars.
4
Locate the relied-upon view: personal judgment (ra'y al-mubtala)
The text states that delegating to the person's ghalabat al-zann (overwhelming probability) is the origin of the madhhab and the chosen view among early and verifying later scholars.
Imam Muhammad's example in the Muwatta' about large and small basins supports this.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider a large pond connected to a stream. According to the relied-upon view, one uses one's own judgment: if it is overwhelmingly likely that impurity from a fallen animal would not reach the other side, the water is considered flowing and remains pure unless properties change.
This aligns with the Hanafi principle of 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions) in a broader sense of considering the person's reasonable estimation.

ومنهم: من حده بالتحريك.
ومنهم: مَن فوَّضه إلى رأي المبتلى، وهذا هو أصل مذهب أئمتنا، والمختار عند قدمائهم، وجمع من محققي متأخريهم.

Book of Purification / Categories of Water
... its attributes, otherwise it becomes impure. And they estimated the qullah (with damma on qaf and tashdid on lam), meaning a jar that holds two and a half qirbas.
The Malikiyya went to that it is absolutely not impure unless its taste, color, or smell changes due to the fall of najas, whether it is less than two qullahs or more.
And I have elaborated exhaustively in mentioning their proofs, and mentioning what refutes them, and what defends them, with reference to tarjih and the sound opinion in 'al-Si'ayah'; whoever wishes to consult it should refer to it. As for our companions (Hanafis), they did not say this nor that, but they chose that flowing water and that which is in the ruling of flowing water does not become impure, and other than that becomes impure, whether one of its attributes changes or not, whether it is one qullah, two qullahs, or less or more. And they said: what is in the ruling of flowing water is that in which the najas when falling into it does not penetrate to the other side. And there is disagreement among them:
Some of them defined it by area.
Some of them defined it by other than area.
As for those who defined it by other than area:
Some of them defined it by turbidity, as transmitted by the author of 'al-Nihayah' from Muhammad ibn Salam that if it is such that if one bathes in it the other side becomes turbid, then it is of the kind where some of it reaches some; if not, then it is of the kind that does not reach.
Some of them defined it by dye, as transmitted in 'al-Nihayah' from Abu Hafs al-Kabir: that saffron is thrown into one side; if it affects the other side, then it is of the kind where some reaches some; otherwise no.
Some of them defined it by agitation.
Some of them delegated it to the judgment of the person tested, and this is the basis of the madhhab of our imams, and the chosen view among their predecessors and a group of verifying later scholars.
Muhammad said in his Muwatta': 'If the basin is large such that if you agitate one side the other side does not move, then that water is not spoiled by the drinking of beasts nor by filth in it, unless it overcomes its smell or taste. If it is a small basin such that if one part is agitated the other part moves, and beasts drink from it or filth falls into it, then one should not perform wudu from it.' End quote. And the explanation of this expression along with its pros and cons I have completed in 'al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad' – let one refer to it. Ibn al-Humam said in 'Fath al-Qadir': 'Abu Hanifa said: according to the apparent narration, the greater judgment of the person tested is considered. If he overwhelmingly thinks that najasa reaches the other side, wudu is not permitted; otherwise it is permitted. From him (Abu Hanifa) there are narrations of considering it by agitation as mentioned in the book – i.e., al-Hidayah – by bathing or wudu or hand. The first is more correct according to a group including al-Karkhi, the author of 'al-Ghayah' and 'al-Yanabi'' and others, and it is more consistent with the principle of Abu Hanifa.' End quote.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and intra-Hanafi exposition on the impurity of water. The author first states the Maliki position: water never becomes impure unless its taste, color, or smell changes, regardless of quantity. Then he mentions the Hanafi position: flowing water (jari) and water in its ruling does not become impure regardless of change, but stagnant water (other than that) becomes impure even without change if it is less than two qullahs. The key concept is 'flowing' – water where najas does not penetrate to the other side. However, Hanafi scholars differed on how to determine this penetration. Some used area (masaha), some used turbidity (by bathing), some used dye (throwing saffron), some used agitation (tahrik) – whether moving one side causes movement in the other. The most relied-upon view is to delegate to the person's own judgment (ra'y al-mubtala) based on overwhelming probability. Imam Muhammad's Muwatta example explains large and small basins. Ibn al-Humam confirms that Abu Hanifa's relied-upon position is to consider the person's judgment, not physical agitation, and that this is consistent with his principles.

Key Terms
الجاري (al-jārī): Flowing water, typically from a spring or river, that is moving and whose parts are in motion.
الخلوص (al-khulūṣ): The penetration or transmission of impurity from one side of the water to the other; if it does not penetrate, the water is considered 'flowing' in ruling.
التحريك (al-taḥrīk): The test of agitating one side of the water to see if the other side moves; if it does, the water is considered interconnected and thus not flowing.
رأي المبتلى (ra'y al-mubtalā): The personal judgment of the person facing the situation, based on their reasonable estimation (ghalabat al-zann) of whether impurity would reach the other side.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what is the primary method to determine if water is 'like flowing water' (ma fi hukm al-jari)?
A) Measuring the area of the water.
B) Throwing saffron to see if it dyes the other side.
C) The personal judgment (ghalabat al-zann) of the person.
D) Agitating the water to see if the other side moves.

Key Takeaway
Flowing water and what is in its ruling does not become impure by mere contact with najas; the relied-upon method to determine this is the person's own judgment of whether impurity penetrates to the other side.

The Large Pond: Stirring Test vs. Testifier's Opinion
Debating the criteria for a large pond: stirring vs. opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi criteria for determining a large pond and explain why the testifier's predominant opinion is the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the testifier's predominant opinion, not the stirring test alone, determines whether a pond is large enough to resist impurity.

When impurity falls into a pond, its size determines whether the water remains pure. The Hanafi school offers two criteria: a practical stirring test and the testifier's subjective opinion. This lesson unpacks the internal debate, the proofs cited, and why later scholars preferred the testifier's opinion as the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two criteria mentioned: (1) stirring the water to see if the other side moves, and (2) the testifier's predominant opinion (ra'y al-mubtala). Note that the second is called 'the foundation of the school'.
The passage presents both as options within the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Some Hanafi scholars, including Imam Muhammad in his Muwatta', rely solely on the stirring test. Understand their literalist approach.
The opponent is not another madhhab but an intra-Hanafi faction.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Imam Muhammad's Muwatta' provides a hadith stating that a pond is large if stirring one side does not move the other. This is a textual proof (dalil naqli).
The hadith is a sound (but ahad) narration.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The preferred view argues that the stirring test is not always feasible and that the testifier's opinion better aligns with Abu Hanifa's principles (usul) of avoiding harm (haraj).
Al-Karkhi and Ibn al-Humam support this tarjih.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: If a pond does not move completely but the testifier believes impurity reaches all parts, ablution is invalid under the preferred view, but valid under the stirring-only view.
This difference affects daily practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم : من حده بالتحريك (١). ومنهم : مَن فوَّضه إلى رأي المبتلى، وهذا هو أصل مذهب أئمتنا، والمختار عند قدمائهم، وجمع من محققي متأخريهم). فقال محمد في «مؤطئه» : «إن كان الحوض عظيماً إن حركت ناحيته لم تتحرك الناحية الأخرى لم يفسد ذلك الماء، ما ما ولغ فيه من سبع ولا ما فيه قذر، إلا أن يغلب على ريحه أو طعمه، فإذا كان حوضاً صغيراً إن تحرَّكت منه ناحية تحركت الناحية الأخرى ، فولغ فيه السباع أو وقع فيه القذر، فلا يتوضأ منه. انتهى (۳) وشرح هذه العبارة مع ما لها وما عليها قد فرغتُ عنه في «التعليق الممجّد على (٤) موطأ محمد فليرجع إليه. وقال ابن الهمام في فتح القدير»: «قال أبو حنيفة : في ظاهر الرواية يعتبر فيه أكبر رأي المبتلى إن غلب على ظنه أنه بحيث تصل النجاسة إلى الجانب الآخر لا يجوز الوضوء منه ، وإلا جاز، وعنه له اعتباره بالتحريك على ما هو مذكور في الكتاب - أي «الهداية» - بالاغتسال أو بالوضوء أو باليد روايات، والأول أصح عند جماعة منهم : الكرخي، وصاحب «الغاية» و«الينابيع وغيرهم»، وهو الأليق بأصل أبـي حنيفة». انتهى
And among them: some defined it by stirring (1). And among them: some delegated it to the opinion of the testifier, and this is the foundation of the school of our imams, and the chosen view among their early scholars and a group of their later verifiers. So Muhammad said in his Muwatta': 'If the pond is large such that if you stir one side, the other side does not move, that water is not spoiled by what a wild animal laps nor by any impurity, unless it overcomes its smell or taste. But if it is a small pond such that if one side moves, the other side moves, and wild animals lap in it or impurity falls into it, then one does not perform ablution from it.' End. And the explanation of this statement with its pros and cons I have already written in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad, so let one refer to it. And Ibn al-Humam said in Fath al-Qadir: 'Abu Hanifa said: In the apparent transmission, the greatest opinion of the testifier is considered: if he predominantly thinks that impurity reaches the other side, ablution is not permissible from it; otherwise, it is permissible. And from him (Abu Hanifa) there is also consideration by stirring as mentioned in the book (i.e., al-Hidayah) with narrations of using a full bath, or ablution, or the hand. The first is more authentic according to a group including al-Karkhi, the author of al-Ghayah and al-Yanabi' and others, and it is most suitable to the foundation of Abu Hanifa.' End.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on defining a large pond (hawd kabir) for water purity. Two criteria are given: (1) the stirring test: if moving one side does not cause the other side to stir, the pond is large; (2) the testifier's predominant opinion (ra'y al-mubtala): if the person testing strongly believes that impurity reaches all parts, it is considered small. The author notes that the second method is the foundation of the Hanafi school and chosen by early scholars. Imam Muhammad in his Muwatta' explicitly uses the stirring criterion, which is a hadith-based ruling. Ibn al-Humam reports that Abu Hanifa's relied-upon view is the testifier's opinion, though there are also narrations of using stirring. The preferred view (as chosen by al-Karkhi and others) is the testifier's opinion, as it aligns with Abu Hanifa's principles.

Key Terms
الحوض (al-hawd): A pond or cistern of water; in this context, a body of standing water used for purification.
التحريك (al-tahrik): Stirring or moving part of the water to see if the rest moves; a practical measure to determine if the pond is large.
رأي المبتلى (ra'y al-mubtala): The opinion or predominant belief of the person testing the water; subjective but authoritative criterion in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, what is the primary criterion for determining whether a pond is large (kabir)?
A) Stirring the water to see if it moves completely
B) The testifier's predominant opinion about whether impurity reaches all parts
C) The pond's area being exactly ten by ten cubits
D) The absence of any change in taste, color, or smell

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the testifier's predominant opinion is the primary criterion for determining the size of a pond, not the stirring test alone.

Area-Based Definition of a Large Pond in Hanafi Fiqh
Listing various area measurements for defining a large pond, with preference for 10×10 cubits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the various area-based definitions of a large pond in Hanafi fiqh, state which became the relied-upon fatwa, and explain why it differs from the original school position.

Core Idea
A large pond is defined by area; the 10×10 cubit estimate became the common fatwa but is not Abu Hanifa's original teaching.

In this passage, the author surveys the different area measurements used by Hanafi scholars to define a large pond for the purposes of taharah. While many later scholars adopted the convenient 10×10 cubit measure and gave fatwa accordingly, the passage cautions that this is not the authentic position of Abu Hanifa and his immediate disciples. Understanding this distinction is crucial for tracing the development of the school's fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three initial opinions
List the three area estimates: 8×8, 12×12, and 15×15 cubits. These are the views of some scholars within the Hanafi school.
2
Recognize the preferred fatwa
A group of later scholars (including Qadi Khan, authors of al-Hidayah, al-Khulasah, al-Tatarkhaniyyah) chose 10×10 cubits and gave fatwa accordingly, considering it easier and more precise.
This became the widely accepted fatwa in practice.
3
Understand the geometry
For a square pond, each side must be 10 cubits, giving an area of 100 sq. cubits. For other shapes, the area must be equivalent to 100 sq. cubits if squared.
The perimeter is 40 cubits, but the area is the decisive measure.
4
Distinguish the cubit types
The cubit used is disputed: the surveying cubit (7 fists with standing thumb), the cloth cubit (6 fists without thumb), or another variant (7 fists without thumb). This affects the actual size of the pond.
The fatwa likely uses the surveying cubit.
5
Evaluate the authority
The passage explicitly states that the 10×10 fatwa is not the true school of Abu Hanifa and his two companions, despite its widespread acceptance. This highlights the difference between the original school (zahir al-riwayah) and later fatwa practice (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
Always check the authority weight of a ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذين قدروه بالمساحة :
١. فمنهم من جعل الكبير ما كان بقدر ثمان في ثمان، وما عداه قليلاً.
۲. ومنهم من اختار اثني عشر في اثنا عشر.
. ومنهم : من اختار خمسة عشر في خمسة عشر.
واختار جمع من أصحابنا التقدير بعشر في عشر، وأفتوا به منهم : قاضي خان في «فتاواه»(۱) ، وصاحب الهداية» فيها ، وفي «مختارات النوازل»، وصاحب «الخلاصة»، و«التاتارخانية»، وهو قول أبي سليمان الجوزجاني ، واختاره كثير من المتأخرين (٢) ، وفرعوا عليه فروعاً، وبنوا عليه نكات بناءً على أنه أسهل وأضبط ، حتى اشتهر في ما بينهم أنه مذهب أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه ، وقد عرفت أنه ليس كذلك.
[١) قوله : في عشرة أذرع ؛ أي بأن يكون كلّ ضلع منه عشرة أذرع ، فيكون حول الماء أربعين ذراعاً، ووجهه مئة ذراع هذا إذا كان الحوض مربعاً بأن تتساوى أضلاعه الأربعة، لما تقرر في علم المساحة أن مساحة المربع تكون بضرب ضلع واحد من أضلاعه في نفسه، وإن كان مدوّراً أو مثلثاً أو نحو ذلك، يعتبر فيه أن يكون بحيث لو ربع صارَ عشراً في عشر. وهل المعتبر ذراع المساحة، ومقداره : سبع قبضات، فوق كل قبضة إبهام قائمة ، والمراد بالقبضة الأصابع الأربعة المضمونة، أو ذراع الكرباس، ومقداره : ست قبضات من دون قيام الأصبع كما في «الفتح» (۳) ، أو سبع قبضات بدون قيام الأصبع كما في «الظهيرية».
And as for those who estimated it by area: 1. Among them are those who made the large one what is as much as eight by eight, and anything else is small. 2. Among them are those who chose twelve by twelve. 3. Among them are those who chose fifteen by fifteen. And a group of our companions chose the estimate of ten by ten and gave fatwa with it, among them: Qadi Khan in his Fatawa, the author of al-Hidayah therein and in Mukhtarat al-Nawazil, the author of al-Khulasah, and al-Tatarkhaniyyah. It is the saying of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, and many later scholars chose it. They derived many subsidiary rulings and built upon it narratives, based on it being easier and more precise, until it became famous among them that it is the school of Abu Hanifa and his two companions, but you have known it is not so. [Saying: 'in ten cubits by ten cubits' means that each side is ten cubits, so the perimeter of the water is forty cubits, and its area is one hundred cubits; this if the pond is square with equal sides, as established in geometry that the area of a square is obtained by multiplying one side by itself. And if it is round or triangular or similar, it is considered such that if squared it would become ten by ten. And is the relevant measure the cubit of area (dhirā' al-misāha), its amount being seven fists, above each fist a standing thumb, and by 'fist' is meant the four fingers closed? Or the cubit of cloth (dhirā' al-karbās), its amount being six fists without raising the thumb, as in al-Fath? Or seven fists without raising the thumb, as in al-Zahīriyyah?
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage surveys different area-based definitions of a large pond. Three opinions exist: 8x8, 12x12, and 15x15 cubits. A group of Hanafi scholars, including Qadi Khan, the author of al-Hidayah, and others, chose 10x10 cubits and gave fatwa accordingly, relying on the authority of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani and many later scholars. This became widely adopted as the school's position, though the author notes it is not the original teaching of Abu Hanifa and his disciples. The passage then clarifies the geometry: for a square pond, each side must be ten cubits; for other shapes, the equivalent area of 100 square cubits. Finally, the measurement of the cubit itself is disputed: the surveying cubit (seven fists with thumb upright) or the cloth cubit (six fists without thumb) or another variant.

Key Terms
المساحة (al-misāha): Geometric area measurement; used to define the size of a pond by its dimensions.
ذِرَاع (dhirā'): A unit of length; the cubit. There is a difference between the surveying cubit and the cloth cubit.
قَبْضَة (qabda): A fist; used as a unit to define the length of a cubit. A fist is the width of four closed fingers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which area estimate for a large pond became widely used in fatwa but is not the original school of Abu Hanifa?
A) 8×8 cubits
B) 10×10 cubits
C) 12×12 cubits
D) 15×15 cubits

Key Takeaway
The 10×10 cubit definition for a large pond is a later fatwa convenience, not the original teaching of Abu Hanifa; students must distinguish between fatwa and original school.

The Measurement of a Large Pool: Ten by Ten Cubits
Explores the different opinions on measuring a large pool

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the ten-by-ten cubit measurement is the relied-upon Hanafi position and identify the earlier divergent opinions.

Core Idea
A large pool is ten by ten cubits: a later consensus, not the founding Imams' own view.

The Hanafi school needed a practical standard to define a 'large pool' (kathir) that remains pure if impurity falls. The early imams did not fix a measurement, leading to various estimates. Later jurists settled on ten cubits by ten cubits as the easiest and most precise.

Guided Path
1
List the different measurements proposed by Hanafi scholars
From the text, identify the various estimates for a large pool: eight by eight, twelve by twelve, fifteen by fifteen, and ten by ten.
Note that these are all intra-madhhab opinions; none is directly from the founding imams.
2
Identify the proponents of the ten-by-ten measurement
The text names Qadi Khan, al-Hidayah, al-Khulasah, al-Tatarkhaniyyah, and Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani as those who chose ten by ten and gave fatwa.
These are later authorities (mutakhkhirin).
3
Understand the reasoning behind the choice
The text says they chose ten by ten because it is easier and more precise (ahwal wa adbat).
Pragmatic consideration for fatwa.
4
Recognize the authority weight
The commentator notes that although this became famous as the madhhab, it is not the actual position of Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Sahibayn).
The ruling is from the fatawa mutakhkhirin, not zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text

كتاب الطهارة/ أقسام المياه عشرة أذرع في عشرة أذرع وأما الذين قدروه بالمساحة : فمنهم من جعل الكبير ما كان بقدر ثمان في ثمان، وما عداه قليلاً. ۲. ومنهم من اختار اثني عشر في اثنا عشر. . ومنهم : من اختار خمسة عشر في خمسة عشر. واختار جمع من أصحابنا التقدير بعشر في عشر، وأفتوا به منهم : قاضي خان في «فتاواه»، وصاحب الهداية» فيها ، وفي «مختارات النوازل»، وصاحب «الخلاصة»، و«التاتارخانية»، وهو قول أبي سليمان الجوزجاني ، واختاره كثير من المتأخرين ، وفرعوا عليه فروعاً، وبنوا عليه نكات بناءً على أنه أسهل وأضبط ، حتى اشتهر في ما بينهم أنه مذهب أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه ، وقد عرفت أنه ليس كذلك. [١) قوله : في عشرة أذرع ؛ أي بأن يكون كلّ ضلع منه عشرة أذرع ، فيكون حول الماء أربعين ذراعاً، ووجهه مئة ذراع هذا إذا كان الحوض مربعاً بأن تتساوى أضلاعه الأربعة، لما تقرر في علم المساحة أن مساحة المربع تكون بضرب ضلع واحد من أضلاعه في نفسه، وإن كان مدوّراً أو مثلثاً أو نحو ذلك، يعتبر فيه أن يكون بحيث لو ربع صارَ عشراً في عشر. وهل المعتبر ذراع المساحة، ومقداره : سبع قبضات، فوق كل قبضة إبهام قائمة ، والمراد بالقبضة الأصابع الأربعة المضمونة، أو ذراع الكرباس، ومقداره : ست قبضات من دون قيام الأصبع كما في «الفتح» ، أو سبع قبضات بدون قيام الأصبع كما في «الظهيرية». ولا تنحسر الأرض بالغرف) ، فحكمه حكم الماء الجاري : فإن كانت " النَّجاسة مرئية لا يُتَوَضًا من موضع النَّجاسة فيه قولان مصححان ، فاختار صاحب الهداية» و«الخلاصة»، «وخزانة المفتيين» الثاني ، واختار أكثرهم منهم : قاضي خان الأوّل ؛ لكونه أليق بالمسموحات. [١]قوله : ولا تنحسر ؛ في تقدير العمق أقوال ذكرها الزَّاهِدِي في «المجتبى» : أحدها : إنّ ما لم يبلغ الكعب فليس بمعتبر. وثانيهما : إن كان مقدار شبر فهو كثير، وما قلّ منه قليل. وثالثها : إن كان ذراعاً فهو كثير، وإلا فهو قليل. ورابعها : ما اختاره المصنّف تبعاً لما صححه صاحب «الهداية» و«الخلاصة» و«الظهيرية»: إنّه إن انكشف أرضُ الحوض بالغرف فهو قليل، وإلا فهو كثير. وفيه أقوال أخر أيضاً أوردناها في «السعاية) فليرجع إليه. (٢) قوله : فحكمه ؛ الفاء إما تفريعية : يعني لما جاز الوضوء من حوض يكون عشراً في عشر علم منه أن حكمه حكم الجاري في أنه يجوز الوضوء منه، وإن وقع فيه نجس، وإما تعليلية، والمقصود منه ذكرُ علّة الحكم الذي ذكره المصنف له بالاستثناء. [٣] قوله : فإن كانت ... الخ ؛ الظاهر أنّ الفاء للتفريع، وحينئذ يرد عليه : أنّ الماء الجاري إذا كان ما لا يلاقي النجاسة منه أكثر مما يلاقي جازَ الوضوء في أسفله، فإذا كان حكم العشر في العشر حكم الجاري، ينبغي أن يكون حكمه كذلك، لا ما ذكره. وحاصل المسألة : النجاسة الواقعة في الحوض لا تخلو: إما أن تكون مرئية : كالعذرة والدم.
Book of Purification / Categories of Water. Ten cubits by ten cubits. As for those who estimated by area: some considered large as that which is eight by eight, and what is less than that is little. Some chose twelve by twelve. Some chose fifteen by fifteen. A group of our companions chose the estimation of ten by ten and gave fatwa accordingly, among them: Qadi Khan in his 'Fatawa', the author of 'al-Hidayah' in it and in 'Mukhtarat al-Nawazil', the author of 'al-Khulasah', and 'al-Tatarkhaniyyah', and it is the saying of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, and many later scholars chose it. They derived many branches from it and built points upon it because it is easier and more precise, until it became famous among them that it is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and his two companions, but you know it is not like that. [Footnote] His saying: 'ten cubits' means that each side is ten cubits, so the water's perimeter is forty cubits and its area is one hundred square cubits. This is if the basin is square with equal sides. As established in geometry, the area of a square is found by multiplying one side by itself. If it is circular, triangular, or otherwise, it is considered in such a way that if squared it would become ten by ten. And is the considered cubit the measuring cubit, which is seven fists with each fist having a raised thumb—meaning the four fingers held together—or the cloth cubit, which is six fists without raising the thumb as in 'al-Fath', or seven fists without raising the thumb as in 'al-Zahiriyyah'? And the ground does not become exposed by scooping, so its ruling is the ruling of running water. If the impurity is visible, one may not perform wudu from the spot of impurity. There are two sound opinions. The author of 'al-Hidayah', 'al-Khulasah', and 'Khizaanat al-Muftiyyin' chose the second, but most of them, including Qadi Khan, chose the first because it is more in line with the general principles. [Footnote] His saying: 'does not become exposed'—there are statements on the estimation of depth mentioned by al-Zahidi in 'al-Mujtaba': One: what does not reach the ankle is not considered. Second: if it is a span, it is much, and what is less than that is little. Third: if it is a cubit, it is much, otherwise it is little. Fourth: what the author chose following what was declared correct by the author of 'al-Hidayah', 'al-Khulasah', and 'al-Zahiriyyah': if the bottom of the basin becomes exposed by scooping, then it is little; otherwise it is much. There are also other statements which we have presented in 'al-Si'ayah', so refer to it. (2) His saying: 'so its ruling'—the fa is either for branching, meaning: since wudu is permitted from a basin that is ten by ten, it is understood that its ruling is the ruling of running water in that wudu is permitted from it even if impurity falls into it; or it is for causation, and the intent is to mention the reason for the ruling which the author gave by exception. [3] His saying: 'If it is...'—apparently the fa is for branching, and then an objection arises: if running water, when the part not contacting the impurity is more than the part contacting, then wudu is allowed at its lower end, then if the ruling of ten by ten is the same as running water, its ruling should be the same, not what he mentioned. The conclusion of the issue: impurity that falls into the basin is either visible, like stool or blood.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of a large pool of water in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by presenting the different opinions among Hanafi scholars on the minimum area that constitutes a large pool (kathir). Some said eight by eight cubits, others twelve by twelve, some fifteen by fifteen. However, many later Hanafi authorities, including Qadi Khan, the author of al-Hidayah, and others, chose ten by ten cubits as the standard. They considered it easier and more precise, and it became widely accepted as the madhhab. The commentator notes, however, that this is not the actual position of Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), though the ten-by-ten measurement is the operative fatwa among later scholars. The commentary then explains that if the pool is square, ten by ten means each side is ten cubits, giving an area of one hundred square cubits. If the pool is circular or triangular, it should be such that if squared it would be ten by ten. There is also a discussion on the type of cubit used: the measuring cubit (seven fists with thumb extended) or the cloth cubit (six fists without thumb or seven fists without thumb). The next part addresses the depth condition: the water is considered large (kathir) only if the ground does not become exposed by scooping. Various opinions on the required depth are given: ankle-deep, one span, one cubit, or the correct view according to al-Hidayah that if the bottom becomes exposed by scooping it is little, otherwise it is much. The ruling of such a pool is that it is like running water: if impurity falls into it, wudu is valid as long as the water is not visibly changed. However, if the impurity is visible, there are two opinions: one prohibits wudu from that spot (the view of Qadi Khan), and the other permits it (the view of al-Hidayah and others), with the latter being more in line with the general principle of running water. The commentator then addresses a potential objection regarding the analogy to running water and clarifies the logic.

Key Terms
عشرة أذرع في عشرة أذرع ('asharat adhru' fi 'asharat adhru'): A square pool measuring ten cubits on each side, giving a total area of one hundred square cubits, considered the minimum for a large pool by many later Hanafi scholars.
الذراع (al-dhira'): A unit of length; two types are mentioned: the measuring cubit (7 fists with thumb) and the cloth cubit (6 fists without thumb or 7 fists without thumb).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which measurement for a large pool is the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) among later Hanafi scholars?
A) Eight by eight cubits
B) Ten by ten cubits
C) Twelve by twelve cubits
D) Fifteen by fifteen cubits

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi asks for the minimum size of a large pool; the relied-upon answer is ten by ten cubits, but we must know this is a later ijtihad, not a direct transmission from the founding imams.

Depth Condition and the Ruling of Large Water as Running Water
Discusses the depth condition and the ruling of large water as running water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the depth condition for a large pool and apply the ruling that it is treated like running water when impurity falls into it.

Core Idea
A large pool must be deep enough that the ground stays covered; it then has the same ruling as running water.

After settling on the area measurement, Hanafi jurists also needed to specify a depth condition. Without sufficient depth, the water might not be considered 'large' if the bottom becomes exposed. Once the pool meets both area and depth criteria, its ruling mirrors that of running water: impurity does not render the entire pool impure unless it visibly changes the water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the depth condition from the text
The text says 'la tanhasiru al-ardu bi al-ghurf' - the ground does not become exposed by scooping. This is the key practical test: if you can scoop water and the bottom is still covered, the pool is considered deep enough.
This is a condition, not a fixed measurement.
2
List the different opinions on depth
The text mentions four opinions: ankle-deep, a span, a cubit, and the chosen view (from al-Hidayah) that if scooping exposes the bottom it is little, otherwise much.
The preferred view is the functional test rather than a fixed number.
3
Understand the ruling of a large pool
A pool that satisfies area and depth conditions has the same ruling as running water (hukm al-ma' al-jari): it is not rendered impure by impure contact unless the impurity changes its color, taste, or odor.
This is a major dispensation (rukhsah).
4
Analyze the dispute about visible impurity
If impurity is visible, there are two opinions: one says wudu is not valid from the spot (Qadi Khan), the other says it is permissible (al-Hidayah, al-Khulasah). The second is considered more consistent with the analogy to running water.
The commentator notes an objection to the first opinion based on the running water analogy.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of a large pool of water in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by presenting the different opinions among Hanafi scholars on the minimum area that constitutes a large pool (kathir). Some said eight by eight cubits, others twelve by twelve, some fifteen by fifteen. They considered it easier and more precise, and it became widely accepted as the madhhab. There is also a discussion on the type of cubit used: the measuring cubit (seven fists with thumb extended) or the cloth cubit (six fists without thumb or seven fists without thumb). The commentator then addresses a potential objection regarding the analogy to running water and clarifies the logic.

Key Terms
لا تنحسر الأرض بالغرف (la tanhasiru al-ardu bi al-ghurf): The condition that when water is scooped from the pool, the ground does not become exposed, indicating sufficient depth.
حكم الماء الجاري (hukm al-ma' al-jari): A pool of ten by ten with proper depth is treated like running water in that it does not become impure by contact with impurity unless the impurity changes it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred depth opinion (from al-Hidayah), if scooping water exposes the bottom of a pool, what is the pool considered?
A) Large (kathir), ignoring the exposure
B) Small (qalil), because the depth is insufficient
C) Neither; a different measurement method is needed
D) It depends on the area of the pool

Key Takeaway
A large pool must be deep enough that the ground does not appear when water is scooped; then it is like a river – impurity does not spread unless it changes the water.

Defining Much Water: Ground Not Exposed by Scooping
A definitional ruling expanded by later commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for water to be considered kathir and explain why the preferred criterion is exposure by scooping rather than fixed depth.

Core Idea
Water in a tank is 'much' if scooping does not expose its ground — a practical, case-based criterion.

This lesson examines the Hanafi definition of 'much water' (kathir) as stated in Al-Hidayah. While some scholars attempted to quantify depth, the authoritative view relies on the observable effect of scooping. This distinction impacts rulings on purification when impurity falls into the water: kathir water remains pure unless its properties change, while qalil water becomes impure upon any contact with impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Understand that the water in a tank of ten by ten cubits is treated like flowing water: impurity does not render it najis unless it visibly changes.
The ruling hinges on whether the water is 'much' (kathir) or 'little' (qalil).
2
Analyze the stated condition
The matn states: the water is kathir if scooping does not expose the ground. Focus on the word 'لا تنحسر' (does not become exposed).
This condition is a practical test, not a fixed measurement.
3
Recognize the depth measurement dispute
The commentary lists four opinions about depth: ankle, span, cubit, and exposure. Understand that these represent scholarly attempts to codify the condition.
Most of these opinions come from later authorities, not the Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Identify the preferred opinion
The author follows the tarjih of Al-Hidayah, Al-Khulasah, and Al-Zahiriyyah: the water is qalil if the ground becomes exposed; otherwise it is kathir.
This view prioritizes the observable effect over arbitrary depth.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a tank, check whether scooping reveals the bottom. If yes, water is little; if no, it is much. Then apply the relevant impurity ruling.
This is the operative rule for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تنحسر الأرض بالغرف، فحكمه حكم الماء الجاري: فإن كانت النَّجاسة مرئية لا يُتَوَضًا من موضع النَّجاسة فيه قولان مصححان، فاختار صاحب الهداية» و«الخلاصة»، «وخزانة المفتيين» الثاني، واختار أكثرهم منهم: قاضي خان الأوّل؛ لكونه أليق بالمسموحات. [قوله: ولا تنحسر؛ في تقدير العمق أقوال ذكرها الزَّاهِدِي في «المجتبى»: أحدها: إنّ ما لم يبلغ الكعب فليس بمعتبر. وثانيهما: إن كان مقدار شبر فهو كثير، وما قلّ منه قليل. وثالثها: إن كان ذراعاً فهو كثير، وإلا فهو قليل. ورابعها: ما اختاره المصنّف تبعاً لما صححه صاحب «الهداية» و«الخلاصة» و«الظهيرية»: إنّه إن انكشف أرضُ الحوض بالغرف فهو قليل، وإلا فهو كثير. وفيه أقوال أخر أيضاً أوردناها في «السعاية» فليرجع إليه.
And the ground does not become exposed by scooping, so its ruling is the ruling of flowing water. If the impurity is visible, one does not perform ablution from the place of the impurity; there are two authenticated opinions. The author of Al-Hidayah, Al-Khulasah, and Khizanat al-Muftiyin chose the second opinion. Most of them (including Qadi Khan) chose the first, because it is more suitable to allowances. [His statement: 'And does not become exposed'; regarding determining depth, there are statements mentioned by al-Zahidi in Al-Mujtaba: One: What does not reach the ankle is not considered. Two: If it is a span, it is much; what is less than that is little. Three: If it is a cubit, it is much; otherwise it is little. Four: What the author chose, following what was authenticated by the author of Al-Hidayah, Al-Khulasah, and Al-Zahiriyyah: that if the ground of the tank is exposed by scooping, it is little; otherwise it is much. There are also other statements that we mentioned in Al-Si'ayah, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage defines when a water tank (حوض) of ten by ten cubits retains the status of 'much water' (ماء كثير), which is treated like flowing water regarding impurity. The matn states the basic condition: the ground of the tank must not become exposed by scooping. If scooping does not expose the bottom, the water is considered 'much' (كثير) and can be used for purification even if impurity falls into it, provided the impurity does not change its properties. The commentator then discusses disagreements among Hanafi scholars about the precise measurement that constitutes 'not exposing ground'. He lists four opinions: (1) depth not reaching the ankle is disregarded; (2) a span's depth makes it 'much'; (3) a cubit's depth makes it 'much'; (4) the preferred opinion (also chosen by the author) is that if the bottom becomes exposed by scooping, it is 'little', otherwise 'much'. The author refers to his other work Al-Si'ayah for further details. This is a technical definition: the criterion is not an absolute depth but the fact of exposure, which pragmatically relates to the tank's size.

Key Terms
غرف (gharf): Scooping or taking water out of the tank with a container.
كثير (kathir): Water that does not become impure by mere contact with impurity unless it changes; here defined by the condition that the ground does not become exposed by scooping.
الكعب (al-ka'b): The ankle bone; used as a measure of depth in some opinions.
حوض (hawd): A water tank, typically of size ten by ten cubits in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Water in a ten-by-ten tank is 'much' if scooping does not expose the ground – a simple, observable condition that determines its purity ruling upon contact with impurity.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Impurity in a Large Water Tank – Three Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on impurity in a large water tank

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions on using water from a large tank after impurity falls in, and state which view is preferred by later authorities.

Core Idea
In a large tank, impurity's visibility determines permissibility of using the spot per Bukhara scholars; Abu Yusuf says no restriction unless water changes.

This passage delves into a nuanced disagreement within the Hanafi school about the purity of water at the precise spot where impurity falls into a large tank (10x10 cubits). While the tank is analogized to flowing water, scholars differ on whether one must avoid that spot. The commentator and later authorities weigh the arguments, presenting three distinct intra-school positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
A large tank (10x10) is ruled like flowing water: ablution is permissible despite impurity. The question is whether the spot where impurity fell becomes impure.
The passage assumes the water itself remains pure unless its properties change.
2
Understand the two types of impurity
Impurity is either visible (feces, blood) or invisible (urine, wine). This distinction is crucial for the Bukhara position.
Visibility affects whether the spot is considered contaminated.
3
State the Bukhara position
For visible impurity, avoid the spot; for invisible impurity, one can use the spot. Reason: analogy to flowing water where invisible impurity does not contaminate the spot.
This is the view chosen by the commentator (Al-Marghinani).
4
State the Iraq position
No distinction between visible and invisible: one must always use a spot away from where the impurity fell.
This view is also recorded in authoritative texts like Al-Dhakhirah.
5
Explain Abu Yusuf's view and tarjih
A large tank is exactly like flowing water: it does not become impure at all by mere fall of impurity, visible or invisible, unless the water changes. This view is preferred by Ibn al-Humam and later authorities as the strongest, based on the principle of abundance (kathrah).
Mufta bihi: one may use water from any part of the tank regardless of the impurity's location, as long as the water has not changed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: فحكمه؛ الفاء إما تفريعية: يعني لما جاز الوضوء من حوض يكون عشراً في عشر علم منه أن حكمه حكم الجاري في أنه يجوز الوضوء منه، وإن وقع فيه نجس، وإما تعليلية، والمقصود منه ذكرُ علّة الحكم الذي ذكره المصنف له بالاستثناء. [قوله: فإن كانت ... الخ؛ الظاهر أنّ الفاء للتفريع، وحينئذ يرد عليه: أنّ الماء الجاري إذا كان ما لا يلاقي النجاسة منه أكثر مما يلاقي جازَ الوضوء في أسفله، فإذا كان حكم العشر في العشر حكم الجاري، ينبغي أن يكون حكمه كذلك، لا ما ذكره. وحاصل المسألة: النجاسة الواقعة في الحوض لا تخلو: إما أن تكون مرئية: كالعذرة والدم. أو غير مرئية: كالبول ونحو ذلك. فإن كانت مرئية لا يتوضأ من موضع وقوع النجاسة، بل يتوضأ من جانب آخر. وإن كانت غير مرئية يتوضأ من ذلك الجانب أيضاً؛ لأنه بمنزلة الماء الجاري، والجواب في الجاري على هذا الوجه هو أنه يجوز الوضوء من ذلك الجانب أيضاً، إذا كانت النجاسة غير مرئية، وإن كانت مرئية يتوضأ من الجانب الآخر، وهذا قول مشايخ بخارا. وأما مشايخ العراق فقالوا: لا فرق بين المرئية وغيرها في أنه لا يتوضأ من ذلك الجانب، بل من الجانب الآخر. كذا في «الذخيرة» و«فتاوی قاضي خان» وبهذا علم أنّ ما اختاره الشارحُ هاهنا من الفرق إنما هو قول مشايخ بخارا، وكذا قال صاحب «الخلاصة»: «هل يتنجس موضع الوقوع؟ فنقول: النجاسة على نوعين: مرئية وغير مرئية. والمرئية: كالعذرة والجيفة. وغير المرئية: كقطرة خمر. ففي المرئية يتنجس موضع الوقوع بالإجماع، ويترك موضع النجاسة بقدر الحوض الصغير. وأما في غير المرئية فعند مشايخ العراق كذلك، وعند مشايخ بلخ وبخارا يجوز التوضؤ من موضع الوقوع». انتهى والذي رجحه ابن الهمام في فتح القدير وتلميذه ابن أمير حاج في «الحلبة شرح المنية» وغيرهما ما روي عن أبي يوسف أن الكثير كالعشر في العشر: كالجاري في أنه لا ينجس بوقوع النجاسةِ مطلقاً مرئية كانت أو غير مرئية إلا بالتغير، فيجوز الوضوء من جميع الجوانب فيهما؛ لأنَّ الدليل إنّما يقتضي عند الكثرة عدم التنجس إلا بالتغير من غير فصل.
His statement: 'So its ruling'; the fa (so) is either for elaboration meaning: since it is permissible to perform ablution from a tank that is ten by ten, it is known that its ruling is the ruling of flowing water in that ablution is permissible from it even if impurity falls into it. Or it is causative, and the purpose is to mention the reason for the ruling the author mentioned by exception. [His statement: 'If it is ... etc.]; apparently the fa is for elaboration, then an objection arises: regarding flowing water, if the part not contacting the impurity is more than the part contacting it, ablution is permissible in its lower part. So if the ruling of a ten by ten tank is the ruling of flowing water, its ruling should be like that, not what he mentioned. The summary of the issue: the impurity falling in the tank is either visible like feces and blood, or invisible like urine and the like. If visible, one does not perform ablution from the place of the impurity but rather from another side. If invisible, one performs ablution from that side as well, because it is like flowing water. The answer regarding flowing water in this manner is that ablution is permissible from that side as well if the impurity is invisible; if visible, one performs ablution from the other side. This is the statement of the scholars of Bukhara. As for the scholars of Iraq, they said: there is no difference between visible and invisible in that one does not perform ablution from that side, but rather from the other side. So in Al-Dhakhirah and Fatawa Qadi Khan. Hence it is known that what the commentator chose here regarding the difference is only the statement of the scholars of Bukhara. The author of Al-Khulasah also said: 'Does the place of occurrence become impure? We say: impurity is of two types: visible and invisible. Visible like feces and carcass. Invisible like a drop of wine. For visible, the place of occurrence becomes impure by consensus, and the place of impurity is left to the extent of a small tank. As for invisible, according to the scholars of Iraq it is likewise, and according to the scholars of Balkh and Bukhara, ablution is permissible from the place of occurrence.' End. What Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir and his student Ibn Amir Hajj in Al-Halbah Sharh al-Munyah and others preferred is what was narrated from Abu Yusuf that much water like a ten by ten is like flowing water in that it does not become impure by the occurrence of impurity absolutely whether visible or invisible except by change, so ablution is permissible from all sides in both cases; because the evidence only requires when there is abundance that it does not become impure except by change without distinction.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage discusses the practical ruling when impurity falls into a large tank (10x10 cubits) that is considered 'much' water. The commentator first clarifies that the tank's ruling is analogized to flowing water. Then he addresses a subtlety: in flowing water, if the part not contacting impurity is larger than the part that does, ablution is permissible even at the site of impurity? The text clarifies: in flowing water, if impurity is visible, one should avoid the exact spot; if invisible, one can use the spot. For a large tank, the same distinction applies according to the scholars of Bukhara: for visible impurity (like feces, blood), one must avoid the spot; for invisible impurity (like urine, wine), one can use the same spot. The scholars of Iraq, however, make no distinction: one must always use a side away from the impurity, regardless of visibility. The passage then quotes Al-Khulasah confirming this. Finally, it notes that later authorities like Ibn al-Humam and his student prefer the view of Abu Yusuf that a large tank is exactly like flowing water: it does not become impure at all by the mere fall of impurity (visible or invisible) unless the water's properties change (color, taste, smell). Hence, one may use any part of the tank. This view is based on the principle that abundance prevents impurity except with change. The passage thus presents three Hanafi positions: the strict Iraqi view, the moderate Bukharan view allowing use at the spot for invisible impurity, and the most lenient view of Abu Yusuf (preferred by later scholars) allowing full use.

Key Terms
مرئية (mari'ah): Impurity that is visible to the eye, such as feces, blood, or a carcass.
غير مرئية (ghayr mari'ah): Impurity that is not visible, such as urine or a drop of wine.
الجاري (al-jari): Flowing water, which in Hanafi fiqh is ruled not to become impure by simple contact with impurity unless it changes.
كثرة (kathrah): Abundance; the principle that a large quantity of water (e.g., 10x10) does not become impure by mere contact with impurity unless it changes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view of Ibn al-Humam and later authorities, how should one treat a large tank (10x10) after a drop of urine falls into it?
A) One must avoid the spot where the urine fell
B) One can use water from any part of the tank
C) Only use water from the opposite side
D) The tank becomes impure and cannot be used

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school includes multiple valid positions on impurity in large water tanks; the most relied-upon view (Abu Yusuf's) holds that abundance prevents impurity unless water changes.

Wudu from a Pool When Impurity Falls: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wudu from a pool after an impurity falls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on wudu from a pool after an impurity falls and identify the mufta bihi position according to later authorities.

Core Idea
The effect of impurity in a large pool depends on visibility, but the relied-upon view rejects this distinction entirely.

When an impurity falls into a pool, do you need to move away from that side before doing wudu? This passage presents a fascinating intra-Hanafi dispute between the scholars of Bukhara and Iraq, and ultimately the view of Abu Yusuf is preponderated, simplifying the ruling for large quantities of water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
The passage presents three views: (1) Bukhara: distinguish visible/non-visible, allow wudu from same side for non-visible; (2) Iraq: no distinction, always move to another side; (3) Abu Yusuf (mufta bihi): large pool (10x10 cubits) like flowing water, wudu from any side unless water changes.
Abu Yusuf's view overrides the other two.
2
Understand the basis of each position
Bukhara uses qiyas to flowing water for non-visible; Iraq relies on the principle that still water is easily affected; Abu Yusuf invokes the maxim that a large quantity does not become impure without change.
The tarjih favors simplicity and practical ease.
3
Note the sources cited
The passage cites al-Dhakhirah, Fatawa Qadi Khan, al-Khulasa, and later tarjih from Fath al-Qadir and al-Halba Sharh al-Munyah.
These are authoritative later Hanafi texts.
4
Apply the tarjih
The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view: a large pool (10x10 or more) is like flowing water, so wudu is permissible from all sides unless the water changes in taste/color/odor.
This resolves the dispute for most practical cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو غير مرئية : كالبول ونحو ذلك.
فإن كانت مرئية لا يتوضأ من موضع وقوع النجاسة، بل يتوضأ من جانب آخر. وإن كانت غير مرئية يتوضأ من ذلك الجانب أيضاً ؛ لأنه بمنزلة الماء الجاري، والجواب في الجاري على هذا الوجه هو أنه يجوز الوضوء من ذلك الجانب أيضاً، إذا كانت النجاسة غير مرئية، وإن كانت مرئية يتوضأ من الجانب الآخر، وهذا قول مشايخ بخارا.
وأما مشايخ العراق فقالوا : لا فرق بين المرئية وغيرها في أنه لا يتوضأ من ذلك الجانب، بل من الجانب الآخر. كذا في «الذخيرة» و«فتاوی قاضي خان» وبهذا علم أنّ ما اختاره الشارحُ هاهنا من الفرق إنما هو قول مشايخ بخارا.
وكذا قال صاحب «الخلاصة»: «هل يتنجس موضع الوقوع؟ فنقول : النجاسة على نوعين : مرئية وغير مرئية. والمرئية : كالعذرة والجيفة. وغير المرئية : كقطرة خمر. ففي المرئية يتنجس موضع الوقوع بالإجماع، ويترك موضع النجاسة بقدر الحوض الصغير. وأما في غير المرئية فعند مشايخ العراق كذلك، وعند مشايخ بلخ وبخارا يجوز التوضؤ من موضع الوقوع». انتهى والذي رجحه ابن الهمام في فتح القدير وتلميذه ابن أمير حاج في «الحلبة شرح المنية» وغيرهما ما روي عن أبي يوسف أن الكثير كالعشر في العشر : كالجاري في أنه لا ينجس بوقوع النجاسة مطلقاً مرئية كانت أو غير مرئية إلا بالتغير، فيجوز الوضوء من جميع الجوانب فيهما؛ لأنَّ الدليل إنّما يقتضي عند الكثرة عدم التنجس إلا بالتغير من غير فصل.
وإن كانت غير مرئية يُتَوَضأ من جميع الجوانب، وكذا من موضع غسالته. قوله : بل من الجانب الآخر؛ اختلف في مقدار التنحي على أقوال ذكرناها في «السعاية»، والذي صححه صاحب «البحر» تبعاً لشارح «المنية» أنه يتحرى، فإن وقع تحريه على أنّ النجاسة لم تخلص إلى هذا الموضع جاز منه الوضوء.
قوله وكذا من موضع غسالته؛ هذا حكم إجماعي كما صرح به في «الخلاصة»، فإنّه إن كان الماء المستعمل طاهراً فظاهر، ولأنه كان نجساً فكذلك؛ لكون نجاسته غير مرئية، وذكر في «الذخيرة» خلافاً فيه، وقد بينا بطلانه في «السعاية».
Or non-visible: like urine and the like. If it is visible, one does not perform wudu from the place where the impurity fell, but rather from another side. And if it is non-visible, one performs wudu from that side as well; because it is like flowing water. And the answer concerning flowing water in this manner is that it is permissible to perform wudu from that side as well, when the impurity is non-visible. And if it is visible, one performs wudu from the other side. And this is the statement of the scholars of Bukhara. And as for the scholars of Iraq, they said: There is no difference between visible and non-visible in that one does not perform wudu from that side, but rather from the other side. This is in al-Dhakhirah and Fatawa Qadi Khan. And by this it is known that what the commentator chose here of the distinction is only the statement of the scholars of Bukhara. And likewise the author of al-Khulasa said: 'Does the place of fall become impure? We say: Impurity is of two types: visible and non-visible. Visible: like stool and carcass. Non-visible: like a drop of wine. For visible, the place of fall becomes impure by consensus, and one leaves the place of impurity to the extent of a small basin. And regarding non-visible, according to the scholars of Iraq it is the same, and according to the scholars of Balkh and Bukhara, it is permissible to perform wudu from the place of fall.' End quote. And what Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir and his student Ibn Amir Hajj in al-Halba Sharh al-Munyah and others preponderated is what was narrated from Abu Yusuf: that a large quantity like a 10x10 [cubit pool] is like flowing water in that it does not become impure by the fall of impurity, absolutely, whether visible or non-visible, unless by change. So wudu is permissible from all sides in both [cases]; because the evidence only requires in the case of large quantity the absence of impurity except by change without further condition. And if it is non-visible, one performs wudu from all sides, and likewise from the place of its washing water. His statement: 'But from the other side' – there is a difference of opinion regarding the amount of moving away, on several statements which we have mentioned in al-Si'ayah. And what the author of al-Bahr corrected, following the commentator of al-Munyah, is that he should estimate (taharrī). If his estimation is that the impurity did not reach this spot, then wudu from it is permissible. His statement: 'And likewise from the place of its washing water' – this is a unanimous ruling, as stated in al-Khulasa. For if the used water is pure, it is obvious; and if it was impure, it is likewise due to the fact that its impurity is non-visible. And it was mentioned in al-Dhakhirah that there is a difference regarding it, and we have shown its invalidity in al-Si'ayah.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on performing wudu from a standing pool (or large basin) after an impurity falls into it. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute, presenting three positions. First, the scholars of Bukhara distinguish between visible and non-visible impurities. For visible impurities (like stool or a carcass), one must not use the water from the side where the impurity fell; instead, one moves to another side. For non-visible impurities (like a drop of wine or urine), they consider the water like flowing water—since the impurity is not seen, it does not affect the entire pool, so wudu is permissible from the same side. Second, the scholars of Iraq make no distinction: regardless of visibility, one must avoid the side where the impurity fell and use another side. This is recorded in al-Dhakhirah and Fatawa Qadi Khan. The commentator (author of the sharh) adopted the Bukhara view. Third, the passage then cites the view of Abu Yusuf, as preponderated by Ibn al-Humam and others, that a large pool (of ten by ten cubits or more) is equivalent to flowing water: it does not become impure by the mere fall of any impurity, visible or non-visible, unless the water changes in taste, color, or odor. Therefore, wudu is permissible from all sides without moving away. The commentary then clarifies two points: (1) The amount of moving away from a visible impurity: the correct view is that one should estimate (taharrī) based on likelihood of impurity reaching that spot. (2) The ruling on using water that has already been used for wudu (ghasalah) from the same pool: this is unanimously permissible, whether the used water is considered pure or impure, because its impurity (if any) is non-visible, similar to the case of non-visible fall.

Key Terms
مرئية (mar'iyyah): Impurity that is visible to the eye, such as solid excrement or a dead animal.
غير مرئية (ghayr mar'iyyah): Impurity that is not visible, such as a drop of wine or urine that dissolves.
مشايخ بخارا (Mashayikh Bukhārā): Hanafi scholars from Bukhara (Central Asia) who held the view of distinguishing visible and non-visible impurities.
مشايخ العراق (Mashayikh al-'Irāq): Hanafi scholars from Iraq who held that no distinction is made between visible and non-visible impurities; one must always move away from the side of the fall.
كثير (kathīr): A large quantity of water, typically defined as a pool ten by ten cubits (around 5x5 meters or 200 gallons), which is considered like flowing water in impurity rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the scholars of Bukhara and the scholars of Iraq regarding wudu from a pool when a non-visible impurity falls into it?
A) Bukhara scholars require moving to another side; Iraq scholars allow wudu from the same side.
B) Both schools agree that wudu is allowed from the same side for non-visible impurities.
C) Bukhara scholars allow wudu from the same side; Iraq scholars require moving to another side.
D) Both schools agree that wudu is not allowed from any side until the water is changed.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position is that a large pool (10x10 cubits or more) is like flowing water: it does not become impure by the mere fall of impurity unless the water changes.
