[٢]قوله : فعند ... الخ ؛ اعلم أنه رويت عن أبي حنيفة له فيه ثلاث روايات(4) : أحدها: أنه نجس مغلّظاً ، رواه الحسن بن زياد ه ، وأخذ به، وسنده هو القياس على كون مال الصدقة مطهراً محرَّماً على بني هاشم مع الأحاديث الدالة على خروج الخطايا بالماء. وثانيها: إنه نجس مخفّفاً ، رواه أبو يوسف له وأخذ به، فإنّ للبلوى تأثيراً في تخفيف النجاسة. وثالثها: إنّه طاهر ، رواه محمد الله ، وأخذ به، وعليه الفتوى ، كما في «البناية»، وهو الأقوى من حيث الدليل، كما بسطناه في «السعاية»). ومن لعائف الأقوال ما في «الميزان» لعبد الوهاب الشعراني له قال : «سمعت سيدي الخواص الله قال : مدارك الإمام أبي حنيفة له دقيقة، لا يطلع عليها إلا أهل الكف من أكابر الأولياء، وكان أبو حنيفة الله إذ رأى ماء الميضأة يعرف سائر الذنوب التي فيه، فلذا جعلَ ماءَ الطهارة إذا تطهر به المكلّف له ثلاثة أحوال : أحدها : إنه كالنجاسةِ المغلّظة ؛ لاحتمال أن يكون المكلف ارتكب كبيرة. والثاني : إنّه كالنجاسةِ المتوسطة ؛ لاحتمال أن يكون ارتكب صغيرة. والثالث : إنّه طاهر في نفسه غير مطهّر لغيره ؛ لاحتمال أن يكون ارتكب مكروهاً، أو خلاف الأولى». استدلال [١] قوله : ونحن نقول ... الخ ؛ اختلفت النسخ . هاهنا، ففي بعضها هكذا : ونحن نقول : لو كان طاهراً لجاز في السفر الوضوء به ثمّ الشرب منه ... الخ، وفي بعضها : ونحن نقول : لو كان طاهرا مطهرا الجاز... الخ، فعلي النسخة الأولى هوا على نجاسة الماء المستعمل نصرة لمذهب أبي حنيفة الله ورداً على من قال بالطهارة. وحاصله : أنه لو كان الماء المستعمل طاهراً في نفسه لجاز في السفر أن يتوضأ بالماء، ثم يشرب غُسالته مع أنه لم يقل به أحد – أي لم يقــل بجواز الـشـرب مـن المستعمل، أو لم يقل بالوضوء ثم الشرب منه أحد – بـل جـوزوا التيمم عند خوف العطش، ومن المعلوم أن الحرمة لا للكرامة كما في الإنسان آية النجاسة، فعلم أنه نجس. ولعلك تتفطّن من هذا البيان أن ضمير «كان» راجع إلى الماء المستعمل، وضمير «به» راجع إلى نفس الماء قبل استعماله، وضمير: «منه» ( «منه» راجع إلى الماء بعد استعماله ، والإشارة بذلك إلى جواز الشرب أو التوضؤ ثم الشرب. (١) ينظر : مرشد أقرب المسالك ) (ص) (۳)، والمرشد المعين وشرحه مختصر الدر الثمين المورد المعين»(ص۲۳)، (مختصر خليل» (ص) (٤) ، و(حاشية الدسوقي (١) : (٤٢)، والتاج والإكليل» (١ : ٦٦) ، و((الفواكه الدواني (۱) : ۱۲۵ ، ولكنهم قالوا : كره ماء مستعمل في حدث. (۲) قال الشربيني في مغني المحتاج) (۱) : (۲۰ أن مذهب الشافعي القديم هو أن الماء طهور. (۳) «الهداية» (۱ : ۹۰). The third difference: regarding its ruling. According to Abu Hanifa: it is ritually impure with severe impurity. According to Abu Yusuf: it is ritually impure with light impurity. According to Muhammad: it is pure but not purifying. (1) According to Malik and al-Shafi'i in his old position: it is pure and purifying. And the second [from al-Shafi'i]: it becomes used (musta'mal) merely by leaving the limb in wudu, and from the entire body in ghusl, and this is what is chosen in 'al-Hidayah'. [1] The commenter says: 'He said: regarding its ruling; it would be better to say: regarding its attribute.' [2] The commenter says: 'He said: According to... etc.; know that three transmissions are narrated from Abu Hanifa: one: it is severely impure, narrated by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, and he adopted it, and its basis is qiyas upon the fact that charity wealth is purifying and forbidden to Banu Hashim along with the hadiths indicating the exit of sins with water. Second: it is lightly impure, narrated by Abu Yusuf, and he adopted it; for the difficulty of the situation has an effect in lightening the impurity. Third: it is pure, narrated by Muhammad, and he adopted it, and upon it is the fatwa, as in 'al-Binaya', and it is strongest in terms of evidence, as we explained in 'al-Si'aya'. And among the subtle sayings is what is in 'al-Mizan' of 'Abd al-Wahhab al-Sha'rani who said: 'I heard my master al-Khawwas say: The reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa is subtle, not understood except by people of sufficiency from the great saints. Abu Hanifa, when he saw the water of the wudu basin, knew all the sins contained in it. Hence he gave three states for the water of purity when the obligated one performs purification with it: one: it is like severe impurity; because the obligated person may have committed a major sin. Second: it is like intermediate impurity; because he may have committed a minor sin. Third: it is pure in itself but not purifying for other than itself; because he may have committed a disliked act or what is contrary to the better.' Evidence: [1] The commenter says: 'He said: and we say... etc.; the manuscripts differ here. In some it is: And we say: if it were pure, it would be permissible in travel to perform wudu with it and then drink from it... etc. In some: And we say: if it were pure and purifying, it would be permissible... etc. According to the first version, it is a support for the impurity of used water, a vindication of Abu Hanifa's school, and a refutation of those who say it is pure. Its conclusion: if used water were pure in itself, it would be permissible in travel to perform wudu with the water and then drink its wash water, but no one has said that – i.e., no one said the permissibility of drinking from used water, or no one said wudu then drinking from it – rather they permitted tayammum when fearing thirst, and it is known that prohibition is not for honor as it is in the human being a sign of impurity, so it is known that it is impure. And perhaps you will notice from this explanation that the pronoun of “kāna” refers to the used water, the pronoun of “bihi” refers to the water itself before its use, and the pronoun of “minhu” refers to the water after its use, and the indication by that is to the permissibility of drinking or performing wudu then drinking.' (1) See: ... (2) al-Shirbini said... (3) al-Hidayah... (4) al-Qari said...
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage addresses the third point of difference concerning the legal status of water that has been used for ritual purification (الماء المستعمل). The base text first states the positions: Abu Hanifa considers it severely impure (najis ghaliz), Abu Yusuf lightly impure (najis khafif), Muhammad al-Shaybani pure but not purifying (tahir ghayr tahur). The commentator then elaborates on three transmissions from Abu Hanifa: one from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (severely impure), one from Abu Yusuf (lightly impure), and one from Muhammad (pure). He notes that the fatwa is on Muhammad's position, which is also the strongest in evidence. He then relates a spiritual insight from al-Sha'rani suggesting that Abu Hanifa's three positions correspond to the possible sin levels of the user. Finally, the commentator discusses a variant reading of a proof: if used water were pure, one could do wudu with it in travel and drink it, but since no one says that, it indicates impurity. However, the mufta bihi is that it is tahir ghayr tahur, and the argument is to support Abu Hanifa's view in a specific transmission. The overall lesson is a detailed exposition of the intra- and inter-madhhab disagreement on used water.

Key Terms
الماء المستعمل (al-māʾ al-mustaʿmal): Water that has been used for a valid act of purification (wudu or ghusl), having left the limb without change in its properties.
نجاسة غليظة (najāsah ghalīẓah): Severe ritual impurity requiring washing three times, with rubbing for tangible impurities.
طاهر غير طهور (ṭāhir ghayr ṭahūr): Pure in itself but not legally purifying for others (i.e., cannot be used for wudu or ghusl).
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides strict qiyas for ease or public interest; the basis for Muhammad's view that used water is not impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon (muftā bihī) Hanafi position regarding water used for wuḍūʾ?
A) It is severely impure (najis ghalīẓ).
B) It is pure and purifying (ṭāhir muṭahhir).
C) It is pure but not purifying (ṭāhir ghayr ṭahūr).
D) It is lightly impure (najis khafīf).

Key Takeaway
Used water is ṭāhir ghayr ṭahūr in the Hanafi muftā bihī: pure in itself but not legally purifying for further ritual use.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Dispute on Used Water: Purity and Purification
Inter-madhhab dispute with critique of the Hanafi argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi, intra-Hanafi, and Shafi'i positions on used water, reproduce the Hanafi reductio argument, and explain the critique of that argument based on the hadith of al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid.

Core Idea
Used water is either impure, pure but not purifying, or pure and purifying — the disagreement turns on whether it can be drunk.

This passage examines a classic debate about water used in purification. The Hanafi school holds a unique position that distinguishes purity (taharah) from purificatory power (tahuriyyah). However, the author also critiques the standard Hanafi proof, exposing a weakness that you must understand to avoid oversimplifying the ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three views: impure (Abu Hanifa), pure but not purifying (Muhammad), pure and purifying (Shafi'i/Maliki). Note the mufta bihi is Muhammad's view.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that used water is pure (tahir) but not purifying (ghayr mutahhir).
2
Follow the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: if used water were pure, in travel one could perform wudu with it and then drink the runoff; but no scholar permits that (they permit tayammum instead). Hence the water must be impure.
The proof assumes that purity implies permissibility for both wudu and drinking.
3
Examine the two manuscript versions
The base text appears in two versions: one argues for impurity (supporting Abu Hanifa), the other argues against both purity and purification (supporting Muhammad). This shows internal Hanafi diversity.
The second version refutes Malik's view by denying both tahir and tahur.
4
Analyze the critique of the proof
The commentator points out that the claim 'no one permits drinking used water' is false; Shafi'is permit it, citing the hadith of al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid who drank the Prophet's wudu water. This undermines the reductio.
The Hanafi response is that the hadith is specific to the Prophet's baraka, not a general ruling.
5
Assess the tarjih and thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if impure, it cannot be used for wudu; if pure but not purifying, it can be drunk but not used for wudu; if pure and purifying, it is fully valid. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the middle view.
The tarjih is based on evidence, as detailed in al-Binaya and al-Si'aya.

Source Text

ونحن نقول : لو كان طاهراً لجاز في السفر الوضوء به ، ثمَّ الشُّربُ منه : أي لا يجوز الوضوء بالماء المستعمل، ولا الشرب، ولم يقل أحد بذلك. وثالثها : إنّ [؟] طاهر ، رواه محمد الله ، وأخذ به، وعليه الفتوى ، كما في «البناية»، ومن وهو الأقوى من حيث الدليل، كما بسطناه في «السعاية»). [لطائف؟] الأقوال ما في «الميزان» لعبد الوهاب الشعراني له قال : «سمعت عد الخواص الله قال : مدارك الإمام أبي حنيفة له دقيقة، لا يطلع عليها إلا أهل الكف من أكابر الأولياء، وكان أبو حنيفة الله إذ رأى ماء الله ورداً على من قال بالطهارة. وحاصله : أنه لو كان الماء المستعمل طاهراً في نفسه لجاز في السفر أن يتوضأ بالماء، ثم يشرب غُسالته مع أنه لم يقل به أحد – أي لم يقــل بجواز الـشـرب مـن المستعمل، أو لم يقل بالوضوء ثم الشرب منه أحد – بـل جـوزوا التيمم عند خوف العطش، ومن المعلوم أن الحرمة لا للكرامة كما في الإنسان آية النجاسة، فعلم أنه نجس. ولعلك تتفطّن من هذا البيان أن ضمير «كان» راجع إلى الماء المستعمل، وضمير «به» راجع إلى نفس الماء قبل استعماله، وضمير: «منه» ( «منه» راجع إلى الماء بعد استعماله ، والإشارة بذلك إلى جواز الشرب أو التوضؤ ثم الشرب. فإن قلت : : يلزم حينئذ تفكيك الضمائر ؟ قلت : لا بأس به، فإنّ ذات الماء المطلق والمستعمل واحدة، والاختلاف باعتبار وصف، ولا حرج في أن يرجع ضمير إلى ذات شيء مع قطع النظر عن الوصف، وآخر إليه، مع لحاظ الوصف. وعلى النسخة الثانية : ١. يحتمل أن يكون المطهر بصيغة اسم المفعول. . ويحتمل أن يكون اسم فاعل مفيدا لمعنى الطهور. فعلى الأول يكون تأكيداً للطاهر، ويكون ماله هو مآل النسخة الأولى، وعلي الثاني يكون الغرض من هذا الكلام الردّ على من قال بالطهارة والطهورية جميعاً، نصرة لمذهب محمد ، ورداً على مالك ومن وافقه، ويرجع كل ضمير إلى المستعمل من حيث أنه مستعمل. وحاصله : أنه لو كان المستعمل طاهراً أو طهوراً لجاز الوضوء بالمستعمل ، والشرب منه عند العطش، وليس كذلك ولا يخفى على المتفطن ما في هذا الكلام على كلتا النسختين من الاختلال والإعضال : أما أولاً : فلان الحكم بعدم قول أحدٍ بجواز الوضوء بالماء المستعمل خطأ، فإن من قال بطهوريته قد ذهب إليه، وكذا الحكم بعدم قول أحد جواز الشرب من الغُسالة خطأ ، فإنّ كلّ من قال بالطهارة قال به. وأثبت ذلك بحديث السائب بن يزيد الله المروي في «صحيح البخاري» وغيره ، - قال : ذهبت بي خالتي إلى النبي الله فقالت : إنّ ابن أختي وقع – أي وجع في قدميه فمسح رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم برأسي ودعا لي بالبركة، ثم توضاً، فشربت من وضوئه (۱). قال القسطلاني(٢) : «أي المتقاطر من أعضائه الشريفة».
And we say: If it were pure, then in travel it would be permissible to perform ablution with it, and then drink from it; meaning: ablution with used water is not permissible, nor drinking, and no one has said that. And the third opinion: that it is pure, related by Muhammad, and he acted upon it, and the fatwa is upon it, as in al-Binaya, and it is the strongest in terms of evidence, as we have detailed in al-Si'aya. Among the subtle opinions is what is in al-Mizan by Abd al-Wahhab al-Sharani who said: I heard from the elite of the righteous that they said: The insights of Imam Abu Hanifa are subtle, only known to the proficient among the great saints. Abu Hanifa, when he saw the water of the ablution basin, would know all the sins present in it, so he considered water of purification, when a legally responsible person purifies with it, to have three states: first, it is like severe impurity, because the person may have committed a major sin; second, it is like moderate impurity, because they may have committed a minor sin; third, it is pure in itself but not purifying for others, because they may have committed a disliked act or something contrary to what is better. Evidence [1] His statement: 'And we say...' etc. The manuscripts differ here. In some it is: 'And we say: If it were pure, then in travel it would be permissible to perform ablution with it and then drink from it...' etc. In others: 'And we say: If it were pure and purifying...' etc. According to the first version, it supports the impurity of used water, advocating the school of Abu Hanifa and refuting those who say it is pure. The summary: if used water were pure in itself, then in travel it would be allowed to perform ablution with the water, then drink its wash water, while no one has said that – meaning no one has said it is permissible to drink from used water, or no one has said to perform ablution then drink from it – rather they allowed tayammum when fearing thirst. And it is known that prohibition is not for honor as in the verse about impurity of humans. So it is known that it is impure. Perhaps you will realize from this explanation that the pronoun in 'كان' refers to used water, the pronoun in 'به' refers to the water before use, and the pronoun 'منه' refers to the water after use, and the reference to that is the permissibility of drinking or ablution then drinking. If you say: this necessitates distinguishing the pronouns? I say: there is no harm in that, for the essence of absolute water and used water is one, the difference is by attribute, and there is no difficulty in one pronoun referring to the essence of something without regard to the attribute, and another to it with regard to the attribute. As for the second version: 1. It is possible that 'al-mutahhir' is in the passive participle form, 2. Or it is an active participle conveying the meaning of 'tahur'. In the first case, it is an emphasis of 'pure', and its outcome is the same as the first version. In the second, the purpose of this statement is to refute those who say it is both pure and purifying, advocating the school of Muhammad, and refuting Malik and those who agree with him. And every pronoun returns to used water in that it is used. The summary: if used water were pure or purifying, then it would be allowed to perform ablution with used water and drink it when thirsty, but it is not so. It is not hidden from the discerning person what is in this statement, in both versions, of confusion and difficulty: firstly, the ruling that no one says it is permissible to perform ablution with used water is a mistake, for those who say it is purifying hold that view. Similarly, the ruling that no one says it is permissible to drink from the wash water is a mistake, for everyone who says it is pure says so. And this is proven by the hadith of al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid in Sahih al-Bukhari and others, who said: My maternal aunt took me to the Prophet and said: 'My sister's son has pain' – meaning pain in his feet – so the Messenger of Allah wiped my head and prayed for blessing for me, then he performed ablution, and I drank from his ablution water. Al-Qastalani said: 'meaning the dripping from his noble limbs.'
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal status of used water (al-ma' al-musta'mal). The author presents the Hanafi argument that used water is impure, not merely non-purifying. The proof is by way of reductio ad absurdum: if used water were pure, then in travel one could perform wudu with it and then drink the runoff; but no scholar permits that. Instead, they allow tayammum when water is scarce, indicating the water is impure. The author then discusses two manuscript versions of the base text. The first version argues for impurity, supporting Abu Hanifa's view. The second version, where the word 'mutahhir' appears, either emphasizes purity or means 'purifying'. On this second reading, the argument refutes those (like Malik) who consider used water both pure and purifying, supporting Imam Muhammad's position. The commentary then critiques the argument: the claim that no one permits drinking used water or performing wudu with it is false. Those who consider it pure (like the Shafi'is) do allow drinking it, citing the hadith of al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid who drank from the Prophet's wudu water. This hadith, recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari, shows that the Prophet's used water was drunk, implying it is pure. The author thus exposes the weakness in the Hanafi argument as presented, though the Hanafi school ultimately maintains its position based on other evidence.

Key Terms
الماء المستعمل (al-ma' al-musta'mal): Water that has been used in a valid act of purification (wudu/ghusl) and has already been used to wash a limb.
طاهر (tahir): Pure in essence, not carrying ritual impurity, but not necessarily purifying for others.
طهور (tahur): Pure and purifying, meaning it can be used for wudu or ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main evidence used by those who say used water is pure?
A) A Quranic verse on water being pure
B) The hadith of al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid drinking the Prophet's wudu water
C) Consensus of the Companions that used water is pure
D) Analogy between used water and pure water

Key Takeaway
Used water is pure but not purifying in the relied-upon Hanafi view, though this remains a point of legitimate scholarly disagreement.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
15 min
The Hanafi Position on Used Water: Pure but Not Purifying
Critique of an argument that used water is impure or not purifying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on used water (tahir but not tahur), cite the hadith of al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid as evidence, and critique the opposing Maliki position using the five arguments from the text.

Core Idea
Used water is pure to drink but not purifying for wudu.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute over the legal status of water that has been used for wudu or ghusl. The Hanafi school, following Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, holds that such water is tahir (pure) but not tahur (purifying). This means it cannot be used to perform wudu, but it is permissible to drink. The passage we study refutes the view of Imam Malik, who considers used water tahur. We will analyze the Hanafi evidence and the logical critiques presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute
Identify the two positions: Hanafi (tahir only) vs. Maliki (tahur). Recognize the practical consequence: if used water is tahur, it can be used for wudu; if only tahir, it cannot.
The Hanafi position relies on the distinction between tahara (purity) and tahuriyya (purifying capacity).
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
Read the hadith of al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid in Sahih al-Bukhari: the Prophet (ﷺ) performed wudu and the companion drank the dripping water. This proves the water is pure (tahir) but, as Hanafis argue, does not prove it is purifying.
The hadith is used to counter any claim that used water is impure (najis).
3
Analyze the five critiques
Work through each of the five points the author makes to refute the opposing argument. Focus on how each critique exposes logical flaws.
The critiques address factual inaccuracies, alternative reasons for prohibition, the tayammum analogy, the distinction between tahir and tahur, and the irrelevance of travel restriction.
4
Recognize the practical consequence
Understand that in daily fiqh, used water is permissible to drink but invalid for wudu. This affects rulings on using leftover wudu water for purification.
The mufta bihi position is that used water is tahir but not tahur, following Imam Muhammad.

Source Text

إليه، مع لحاظ الوصف.
وعلى النسخة الثانية :
١. يحتمل أن يكون المطهر بصيغة اسم المفعول. . ويحتمل أن يكون اسم فاعل مفيدا لمعنى الطهور.
فعلى الأول يكون تأكيداً للطاهر، ويكون ماله هو مآل النسخة الأولى، وعلي الثاني يكون الغرض من هذا الكلام الردّ على من قال بالطهارة والطهورية جميعاً، نصرة لمذهب محمد ، ورداً على مالك ومن وافقه، ويرجع كل ضمير إلى المستعمل من حيث أنه مستعمل.
وحاصله : أنه لو كان المستعمل طاهراً أو طهوراً لجاز الوضوء بالمستعمل ، والشرب منه عند العطش، وليس كذلك ولا يخفى على المتفطن ما في هذا الكلام على كلتا النسختين من الاختلال والإعضال :
أما أولاً : فلان الحكم بعدم قول أحدٍ بجواز الوضوء بالماء المستعمل خطأ، فإن من قال بطهوريته قد ذهب إليه، وكذا الحكم بعدم قول أحد جواز الشرب من الغُسالة خطأ ، فإنّ كلّ من قال بالطهارة قال به.
وأثبت ذلك بحديث السائب بن يزيد الله المروي في «صحيح البخاري» وغيره ،

قال : ذهبت بي خالتي إلى النبي الله فقالت : إنّ ابن أختي وقع – أي وجع في قدميه فمسح رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم برأسي ودعا لي بالبركة، ثم توضاً، فشربت من وضوئه (۱). قال القسطلاني(٢) : «أي المتقاطر من أعضائه الشريفة».
(۱) في «صحيح البخاري» (۱) (۸۱) ، وفيه: «إن ابن اختي وجع فمسح رأسي..... (۲) في إرشاد الساري بشرح البخاري (۱ : ۲۷۱).
كتاب الطهارة/ أقسام المياه
وأما ثانياً : لو سُلّمَ عدم قول أحد بجواز الشرب به فنقول : لا يدلّ ذلك على
النجاسة، فإن عدم جواز شرب شيء وأكله قد يكون للمضرة والخباثة. وأيضاً : يجوز أن يكون ذلك لقوّة الشبهة في طهارته ؛ للاختلاف الواقع من
الأئمة.
وأيضاً : يجوز أن يكون منعهم من ذلك على طريق الأولوية بقصد النظافة، لا على طريق الحرمة.
وأما ثالثاً ؛ فلأنهم حكموا بجواز التيمم لخوف عطش حيوان محترم كدابة الرجل مع أن شرب الغسالة ليس محرَّماً على الدَّابة، فعلِمَ أنه ليس ذلك إلا دفعاً للحرج لا
للنجاسة.
.
وأما رابعاً : فلان عدم جواز التوضؤ به ثمّ الشرب منه إنما هو لعدم الطهورية ، وهذا لا يدل على عدم الطهارة، فالملازمة التي ذكرها بقوله : «لو كان طاهرا ...» الخ على النسخة الأولى باطلة.
وأما خامساً : فلأن التقييد بقوله : «في السفر» لغو ؛ فإنّ الحكم في الحضر والسفر
سواء، إلا أن يقال: إنّه اتفاقي بناءً على أنّ الاحتياج في السفر أشدّ من غيره، وفي المقام أبحاث أخر، أيضاً مذكورة في «السعاية»(۱).
If you say: Then it would necessitate splitting the pronouns? I say: There is no harm in it, for the substance of absolute water and used water is one, and the difference is by way of a quality, and there is no difficulty in one pronoun referring to the substance of something without consideration of the quality, and another pronoun referring to it with consideration of the quality. And according to the second version: 1. It is possible that 'al-mutahhir' is in the form of the passive participle, and it is possible that it is an active participle meaning tahur. On the first, it is an emphasis on tahir, and what it entails is the outcome of the first version. On the second, the purpose of this speech is to refute those who say both purification and purifyingness, in support of the school of Muhammad, and as a refutation of Malik and those who agree with him, and every pronoun refers to used water insofar as it is used. The summary: If used water were tahir or tahur, then it would be permissible to perform wudu with used water and to drink from it when thirsty, but it is not so, and it is not hidden from the perceptive person what this speech contains of disorder and difficulty on both versions: First: The ruling that no one says it is permissible to perform wudu with used water is false, for those who say it is tahur have gone to that. Likewise, the ruling that no one says it is permissible to drink from ghusalah (wash-water) is false, for everyone who says it is tahir has said so. And he proves that with the hadith of al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid narrated in Sahih al-Bukhari and others, he said: My maternal aunt took me to the Prophet (ﷺ) and said: My nephew is in pain – meaning pain in his feet – so the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) wiped my head and prayed for blessing for me, then he performed wudu, and I drank from his wudu water. (1) Al-Qastallani said: 'i.e., the dripping water from his noble limbs.' (1) In Sahih al-Bukhari (1)(81), and in it: 'My nephew is in pain, he wiped my head...' (2) In Irshad al-Sari fi Sharh al-Bukhari (1:271). Second: If it is accepted that no one says it is permissible to drink from it, we say: That does not indicate impurity, for the impermissibility of drinking or eating something may be due to harm and filth. Also, it is possible that it is due to the strength of doubt about its purity because of the disagreement among the imams. Also, it is possible that their prohibition of that is by way of preference with the intention of cleanliness, not by way of prohibition. Third: Because they ruled the permissibility of tayammum for fear of thirst of a respected animal like a man's riding beast, while drinking ghusalah is not forbidden for the beast, so it is known that that is only to remove hardship, not for impurity. Fourth: The impermissibility of performing wudu with it and then drinking from it is only due to lack of tahuriyya, and this does not indicate lack of tahara. So the implication that he mentioned in his statement 'If it were tahir...' etc. according to the first version is false. Fifth: The restriction by his statement 'in travel' is meaningless, for the ruling in residence and travel is the same, unless it is said that it is coincidental based on the fact that need is greater in travel. And in this context there are other investigations also mentioned in 'al-Si'ayah' (1).
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal status of used water (al-ma' al-musta'mal). The author critiques an argument that used water is neither pure (tahir) nor purifying (tahur), and in doing so defends the position of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani against Imam Malik and those who agree with him. The discussion begins by addressing two textual versions of a statement about used water. In the first version, the text apparently denies that anyone permits wudu with used water or drinking it. The author points out that this is false: those who consider used water tahur permit wudu with it, and those who consider it tahir permit drinking it. The author supports the purity of used water by citing the hadith of al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid, in which the Prophet (ﷺ) performed wudu and then the companion drank the dripping water from his limbs. This hadith, recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari, demonstrates that used water is pure enough to drink. However, the Hanafi position distinguishes between tahara (purity) and tahuriyya (purifying quality). Used water is tahir (pure) but not tahur (purifying), so it cannot be used for wudu (which requires tahur water), but it is permissible to drink. The author then presents five critiques (first to fifth) to further refute the opposing argument. These critiques highlight logical flaws: (1) The claim that no one permits wudu/drinking is factually wrong. (2) Even if no one permitted drinking, that does not prove impurity, as prohibition may be for other reasons. (3) The permissibility of tayammum for an animal's thirst shows that drinking used water is not inherently problematic. (4) The lack of tahuriyya does not imply lack of tahara. (5) The restriction to travel is meaningless as the ruling is the same in residence. The passage thus firmly establishes that used water is pure but not purifying, a key Hanafi doctrine.

Key Terms
الماء المستعمل (al-ma' al-musta'mal): Water that has been used for ablution (wudu) or ritual bath (ghusl), having touched a pure body part with the intention of worship.
طاهر (tahir): Pure in itself, meaning it is free from ritual impurity (najasa) and is not najis, but it does not have the capacity to remove ritual impurity (hadath).
طهور (tahur): Having the quality of purifying, i.e., water that can be used to remove ritual impurity (hadath) and remove najasa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in the passage, what is the ruling of using water that has been used for wudu (al-ma' al-musta'mal)?
A) It is najis and cannot be used for any purpose.
B) It is tahir (pure) but not tahur (purifying), so it can be drunk but not used for wudu.
C) It is tahur (purifying) and can be used for wudu.
D) It is pure only when needed, but otherwise disliked.

Key Takeaway
Used water is pure (tahir) but not purifying (tahur) in the Hanafi school; it can be drunk but not used for wudu.

Five Rational Arguments: Prohibition of Drinking Does Not Imply Impurity
Five rational arguments rebutting the claim that undrinkable water is impure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between prohibition of drinking and ritual impurity, and apply these rational arguments to similar cases.

Core Idea
Prohibition of drinking does not prove impurity; harm, doubt, or hygiene may be the reason.

This passage continues a series of rational arguments defending the Hanafi position that used washing water (ghusalah) is pure (tahir) even if not drinkable. The author presents five distinct rebuttals to the claim that if water cannot be drunk, it must be impure. These arguments rely on common sense and legal reasoning, not direct textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the underlying Hanafi ruling: used washing water is pure (tahir) but lacks purifying power (tahuriyyah). It is not najis.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that if water is not drinkable, it must be impure (najis). This is the logical implication being rebutted.
3
Analyze arguments 1-2: alternative reasons
The first two arguments show that prohibition of drinking may be due to harm/foulness or due to scholarly doubt, not impurity.
4
Analyze arguments 3-5: rational counterexamples
The third argument uses tayammum for animals to show the reason is hardship, not impurity; the fourth stresses that lack of tahuriyyah does not imply lack of taharah; the fifth shows the travel restriction is meaningless.
5
Synthesize the legal principle
Impurity requires clear evidence (najis), not merely prohibition of drinking. The water's inherent quality (tahuriyyah) is distinct from its purity (taharah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ثانياً : لو سُلّمَ عدم قول أحد بجواز الشرب به فنقول : لا يدلّ ذلك على النجاسة، فإن عدم جواز شرب شيء وأكله قد يكون للمضرة والخباثة. وأيضاً : يجوز أن يكون ذلك لقوّة الشبهة في طهارته ؛ للاختلاف الواقع من الأئمة. وأيضاً : يجوز أن يكون منعهم من ذلك على طريق الأولوية بقصد النظافة، لا على طريق الحرمة. وأما ثالثاً ؛ فلأنهم حكموا بجواز التيمم لخوف عطش حيوان محترم كدابة الرجل مع أن شرب الغسالة ليس محرَّماً على الدَّابة، فعلِمَ أنه ليس ذلك إلا دفعاً للحرج لا للنجاسة. وأما رابعاً : فلان عدم جواز التوضؤ به ثمّ الشرب منه إنما هو لعدم الطهورية ، وهذا لا يدل على عدم الطهارة، فالملازمة التي ذكرها بقوله : «لو كان طاهرا ...» الخ على النسخة الأولى باطلة. وأما خامساً : فلأن التقييد بقوله : «في السفر» لغو ؛ فإنّ الحكم في الحضر والسفر سواء، إلا أن يقال: إنّه اتفاقي بناءً على أنّ الاحتياج في السفر أشدّ من غيره، وفي المقام أبحاث أخر، أيضاً مذكورة في «السعاية»(۱).
And as for the second: If it is granted that no one says it is permissible to drink it, we say: That does not indicate impurity, for the impermissibility of drinking or eating something may be due to harm and foulness. Also, it may be due to the strength of doubt about its purity because of the disagreement among the imams. Also, their prohibition of it may be as a matter of priority with the intention of cleanliness, not as a matter of prohibition. And as for the third: Because they ruled on the permissibility of tayammum out of fear of thirst of a respected animal like a person's mount, even though drinking the washing water is not forbidden for the animal. So it is known that this is only to remove hardship, not because of impurity. And as for the fourth: Because the impermissibility of performing wudu with it and then drinking from it is only due to its lack of purifying capability, and this does not indicate lack of purity. So the implication he mentioned in his words 'If it were pure...' etc. according to the first version is invalid. And as for the fifth: Because the restriction in his words 'in travel' is superfluous; for the ruling in residence and travel is the same, unless it is said that it is accidental based on the fact that the need in travel is greater than elsewhere. And there are other discussions on this matter, also mentioned in 'al-Sa'ayah'.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is part of a larger inter-madhhab dispute defending the position that used washing water (ghusalah) is pure (tahir) even if not permissible for drinking. The author presents five rational arguments (the second to fifth in a series) to refute the claim that if water cannot be drunk, it must be impure. First argument: impermissibility of drinking may be due to harm, not impurity. Second: it may be due to caution or preference among scholars. Third: the allowance of tayammum for animals shows the reason is hardship, not impurity. Fourth: non-validity for wudu indicates lack of purifying power (tahuriyyah), not impurity. Fifth: the mention of travel in the opposing view is irrelevant as the rule applies equally in residence. This is typical Hanafi rational argumentation.

Key Terms
النجاسة (al-najāsah): Ritual impurity that prevents validity of prayer and requires washing
الطهورية (al-ṭahūriyyah): Quality of a substance that allows it to be used for purification (wudu/ghusl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, why can't wudu be performed with the water in question?
A) Because it is impure (najis)
B) Because it lacks purifying power (tahuriyyah)
C) Because it is harmful
D) Because it is disliked (makruh)

Key Takeaway
Prohibition of drinking does not prove impurity; the Hanafi school distinguishes between purity (taharah) and purifying quality (tahuriyyah).

Tanning Purifies Hides: The Ruling and Its Exceptions
Ruling with proof and exception based on essential impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tanning hides and explain the scriptural proof for the exceptions of pig and human.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies every hide except those that are impure in essence: pig and human.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses a practical matter for daily worship: the purity of tanned animal hides. Understanding which skins become pure and which remain impure affects the validity of water containers used for wudu and ghusl. The ruling is grounded in a well-known hadith and hinges on the concept of essential impurity (najis al-ayn).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its scope
The text states: 'Every hide that is tanned has become pure' – this is a general ruling covering all animal hides, whether from animals whose meat is eaten or not, as indicated by the word 'every'.
This generality is supported by the hadith.
2
Identify the evidence and its legal implication
The hadith 'Any hide that is tanned has surely become pure' is cited. The word 'has become pure' (قد طهر) with the particle fa indicates a conditional relationship: tanning causes purification.
The hadith is narrated by multiple sources, giving it strength (zanni al-thubut, but widely accepted).
3
Recognize the exceptions and their reasoning
Pig and human are excluded. The reason given is that they are impure in essence (najis al-ayn); tanning only removes accidental impurities, not essential ones.
The order 'pig then human' is for degradation – the more lowly is mentioned first.
4
Understand the implied ruling about dog skin
By limiting the exception to pig and human, the text implies that the skin of a dog becomes pure through tanning, because the dog is not considered najis al-ayn in the Hanafi school.
This is the preferred view in Al-Hidayah, Ghayat al-Bayan, and Al-Inayah.
5
Grasp the concept of essential vs accidental impurity
Essential impurity (najis al-ayn) cannot be removed by any process, while accidental impurity (najis ‘aridi) can be removed by tanning or washing. Pig and human skin carry essential impurity; all other animal skins carry only accidental impurity.
This distinction is key to the Hanafi position on dog skin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل إهاب دبغ فقد طهر إلا جلد الخنزير والآدمي
[١] قوله : وكل إهاب ... الخ ؛ ذكر هذه المسألة في هذا المقام مع كونها من مسائل باب تطهير الأنجاس ؛ لكونها مناسبة لبحث الوضوء والغُسل ، ففيه إعلام أن الإهاب المدبوغ طاهر، يجوز الوضوء والغُسل من الماء الموضوع فيه. وفي إيراد : «كل» تنبيه على أن الحكم عام في كلّ جلد، سواء كان جلد مأكول اللحم أو غيره ؛ لحديث: «أيما إهاب دبغ فقط طهر» (١) ، أخرجه الترمذي وحسنه ، وابن ماجة ومسلم وأبو داود وغيرهم وفي الباب أخبار كثيرة ذكرناها مع اختلافِ المذاهب في «السعاية»). [٢] قوله : فقد طهر ؛ إدخال الفاءِ على الخبر لتضمن المبتدأ معنى الشرط. [٣]قوله : إلا جلد الخنزير ... الخ، في قصر الاستثناء عليهما إشارة إلى طهارة جلد الكلب أيضاً بالدباغة، بناءً على أنه ليس بنجس العين ، كما اختاره في «الهداية» (۳)، و«غاية البيان»، و«العناية»(4). والوجه في تقديم الخنزير على الآدمي ذكراً أن الموضع موضع إهانة، وفي مثله التعظيم في التأخير. والسبب لعدم طهارة جلد الخنزير بالدباغة أنه نجس العين بجميع أجزائه، فلا تزيل الدباغة نجاسته العينية ؛ فإنّ الدباغة إنّما تزيلُ النجاسة العارضة باختلاط الرطوبات النجسة.
And every hide that is tanned has become pure, except the skin of the pig and the human. [1] His statement: 'And every hide...' He mentioned this issue in this context even though it belongs to the chapter on removing impurities, because it is relevant to the discussion of wudu and ghusl; thus it teaches that a tanned hide is pure, and it is permissible to perform wudu and ghusl from water placed in it. In the wording 'every' there is an indication that the ruling is general for every skin, whether from an animal whose meat is eaten or otherwise, due to the hadith: 'Any hide that is tanned has surely become pure' narrated by Tirmidhi who deemed it good, and by Ibn Majah, Muslim, Abu Dawud, and others. There are many reports in this chapter, which we have mentioned with the differences of schools in al-Sa'ayah. [2] His statement: 'has become pure' – the inclusion of fa' in the predicate indicates that the subject carries the meaning of a condition. [3] His statement: 'except the skin of the pig...' The limitation of the exception to these two indicates the purity of the dog's skin through tanning as well, based on the view that it is not impure in essence, as chosen in al-Hidayah, Ghayat al-Bayan, and al-Inayah. The reason for mentioning pig before human is that the context is one of degradation, and in such a context, respect lies in delaying. The reason for the pig's skin not becoming pure through tanning is that it is impure in essence in all its parts, so tanning does not remove its essential impurity; for tanning only removes accidental impurity arising from mixing with impure moistures.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that tanning purifies the skin of any animal, except pig and human. The commentator explains that this ruling is placed in the context of wudu/ghusl to indicate that tanned hides are pure and can be used for water containers. The generality of 'every hide' is supported by the hadith 'Any hide that is tanned has been purified.' The exception of pig and human implies that the skin of a dog is purified by tanning, because a dog is not considered impure in essence in the preferred Hanafi view. The reason pig skin remains impure is that its impurity is essential (najis al-ayn), not accidental, and tanning only removes accidental impurities. The order of mentioning pig before human is due to degradation: the more lowly is mentioned first.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihāb): The skin of an animal, including its hide
دبغ (dabgh): Tanning process that removes moisture and impurities from skin
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Impure in essence; impurity cannot be removed by any process

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the skin of a pig not become pure through tanning according to this passage?
A) Because tanning is not effective on thick skin
B) Because pig is inherently impure (najis al-ayn)
C) Because the hadith only mentions other animals
D) Because pig skin dissolves during tanning

Key Takeaway
Tanning purifies all animal hides except those of pig and human because only those two are impure in essence (najis al-ayn).

Tanning Purifies All Hides Except Pig and Human
Expounds the ruling on tanned hides with proof and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tanned hides, identify the exceptions for pig and human, and explain the reasoning behind each exception.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies all hides except pig (inherent impurity) and human (respect for dignity).

This lesson explains a key taharah ruling: the status of animal hides after tanning. Based on a prophetic hadith, the Hanafi school holds that tanning removes the impurity of hides, making them permissible for use in wudu and prayer. However, two exceptions are noted: pig skin remains impure because the pig is inherently impure (najis al-'ayn), and human skin, though it can become pure if tanned, is forbidden to use out of respect for human dignity. Understanding this ruling clarifies many everyday scenarios involving leather items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its proof
Read the matn: 'Every hide that is tanned is purified except the skin of the pig and the human.' Note the general word 'every' which includes all animal skins. The proof is the hadith 'Any hide that is tanned is purified' (Sunnah ahad).
The hadith is authentic and reported by multiple narrators.
2
Note the two exceptions and their reasoning
Pig skin is excepted because the pig is najis al-'ayn (inherently impure); tanning only removes accidental impurity. Human skin is excepted not because it remains impure, but because its use is forbidden out of respect (hurmat al-insan).
Dog skin is not excepted, implying it is purified by tanning.
3
Analyse the controversy around human skin
Some scholars object that if human skin becomes pure when tanned, it should not be grouped with pig skin. The commentary resolves this by explaining that the exception is from permissible use, not from purity. The author meant 'it is purified and its use is permissible for honorific purposes' except for pig and human.
The exception is disjunctive (human skin does not accept tanning) or it is an exception from use.
4
Apply the ruling to other animals
Since the hadith is general and the only exceptions are pig and human, all other animal hides (e.g., cow, goat, dog) become pure after tanning and can be used for containers, clothing, etc., even in prayer if they are not imitating the human form.
This is the mufta bihi position: dog hide is pure per al-Hidayah.

Every hide that is tanned is purified except the skin of the pig and the human. He said: 'And every hide... etc.'; he mentioned this issue in this context even though it belongs to the chapter of removing impurities, because it is relevant to the discussion of wudu' and ghusl. So it informs that a tanned hide is pure, and it is permissible to perform wudu' and ghusl with water placed in it. The use of 'every' indicates that the ruling is general for every skin, whether from an animal whose meat is eaten or otherwise, due to the hadith: 'Any hide that is tanned is purified' (reported by al-Tirmidhi who deemed it good, Ibn Majah, Muslim, Abu Dawud, and others). There are many reports on this topic, which we have mentioned along with schools' differences in al-Si'ayah. [2] His saying: 'then it is purified' — the inclusion of 'then' in the predicate to imply a conditional meaning. [3] His saying: 'except the skin of the pig and the human' — limiting the exception to these two indicates the purity of dog's skin through tanning, based on the view that it is not inherently impure, as chosen in al-Hidayah, Ghayat al-Bayan, and al-'Inayah. The reason for mentioning pig before human is that the context is one of humiliation, and in such matters, honor is in delay. The reason for the pig's skin not being purified by tanning is that it is inherently impure in all its parts, so tanning does not remove its inherent impurity; tanning only removes accidental impurity resulting from mixing with impure fluids. ... Then he said: 'and the human skin.' Know that they agreed that the pig's skin is not purified by tanning, and they differed regarding its acceptance of tanning: some said it does not accept because it has multiple layers of skin one upon another; some said it accepts but is not purified. Similarly, they differed regarding human skin's acceptance of tanning: some said it does not accept due to its thinness and delicacy making it impossible to flay and tan; some said it accepts. They agreed that if it is tanned, it is purified, but it is forbidden to flay and tan it out of honor and respect. This is stated in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. Consequently, an objection arises against the author: his exception of human skin from the ruling of purity, placing it alongside the pig's skin in the category of non-purity, is not correct because there is no doubt that if it is tanned, it becomes pure; the utmost is that using it for honorific purposes is not permitted, which is a separate matter. It is answered in several ways: 1) It is a disjunctive exception, meaning: every hide that accepts tanning, when tanned, is purified except the pig's and human's skins which do not accept tanning. This answer is not sound according to those who say tanning is possible for both, which is the more correct view. 2) As mentioned in Dhakhirat al-'Uqba and others: It is an exception from 'purified', and what is meant is the permissibility of using it, expressing the necessary by the necessitating. So it is as if he said: 'Every hide that is tanned, its use is permitted except the pig's and human's skins; their use is not permitted — the first due to its impurity, the second due to its dignity.' This involves clear effort, and the correct answer is that the mention of purification by tanning in this context is to indicate the permissibility of using it, so it is as if he said: 'It is purified and wudu' is permissible.'
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling that tanning purifies hides. The base text states: 'Every hide that is tanned is purified except the skin of the pig and the human.' The commentator explains that this ruling, though belonging to the chapter on removing impurities, is placed here because it relates to the purity of water used for wudu' and ghusl when kept in tanned containers. The word 'every' generalizes the ruling to all animal skins, regardless of whether the animal's meat is eaten, based on the prophetic hadith: 'Any hide that is tanned is purified.' This hadith is authenticated by many scholars. The commentator then notes that limiting the exception to pig and human skin implies that dog skin is purified by tanning, following the Hanafi position that a dog is not inherently impure (najis al-'ayn). This view is chosen in al-Hidayah and other works. The reason pig skin is not purified is that the pig is inherently impure in all its parts; tanning only removes accidental impurities, not inherent ones. Then the passage addresses human skin: scholars agree that pig skin is not purified by tanning, but they differ on whether it can accept tanning. Some say pig skin does not accept tanning due to its multiple layers, while others say it accepts but is not purified. For human skin, there is a similar difference: some say it cannot be tanned due to its delicacy, while others say it can be tanned, and if tanned it becomes pure, but it is forbidden to flay and tan human skin out of respect. This raises an objection against the author: why except human skin from purity when it becomes pure if tanned? The answer is that the exception is either disjunctive (human skin does not accept tanning) or it is an exception from permissibility of use, not from purity itself. The correct answer is that the author's mention of purification is to indicate permissible use, so human skin is excluded from permissible use due to honor, not from purification per se. The Hanafi ruling remains that tanning purifies hides except pig and human, with the understanding that human skin is forbidden to use even if tanned out of respect.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihab): Animal hide or skin before or after tanning.
دبغ (dabgh): Tanning process that transforms raw hide into leather, removing moisture and accidental impurities.
نجس العين (najis al-'ayn): Inherently impure substance whose essence is impure regardless of transformation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the hide of a dog after tanning?
A) It remains impure and cannot be used.
B) It becomes pure and permissible to use.
C) It becomes pure but its use is forbidden out of respect.
D) It is not affected by tanning.

Key Takeaway
Tanning purifies all animal hides except pig (due to inherent impurity) and human (due to respect for dignity).

The Exception of Human Skin from Tanning Purification
Critical refinement on the exception of human skin from tanning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on tanning pig and human skin, explain the basis for each exception, and identify the preferred answer to the objection against the matn author.

Core Idea
Human skin becomes pure if tanned, but tanning and using it are forbidden out of respect for human dignity.

Tanning purifies most animal hides, but two exceptions are discussed in detail: pig skin and human skin. This commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah refines a perceived inconsistency in the matn of Al-Wiqayah: why is human skin grouped with pig skin if it actually becomes pure by tanning? The resolution hinges on distinguishing purity from permissibility of use, with human dignity (karamah) as the operative cause for prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Consensus and Disagreement
First, recognize the consensus (ijma) that pig skin does not become pure by tanning, and the disagreement on whether it accepts tanning at all. Similarly for human skin, note the differing views on acceptance.
The text cites commentaries of Al-Hidayah as source.
2
Trace the Objection
Understand the objection raised against the matn author: he excluded human skin from the purifying effect of tanning, but if tanned, human skin does become pure. So why group it with pig skin?
The objection is based on the fact that purity and permissibility are separate.
3
Analyze the First Two Answers
Examine the two proposed answers: (1) it is a disconnected exception (istithna munsail) meaning they do not accept tanning, but this is weak; (2) the exception is from permissibility (expressed by purity as a necessary condition), but this is forced.
Both answers are deemed insufficient.
4
Adopt the Preferred Third Answer
Learn the correct resolution: the mention of tanning here is to indicate permissibility of use. So the exception means that even though tanned, pig and human hide cannot be used—pig due to impurity, human due to honor.
This answer avoids the contradiction and respects the consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والآدمي، اعلم أنهم اتفقوا على أنّ جلد الخنزير لا يطهر بالدباغة، واختلفوا في قبوله لها : فمنهم من قال : لا يقبل ؛ لأنَّ فيه جلوداً مترادفة، بعضها فوق بعض. ومنهم من قال : يقبلُها لكن لا يطهر. وكذا اختلفوا في قبول جلد الإنسان الدباغة : فمنهم من قال : لا يقبلها ؛ لأنه لرقته ولطافته لا يمكن سلخه ودباغته. ومنهم من قال : يقبلها. واتفقوا على أنه لو دبغ يطهر ، لكن يحرم سلخه ودبغه إعزازاً وإكراماً. كذا في شروح «الهداية». وحينئذ يرد على المصنف أنه لا يصح استثناؤه جلد الآدمي من حكم الطهارة، ونظمُهُ في سلكِ عدم الطهارة مع جلد الخنزير، فإنّه لا شك في أنه لو دبغ طهر ، غاية الأمر أنه لا يحل الانتفاع به شرافة، وهو أمر آخر. وأجيب عنه بوجوه : ١. منها : إنّه استثناء منقطع ، والمعنى : كلّ إهاب يقبلُ الدباغ إذا ذُبِغَ طهر إلا جلد الخنزير والآدمي فإنّهما لا يقبلانه، وفيه أنه لا يستقيم عند من قال بإمكان دباغهما، وهو الأصح. ٢. ومنها : ما في «ذخيرة العقبى» وغيره : إنه استثناء من طهر، والمراد به جواز الانتفاع به، تعبيراً عن اللازم بالملزوم ، فكأنه قال : كلّ إهاب دبغ جاز الانتفاع بهِ إلا جلد الخنزير والآدمي ؛ فإنّه لا يجوز الانتفاع بهما بالأول لنجاسته، وبالثاني لكرامته. وفيه تكلّف واضح، والحق الصواب في الجواب أن يقال : ذكر طهارة الجلد بالدباغ في هذا المقام إنما هو لبيان جواز الانتفاع به، فكأنه قال : طهر وجاز الوضوء والغسل وغيرهما من صور الانتفاع به إلا جلد الخنزير والآدمي، فإنه لا يحل الانتفاع به للنجاسة في الأول، وللكرامة في الثاني.
His statement: 'and the human [skin]'. Know that they agreed that the skin of a pig does not become pure by tanning, and they differed regarding its acceptance of tanning: some said it does not accept because it has layered skins, one on top of another; some said it accepts but does not become pure. Similarly, they differed regarding the acceptance of human skin for tanning: some said it does not accept because due to its thinness and delicacy it cannot be flayed and tanned; some said it accepts. And they agreed that if it is tanned, it becomes pure, but its flaying and tanning are forbidden out of honor and respect. Thus it is in the commentaries of Al-Hidayah. At this point, an objection arises against the author: that his exclusion of human skin from the ruling of purity is not correct, and his arranging it in the category of non-purity with pig skin, for there is no doubt that if it were tanned it would become pure, the utmost is that it is not permissible to benefit from it out of honor, and that is another matter. And it was answered with several ways: 1. Among them: it is a disconnected exception, meaning: every hide that accepts tanning, if tanned becomes pure, except pig and human skin, for they do not accept it. But this is not valid according to those who say tanning them is possible, and that is the most correct. 2. Among them: what is in 'Dhakhirat al-Uqba' and others: that it is an exception from purity, and what is meant by it is the permissibility of benefiting from it, expressing the necessary by the necessary condition. As if he said: every hide that is tanned, it is permissible to benefit from it, except pig and human skin: it is not permissible to benefit from them, the first due to its impurity, the second due to its honor. And there is obvious difficulty in this, and the correct answer is to say: the mention of the purity of hide by tanning in this context is only to explain the permissibility of benefiting from it, as if he said: it becomes pure, and it becomes permissible to perform wudu, ghusl and other forms of benefiting from it, except pig and human skin: it is not permissible to benefit from them, the first due to impurity, the second due to honor.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiyah) on the matn of Al-Wiqāyah. It addresses the exception of human skin from the general ruling that tanning purifies hides. The commentators note that there is consensus (ijmā) that pig skin does not become pure by tanning, but they differ on whether it accepts tanning. For human skin, they agree it can become pure if tanned, but it is forbidden to flay and tan it out of respect for human dignity. An objection is raised against the author for grouping human skin with pig skin in the exception, since human skin does become pure if tanned. The preferred response is that the ruling on tanning is mentioned to indicate the permissibility of using the hide, so the exception means that even though tanned, pig and human skin cannot be used—pig skin due to its inherent impurity, human skin due to honor.

Key Terms
دباغ (dibāgh): The process of treating animal hide to remove impurities and odor, typically with tannins.
إهاب (ihāb): The skin or hide of an animal.
كرامة (karāmah): Honor and dignity of humans, which prohibits using human skin even if purified.
استثناء منقطع (istithnā' munqaṭi'): A disconnected exception where the excepted item is not part of the category of the general term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred answer in the text, why is human skin excepted from the permissibility of using tanned hide?
A) Because it cannot become pure by tanning
B) Because tanning and using it are forbidden due to human dignity (karamah)
C) Because it is impure like pig skin
D) Because it does not accept tanning

Key Takeaway
Human skin becomes pure by tanning, but its use is forbidden out of respect for human dignity, not because of impurity.

Real vs. Juristic Tanning: Does Water Re-Impurify Sun-Dried Hide?
Defining tanning and discussing Hanafi dispute on water re-impurifying sun-dried skin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between real and juristic tanning and state the mufta bihi ruling on whether water re-impurifies a sun-dried hide.

Core Idea
Sun-drying purifies a hide only while dry; the chosen Hanafi view holds it stays pure when wet.

Tanning purifies animal hides, but not all tanning is equal. The Hanafi school distinguishes between chemical tanning (permanent purity) and physical drying by sun or dust (conditional purity). A key internal dispute arises: if water touches a hide purified by sun-drying, does impurity return? This lesson walks through the definitions, the narrations, and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish tanning types
Identify the two types: real tanning (using substances like qaraẓ) permanently removes impurity; juristic tanning (sun or dust) purifies only while the hide remains dry.
Real tanning is definitive; juristic tanning is a unique Hanafi category.
2
Locate the disputed issue
The dispute is about the effect of water on a hide purified only by sun-drying. Does wetting it bring back impurity?
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa has two narrations: one says impurity returns, the other says no. Abu Yusuf conditions sun-drying on preservation from decay. Muhammad holds the carcass hide never becomes impure after drying.
4
Analyze the dalil
The reasoning for non-return is that the original impure moisture dissipated; the new moisture is a different substance. An analogy is drawn to earth purified by dryness.
This is a rational (ʿaqlī) proof, not text-based.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states the chosen view (al-mukhtār) from Al-Ghunya is non-return. Identify this as the operative ruling for fatwa.

اعلم أنَّ الدباغة هي إزالة رائحةِ النَّتنِ والرطوبات النجسة من الجلد، فإن كانت بالأدوية كالقرظ ونحوه يطهر الجلد ولا تعودُ نجاسته أبداً، وإن كانت بالتراب أو بالشَّمس يطهر إذا يَبِس ، ثم إن أصابَهُ الماء هل يعودُ نجساً؟ فعن أبي حنيفة : روايتان. وعن أبي يوسف : إن صار بالشمس بحيث لو ترك لم يفسد كان دباغاً. وعن محمد : جلد الميتة إذا يبس ووقع في الماء لا ينجس من غير فصل. قوله : هي إزالة ؛ في إطلاقه إشارة إلى أنه يستوي فيه أن يكون الدابغُ مسلماً أو كافراً أو صبياً أو مجنوناً أو امرأة. كذا في «السراج الوهاج». قوله : فإنّ ... الخ ؛ يشير إلى أن الدباغة على نوعين : ١. حقيقية وهي أن تزال رطوباته بالأدوية كالملح، وقشور الرمان، والعفص، والقرظ - بفتح القاف ورق شجر السلم -. ٢. وحكميّة وهي أن تزالَ بالتشميس : أي إلقاؤه في ضوء الشمس إلى أن تذهب ريحه ورطوبته، أو بالتتريب : أي خلط التراب به وإلقاؤه في الريح، وما أشبه ذلك. قوله : روايتان ؛ في رواية : يعود نجساً ؛ لعودِ الرطوبة المنجسة بابتلاله بالماء ، وفي رواية : لا ؛ لأنَّ البَلَّةَ العائدة ليست تلك الذاهبة، فإنّها تلاشت وذهبت، وهذه غيرها، ونظيره الأرض إذا طهرت باليبس ثمّ أصابها الماء في رواية: تعود نجسة، وفي رواية : لا ، وهو المختار. كذا في «الغنية». قوله : لم يفسد ؛ أي بالتعفّن، وحصول الريح الكريه، فإن فسد دل ذلك على بقاء رطوباته النجسة. قوله : وعن محمد له ؛ وهذا والذي قبله ذكرهما تأييداً لرواية عدم العود، بأنّ
Know that tanning is the removal of the smell of decay and impure moistures from the hide. If it is done with medicinal substances like qaraẓ (a plant) and the like, the hide becomes pure and its impurity never returns. If it is done with dust or sun, it becomes pure when it dries. Then if water touches it, does it become impure again? From Abu Hanifa: two narrations. From Abu Yusuf: if it becomes [dry] by the sun such that if left it would not spoil, then it is [effective] tanning. From Muhammad: the hide of a carcass, when it dries and falls into water, does not become impure without distinction. His statement: 'It is removal' indicates in its generality that it is equal whether the tanner is Muslim or disbeliever, or child, or insane, or woman. Thus in 'Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj'. His statement: 'For ... etc.' indicates that tanning is of two types: 1. Real: removing its moistures with medicines like salt, pomegranate peels, gallnuts, and qaraẓ (with fathah on qaf, leaves of the salam tree). 2. Juristic: removing by sun-drying: i.e., putting it in sunlight until its odor and moisture disappear, or by dusting: i.e., mixing dust with it and putting it in the wind, and similar. His statement: 'two narrations'; in one narration: it returns to impurity due to the return of impure moisture by wetting with water; in another narration: no, because the returning wetness is not the same as that which went, for it dissipated and left, and this is different. Its analogy is the earth when it is purified by dryness, then water touches it; in one narration: it returns to impurity, in another: no, and that is the chosen view. Thus in 'Al-Ghunya'. His statement: 'if it would not spoil' means by rotting and acquiring a bad odor; if it spoils, that indicates the remaining of its impure moistures. His statement: 'and from Muhammad for him' and this and what preceded it are mentioned as support for the narration of non-return, because...
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe (sharḥ) defining tanning and its types. It begins by defining tanning as the removal of foul smell and impure moisture. Two types are distinguished: real tanning (using substances like salt or qaraẓ) which purifies permanently, and juristic tanning (by sun or dust) which purifies only as long as the hide remains dry. The key dispute arises when water touches the sun-dried hide: Abu Hanifa has two narrations—one that it becomes impure again due to the return of moisture, and another that it remains pure because the new moisture is different. Abu Yusuf conditions tanning on the hide becoming so dry it would not spoil. Muhammad holds the hide of a carcass, once dried, never becomes impure from water contact. The commentary supports the narration of non-return, comparing it to land purified by dryness. The text also notes that the tanner's religion or mental capacity does not matter.

Key Terms
دباغة حقيقية (dibāghah ḥaqīqiyyah): Real tanning using chemical substances like salt, pomegranate peels, or qaraẓ.
دباغة حكمية (dibāghah ḥukmiyyah): Juristic tanning by sun-drying or dusting, which purifies only while the hide remains dry.
تشميس (tashmīs): Exposing the hide to sunlight until its odor and moisture are gone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view (al-mukhtār) in the text, what happens when water touches a hide that was purified by sun-drying?
A) It becomes impure again due to return of moisture
B) It remains pure because the new moisture is different
C) It requires re-tanning
D) It becomes impure only if the hide was from a pig

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between real tanning (permanent purity) and juristic sun-drying (conditional purity); the chosen view holds that water does not re-impurify a sun-dried hide.

Tanning and the Dispute on Water After Drying
Defines tanning and presents intra-Hanafi dispute on wetting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two narrations from Abu Hanifa regarding a hide purified by sun/dust tanning when wetted, and identify the mufta bihi view with its supporting evidence from Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Sun/dust tanning purifies; the relied-upon view is that water does not re-impurify because returning moisture is not the original.

This passage addresses a nuanced issue within Hanafi taharah: what happens to a hide that was purified by non-substance methods (sun or dust) when it later becomes wet? The matn and commentary define two types of tanning and present conflicting narrations from Abu Hanifa, alongside supporting stances from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh riwayat within the school and see how analogical reasoning (qiyas) resolves apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
1. Distinguish between real and ruling tanning
Identify the two types: real tanning uses substances (salt, qaraz) and permanently purifies; ruling tanning uses sun or dust and purifies only when dry. Understand that the dispute applies only to ruling tanning.
The matn explicitly states that with substances, impurity never returns.
2
2. Identify the contested issue and the two narrations
After ruling tanning (sun/dust) dries the hide, if water touches it, does the impurity return? Two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one says yes (moisture returns impure consistency), one says no (the returning moisture is new and pure).
These narrations are preserved as riwayat from the Imam.
3
3. Examine the supporting arguments from Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf: if sun-drying prevents decay, it is effective tanning — implying the hide stays pure after wetting. Muhammad al-Shaybani states flatly that a dried carcass hide does not become impure when it falls into water, without distinguishing between water or tanning method.
These positions bolster the non-return narration.
4
4. Recognize the mufta bihi and its analogy
The commentary states the chosen (mukhtar) view is non-return, supported by the analogy of earth: dried earth purified by drying does not become impure when subsequently wetted unless fresh najis is introduced. This qiyas confirms the principle that the original impure moisture has dissipated.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

[١] قوله : هي إزالة ; في إطلاقه إشارة إلى أنه يستوي فيه أن يكون الدابغُ مسلماً أو كافراً أو صبياً أو مجنوناً أو امرأة. كذا في «السراج الوهاج».

[٢] قوله : فإنّ ... الخ ; يشير إلى أن الدباغة على نوعين :
١. حقيقية وهي أن تزال رطوباته بالأدوية كالملح، وقشور الرمان، والعفص، والقرظ - بفتح القاف ورق شجر السلم -.
٢. وحكميّة وهي أن تزالَ بالتشميس : أي إلقاؤه في ضوء الشمس إلى أن تذهب ريحه ورطوبته، أو بالتتريب : أي خلط التراب به وإلقاؤه في الريح، وما أشبه ذلك.

[٣] قوله : روايتان ; في رواية : يعود نجساً ; لعودِ الرطوبة المنجسة بابتلاله بالماء ، وفي رواية : لا ; لأنَّ البَلَّةَ العائدة ليست تلك الذاهبة، فإنّها تلاشت وذهبت، وهذه غيرها، ونظيره الأرض إذا طهرت باليبس ثمّ أصابها الماء في رواية: تعود نجسة، وفي رواية : لا ، وهو المختار. كذا في «الغنية».

[0] قوله : وعن محمد له ; وهذا والذي قبله ذكرهما تأييداً لرواية عدم العود، بأنّ هاتين الروايتين دلتا على الطهارة وحصول الدباغة من غير فصل، فقوله: «من غير فصل» متعلق بكلتيهما.
ومعناه : من غير فصل بين أن يصيبه الماء وبين أن لا يصيبه، وهذا على الرواية عن أبي يوسف له، ومن غير فصل بين أن يدبغ بالأدوية أو بالتشميس، هذا على الثانية، وأكثر الناظرين على تعلقه بالثاني فقط.
Know that tanning is the removal of the smell of stench and impure moistures from the hide. If it is done with substances like qaraz and similar, it purifies the hide and its impurity never returns. If it is done with dust or sun, it purifies when it dries. Then if water touches it, does it become impure? From Abu Hanifa: two narrations. From Abu Yusuf: if it becomes by sun such that if left it would not spoil, it is tanning. From Muhammad: the hide of a carcass when it dries and falls into water does not become impure without distinction. Washing and other forms of using it are permissible except the hide of pig and human, for it is not permissible to benefit from them due to impurity in the first and honor in the second. [1] His saying: it is removal; in its absoluteness is an indication that it is equal whether the tanner is Muslim or disbeliever, child, insane, or woman. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. [2] His saying: 'if... etc.' indicates that tanning is of two types: 1. Real: its moistures are removed by substances like salt, pomegranate peels, gallnuts, and qaraz - with fatha on qaf, leaves of the salm tree. 2. Ruling: its removal by sunning: throwing it in sunlight until its smell and moisture go, or by dusting: mixing dust with it and throwing it in the wind and the like. [3] His saying: two narrations; in one narration: it returns to impurity due to the return of impure moisture by becoming wet with water; in another: no, because the returning moisture is not that which went, as it dissipated and went, and this is other than it. Its analogy is earth when it is purified by drying then water touches it: in one narration it returns to impurity, in another no, and it is the chosen one. Thus in al-Ghunya. [4] His saying: would not spoil; i.e., by decay and obtaining foul smell, for if it spoils that indicates the persistence of its impure moistures. [0] His saying: and from Muhammad; this and what preceded are mentioned in support of the narration of non-return, because these two narrations indicate purity and attainment of tanning without distinction, so his saying 'without distinction' relates to both. Its meaning: without distinction between water touching it or not, and this according to the narration from Abu Yusuf; and without distinction between tanning with substances or with sunning, this according to the second, and most examiners relate it to the second only.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effects of water on a hide purified by sun or dust tanning. The matn first defines tanning as the removal of stench and impure moisture, distinguishing between real tanning (with substances) and ruling tanning (sun, dust). It then raises the question: if water touches the hide after drying, does it become impure again? From Abu Hanifa, there are two narrations: one says it returns to impurity because the impure moisture returns by wetting; the other says no, because the new moisture is not the same as the original. The commentary supports the latter with an analogy: earth dried and then wetted — the chosen view is that it remains pure. Abu Yusuf's condition: if sun-drying makes it resistant to decay, it counts as tanning. Muhammad states unconditionally that a dried carcass hide does not become impure when it falls into water. The commentary explains these narrations support the view of non-return, with 'without distinction' meaning either between water or no water, or between substance and sun tanning, though most attach it only to the second distinction.

Key Terms
دباغة (dibāghah): Removal of foul odor and impure moisture from hide using substances or sun/dust.
تشميس (tashmīs): Drying in the sun as a form of ruling (hukmi) tanning.
رواية (riwāyah): A transmitted narration from the imam reflecting a legal opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It becomes najis again.
B) It remains tahir.
C) It becomes impure only if the water is najis.
D) It depends on the type of animal.

Key Takeaway
Sun/dust tanning purifies a hide; the Hanafi mufta bihi holds that subsequent wetting does not re-impurify it, as the original impure moisture has dissipated.

Purification of Hide by Slaughter (Tazkiyah) Equivalent to Tanning
States that slaughter purifies hide and flesh like tanning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that tazkiyah purifies hide like tanning and apply the qiyas reasoning to distinguish between edible and non-edible animals.

Core Idea
Slaughter purifies hide and flesh just like tanning – it removes impurity at the source.

In the Hanafi school, the purity of animal hide is crucial for its use in prayer and daily life. This lesson explores the principle that Islamic slaughter (tazkiyah) purifies the hide and even the flesh, just as tanning does. The proof is a clear analogy: both remove impure moisture, but slaughter does so more effectively. The passage also extends this to musk, showing the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is tazkiyah (ritual slaughter). The ruling: it purifies the hide and flesh of animals, even if the meat is not eaten (non-edible animals).
The base ruling: 'What is purified by tanning is purified by tazkiyah.'
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is qiyas (analogy) between tazkiyah and tanning. The text states: 'it does the work of tanning in removing impure moistures.'
The type of qiyas is jaliy (evident analogy).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the analogy prove the ruling? Both tanning and tazkiyah remove impure moisture, but tazkiyah does so more effectively because it prevents the moisture from connecting with the meat in the first place.
This makes the analogy even stronger (first awla).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school about whether the flesh of non-edible animals is purified. The correct (sahih) view, as stated in al-Hidayah, is that it is purified.
The passage also mentions that pig and human hide are not purified by tanning, so they are also not purified by tazkiyah.
5
Apply the ruling to musk
The commentary extends the principle to musk: the musk pouch (nafijah) from a gazelle is pure because its drying acts as its tanning, regardless of whether the animal was slaughtered or not.
This shows the school's consistent reasoning across different materials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما طَهُرَ جلده بالدبغ طَهُرَ بالزَّكاة، وكذا لحمه ، وإن لم يؤكل، وما لا فلا

[١] قوله : والصحيح في نافجة المسك ; المسك – بالكسر - : طيب معروف يضرب به المثل في لطف الرائحةِ وحسنها، وحقيقته دمّ يجتمع في سرَّة الظبي بإذن الله في وقت معلوم من السنة بمنزلة المواد التي تنصب إلى الأعضاء، وهذه السرة جعلها الله معدنا للمسك، والنافجة معدنها ومأواها.

[٢] قوله : من غير فصل ; أي من غير فرق بين أن يكون نافجة دابة ذكية أو غير ذكيّة أصابها الماء أو لم يصب ; لأنَّ يبسها دباغها، ولا تعود نجاسته بعده ، فهو طاهر يجوز الصلاة معه على كل حال.

[٣] قوله : بالذكاة ; بالذال المعجمة بمعنى الذبح والوجه في هذا أنها تعمل عمل الدباغ في إزالة الرطوبات النجسة، بل أولى؛ لأنها تمنع اتصال الرطوبات النجسة، والدباغة تزيلها بعد الاتصال.

[٤]قوله : وكذا لحمه ; أي يطهر اللحم بالذبح ، وإن كان لحم ما لا يؤكل، وهو الصحيح، كما في «الهداية»، وفيه اختلاف ذكره في «النهاية».
And that whose hide is purified by tanning is purified by tazkiyah (slaughter), and likewise its flesh, even if it is not eaten. And whatever is not [purified by tanning] is not [purified by tazkiyah]. [1] His saying: and the correct regarding the nafijah of musk; musk (with kasr): is a well-known perfume proverbial for its pleasantness and fineness. Its reality is blood that collects in the navel of the gazelle by Allah's permission at a known time of the year, like the materials that flow to the organs. This navel Allah made a mine for musk, and the nafijah is its mine and abode. [2] His saying: without distinction; i.e., without difference whether the nafijah is from a slaughtered animal or non-slaughtered, whether water touched it or not; because its drying is its tanning, and its impurity does not return after that. So it is pure, and prayer is permissible with it in any case. [3] His saying: by tazkiyah (with the dotted dhal) meaning slaughter; and the rationale is that it does the work of tanning in removing impure moistures, even more so because it prevents the connection of impure moistures, while tanning removes them after connection. [4] His saying: and likewise its flesh; i.e., the flesh is purified by slaughter, even if it is the flesh of what is not eaten, and this is the correct view, as in al-Hidayah, and there is difference mentioned in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof (qiyas) in the Hanafi school: slaughter (tazkiyah) purifies the hide and flesh of an animal just as tanning does, even if the meat is not edible (e.g., wild animals). The proof is qiyas: both remove impure moisture; slaughter even does so more thoroughly by preventing contact with impurities. The commentary extends this to musk: the musk pouch (nafijah) is pure even from a non-slaughtered animal because its drying serves as its tanning, and it does not become impure if wetted. The matn's exception 'and whatever is not [purified]' refers to pig and human hide, which tanning does not purify; similarly slaughter does not purify them.

Key Terms
تذكية (tazkiyah): Islamic ritual slaughter that purifies the animal's hide and meat.
مسك (misk): Musk, a perfume from the blood of a deer's navel pouch.
نافجة (nāfijah): The pouch containing musk in the gazelle's belly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on the hide of a non-edible animal after it has been slaughtered (tazkiyah)?
A) It remains impure.
B) It becomes pure like after tanning.
C) It becomes pure only if tanned.
D) It becomes pure but cannot be used in prayer.

Key Takeaway
Islamic slaughter (tazkiyah) purifies the hide and flesh of animals just as tanning does, even for non-edible animals, with the exception of pig and human.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
