Purity of Hair, Bones, and Sinews of Dead Animals; Human Hair Dispute
Ruling on purity of dead animal parts with reasoning (talil) and intra-Hanafi human hair dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purity of hair, bones, and sinews of dead animals, and distinguish between the two Hanafi opinions on human hair, citing the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Parts of a dead animal that have no life are pure; human hair is a contested case within the Hanafi school with impurity as the relied-upon view.

This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`ayah clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi taharah: not everything from a dead animal is impure. The text explains that hair, bones, sinews, hooves, and horns are pure because they lack life. It also addresses the important case of human hair, where two Hanafi opinions exist, and the later authorities prefer impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed parts and their ruling
The text explicitly lists hair, bone, sinew, hoof, and horn of a dead animal and states that they are pure (tahir).
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
These parts are not considered carrion because they have no life; death only applies to things that contained life. Evidence: they do not feel pain when cut except through attached flesh.
This is a key Hanafi principle: impurity attaches to life, not to inert matter.
3
Note the definition of valid dhabh
The passage defines dhabh (Islamic slaughter) as performed by a Muslim or Kitabi who does not deliberately omit tasmiyah. If tasmiyah is omitted deliberately, the animal is carrion and not purified.
This is relevant to understand what makes an animal 'dead without lawful slaughter' (maytah).
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute on human hair
Human hair is singled out. There are two opinions: pure (from Al-Hidayah, Al-Tuhfah, Al-Bada'i') and impure (from Al-Tanwir, Al-Durr al-Mukhtar). The mufta bihi is impurity.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; later authorities performed tarjih.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
In daily life, one can pray with clothes that have touched the hair of a dead animal (e.g., wool from a naturally dead sheep) because it is pure. However, human hair on clothes or body should be removed before prayer according to the mufta bihi.

Source Text

شعر الميتة، وعظمها، وعصبها، وحافرها، وقرنها، والمراد بالذكاة أن يذبح المسلم أو الكتابي من غير أن يترك التسمية عامداً. ( قوله : والمراد ... الخ؛ يريد أن الذكاة المطهرة للجلد إنما هي الذكاة المعتبرة في الشرع، فلو ذبح المجوسي أو المسلم أو الكتابي - أي اليهودي والنصراني – وترك التسمية عامداً يكون مذبوحه ميتة لا يطهر جلده ولا لحمه بهذا الذبح. قوله : وشعر الميتة ... الخ، والوجه في طهارة هذه الأشياء أن هذه الأشياء ليست بميتة؛ لأنها عبارة عمّا حَلّ فيه الموت بغير وجه شرعي، والموتُ عبارة عن عدمِ الحياة، فلا يحل إلا في ما يحل فيه الحياة؛ ولذا لا يقال للجماد : ميت. وهذه الأشياء لا حياة فيها، بدليل أنه لا يتألّم بقطعها إلا بما يتصل به من اللحم والشحم، ونحو ذلك كذا في «الهداية»، وغيره. قوله : وحافرها؛ أي حافر الفرس والحمار وغيرهما، وهو العظم الذي يكون في أرجلهما من تحت. قوله : وشعر الإنسان ... الخ؛ أفرده بالذكر مع دخوله في إطلاق ما مضى اهتماماً بشأنه. الأول : طهارته : وصححه صاحب التحفة (۷۲)، والهداية (۲۱). واختاره في «البدائع» (١ : ٨٦). الثاني : عدم طهارته اختاره صاحب التنوير (۱ : ۱۲۷)، وقال الحصكفي في «الدر المختار» (۱ : ۱۲۷): «هذا أصح ما يفتى به»، وأقره ابن عابدين في رد المحتار (۱ : ۱۲۷). (۱) العَصَب : عضو أبيض شبيه العظم لين الانعطاف، صلب في الانفصال. ينظر : «السعاية» ( ص ٤١٥). (۲) قيده في الدر المختار (۱ : ۱۳۸ بغير المنتوف : أي رؤوسه التي فيها الدسومة. صحح الزاهدي في «القنية» أيضاً : طهارة ذبيحة المجوسي. وأقره في «البحر»(۱ : ۱۰۹).
The hair of the dead animal, its bone, its sinew, its hoof, its horn, and the meaning of dhabh is that a Muslim or Kitabi slaughters without deliberately omitting the tasmiyah. (The saying: 'and the meaning... etc.' intends that the dhabh which purifies the skin is the dhabh considered valid in the Sharia. So if a Magian or Muslim or Kitabi – i.e., Jew or Christian – slaughters and omits the tasmiyah deliberately, his slaughtered animal is carrion, and neither its skin nor its meat is purified by this slaughter. The saying: 'And the hair of the dead animal... etc.' And the reason for the purity of these things is that these things are not carrion, because carrion refers to that in which death occurs without a lawful means, and death is the absence of life. So it only applies to that which contains life; thus a solid object is not called dead. These things have no life in them, evidenced by the fact that they do not feel pain when cut, except through the attached flesh and fat, etc. This is stated in Al-Hidayah and others. The saying: 'And its hoof' means the hoof of a horse, donkey, etc., which is the bone that is in their legs from below. The saying: 'And the hair of humans... etc.' He mentioned it separately despite its inclusion in the previous general statement due to its importance. The first: its purity – affirmed by the author of Al-Tuhfah (72), Al-Hidayah (21), and chosen in Al-Bada'i' (1:86). The second: its impurity – chosen by the author of Al-Tanwir (1:127), and Al-Haskafi said in Al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:127): 'This is the most correct of what is given as fatwa,' and Ibn Abidin affirmed it in Radd al-Muhtar (1:127).) Al-'asab: a white organ resembling bone, soft in bending, hard in separation. See Al-Si'ayah (p. 415). (He restricted it in Al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:138) to non-plucked: i.e., its ends which have grease. Al-Zahidi also affirmed in Al-Qunyah the purity of the slaughter of a Magian, and it was affirmed in Al-Bahr (1:109).)
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement accompanied by its reasoning (talil). It addresses the purity of certain parts of a dead animal (maytah) and the definition of valid Islamic slaughter (dhabh). The author first lists items: hair, bone, sinew, hoof, and horn of a dead animal, and states that they are pure. The reason given is that these items are not considered 'maytah' because they do not possess life; death only applies to things that were alive. Since these parts have no life, they are not contaminated by the death of the animal. Evidence is provided: they do not feel pain when cut except through attached flesh. Then the author defines dhabh: it must be performed by a Muslim or a Kitabi (Jew/Christian) who does not deliberately omit the tasmiyah (saying 'Bismillah'). If the tasmiyah is omitted deliberately, the slaughtered animal becomes carrion and its skin and meat are not purified. The passage then specifically addresses human hair. Although it is included in the general ruling, it is singled out due to importance. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the first opinion (chosen by Al-Hidayah, Al-Tuhfah, Al-Bada'i') holds human hair is pure; the second opinion (chosen by Al-Tanwir and considered the relied-upon fatwa in Al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar) holds it is impure. The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is impurity for human hair. The text also includes a definition of 'asab (sinew) as a white organ resembling bone. A note is added about the restriction of the ruling on Magian slaughter to non-plucked hair.

Key Terms
شعر الميتة (sha'r al-maytah): The hair of an animal that died without lawful slaughter.
ذكاة (dhabh): Islamic slaughter that purifies the meat and skin; requires a Muslim or Kitabi and deliberate tasmiyah.
تسمية (tasmiyah): Saying 'Bismillah' at the time of slaughter.
عصب (asab): A white organ resembling bone, soft in bending but hard to separate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is impure (najis).
B) It is pure (tahir).
C) It is pure only if the animal was a herbivore.
D) It is impure unless it is wool.

Key Takeaway
The hair, bones, sinews, hooves, and horns of a dead animal are pure; human hair is a disputed case with the preferred opinion being impure.

Purity of a Tooth and Validity of Prayer with It
Ruling that a tooth is pure and prayer valid even if inserted tooth exceeds dirham size

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the purity of a tooth and explain why prayer remains valid even if the reinserted tooth exceeds the size of a dirham, and distinguish the relied-upon view from that of Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
A reinserted tooth is pure because it is bone without sensation; prayer is valid regardless of size.

This passage deals with a practical question: if a person's tooth falls out and they reinsert it into their mouth, does it affect the validity of prayer? The answer touches on the purity of bones and the specific nature of teeth. The Hanafi school had an internal dispute on whether such a tooth could invalidate prayer if it exceeded the size of a dirham. The correct view, as established by the later scholars, is that the tooth is pure and prayer is valid.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the tooth is pure (tahir) because it is bone, and prayer is valid even if the tooth is larger than a dirham.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi variant
Imam Muhammad reportedly held that prayer is invalid if the inserted object exceeds a dirham. This is the variant view.
The majority of Hanafi scholars follow Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
3
Understand the basis of the variant
Some scholars considered the tooth to be sinew (asab) or to have sensation, which could make it impure or affect the prayer.
The tooth's nature as bone or sinew was the point of contention.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The people of the madhhab corrected that the tooth is bone with no sensation, so it remains pure and does not affect prayer.
This is the tarjih (preference) of the school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that prayer is valid regardless of the size of the reinserted tooth.
Imam Muhammad's view is considered marjuh (weak) and not acted upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعظمه طاهر، وتجوز صلاة من أعاد سنه إلى فيه وإن جاوز قدر الدرهم. قوله : وتجوز؛ يعني من سقطت أسنانه كلها أو بعضها فأعادها إلى مكانها وصلّى جازت صلاته، وإن كان المعاد أزيد من قدر الدرهم. قوله : أفرد ... الخ؛ دفع لسؤال مقدّر، وهو أن إفراد مسألة جواز الصلاة بإعادة سنّه بالذكر غير محتاج إليه؛ لأنها فهمت مما مرّ أنّ العظم طاهر، فإنّ السّنّ أيضاً عظم، وحاصله : أنه إنما أفردها بالذكر لوجود الاختلاف فيها، فلرفعه والإشارة إلى عدم اعتباره صرح به. قوله : لمكان؛ علة للإفراد والمكان مصدر ميمي بمعنى الكون، ومنشأ الاختلاف في هذه المسألة اختلافهم في أنّ السّنّ هل هو عظم أم عصب؟ وعلى الأول هل له حس أم لا؟ فإنّ منهم من ذهب إلى أن العظم لا حس فيه إلا السن، وعلى التقدير الثاني هل العصب يكون نجساً أم لا؟ والذي صححه أهلُ المذهب أنه عظم لا حس له.
And its bone is pure, and the prayer of one who returns his tooth to his mouth is permissible even if it exceeds the size of a dirham. The saying: 'and is permissible' means: one whose teeth, all or some, have fallen out and he returns them to their place and prays, his prayer is permissible, even if the returned (object) is more than the size of a dirham. The saying: 'He singled it out... etc.' is a response to an anticipated question: that singling out the issue of the permissibility of prayer by returning his tooth is unnecessary because it is understood from what has passed that bone is pure, and the tooth is also bone. The summary: he singled it out only because there is a difference of opinion regarding it, so to remove it and indicate that it is not considered, he stated it explicitly. The saying: 'due to (the existence of) a place' is the reason for the singling out, and 'makan' is a verbal noun meaning existence. The source of the difference in this issue is their difference concerning whether the tooth is bone or sinew? On the first (view) whether it has sensation or not? For among them is one who held that bone has no sensation except the tooth. On the second view, is sinew impure or not? And that which the people of the madhhab have corrected is that it is bone with no sensation.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a specific ruling on the purity of a human tooth and the validity of prayer when a dislodged tooth is reinserted into the mouth. The core rule is that the tooth is pure (tahir) because it is bone, and bone has been established as pure. Therefore, even if the inserted tooth is larger than the size of a dirham (a coin), the prayer is still valid. The commentary explains that this ruling is stated separately despite being implied from the previous ruling on bones, because there is a difference of opinion (ikhtilaf). Some scholars questioned whether the tooth is bone or sinew, and whether bone has sensation. In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon (sahhah) view is that the tooth is bone without sensation, so it is pure. The opposing view, attributed to Imam Muhammad, reportedly held that if the inserted object exceeds a dirham, the prayer is not permissible. The text rejects this and affirms the validity. The phrase 'due to the existence of a place' (li-makan) means that the reason for the ikhtilaf is the existence of a point of contention. The commentary clarifies that the tooth is not sinew, which would be a different ruling, but bone, and since it has no sensation, it does not affect prayer.

Key Terms
سن (sinn): Tooth; a bone that may have fallen out and is reinserted.
قدر الدرهم (qadr al-dirham): The size of a dirham coin, used as a measure for impurity that prevents prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The prayer is valid because the tooth is pure.
C) The prayer is valid only if the tooth is from the same person.
D) The prayer is invalid because the tooth is impure.

Key Takeaway
The tooth is pure bone without sensation, so its presence in the mouth, regardless of size, does not invalidate prayer.

Ruling on a Well When Impurity Falls
Introduction to the section on wells; general ruling on impurity falling into a well.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a well when impurity falls into it, and explain that there is no difference between light and heavy impurity for the well.

Core Idea
Any impurity, light or heavy, makes a well impure; the classification only matters for clothing.

This lesson begins a new section on wells. The matn states a general principle: when an impure substance falls into a well, the well becomes impure. The commentary clarifies that this applies to all impurities, regardless of their classification as light or heavy. The distinction between light and heavy impurity is only relevant when purifying clothes, not wells. Understanding this ruling prevents the common misconception that a 'light' impurity might not affect the well's water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The subject is a well (bi'r) and an impurity (najas) that falls into it. The ruling concerns the status of the well's water.
2
Extract the ruling
The matn and commentary state that the well becomes impure. The word 'najis' is used in a general sense without qualification.
3
Note the key distinction
The commentary explicitly states that there is no difference between light impurity (e.g., urine of a boy who has not eaten solid food) and heavy impurity (e.g., adult urine or dog saliva) concerning the well. The only place where the distinction applies is in purifying clothing.
Light impurity on clothes requires sprinkling water; heavy impurity requires washing multiple times.
4
Apply to a scenario
If a small amount of baby urine falls into a well, the well water becomes impure and must be purified according to Hanafi rules, just as if it were dog urine.
The method of purification (e.g., removing water or waiting for it to change) is covered later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: بئر وقع فيها نجس. قوله : فصل؛ لما كانت مسائل البئر ممتاز عمّا سبق فصلها عما سبق بفصل، وفي بعض النسخ : لا أثر للفصل هاهنا، وهو أحسن. قوله : نجس؛ بفتح الجيم أو كسرها، وفي إطلاقه إشارة إلى أنه لا فرق بين النجاسة المخففة وبين المغلظة، فإنّ أثر التخفيف إنّما يظهرُ في الثياب، فلو بالت فيها ...

In some copies: there is no effect for the section here, and that is better. Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section on wells. The matn states: 'A well in which an impure substance falls.' The commentary explains that this section is separated from previous topics. There is a textual variant: some copies do not mark the section break, which the author considers preferable. The word 'najis' can be read with either vowel on the jim, and its unrestricted mention indicates that there is no distinction between light impurity (such as the urine of a boy who has not eaten solid food) and heavy impurity (such as the urine of an adult or dog) regarding the well. The effect of the distinction between light and heavy impurity only applies to clothing (i.e., in matters of purifying clothes). The text begins to say 'if one urinates in it...' but is cut off. The complete ruling that follows (outside this window) will detail how the well is purified and the legal incidents.

Key Terms
نجس (najis): An impure substance that falls into the well; includes both light and heavy impurities.
بئر (bi'r): A well; a water source that can become impure.

Purity of Bone and Prayer with Reinserted Tooth
Ruling on bone purity and prayer with reinserted tooth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purity of bone and apply it to the case of a reinserted tooth, identifying the intra-madhhab difference and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Bone is pure; a reinserted tooth, even larger than a dirham, does not invalidate prayer.

The Hanafi school rules that bones of humans and animals (except those inherently impure) are pure (tahir). This principle extends even to a tooth that falls out and is reinserted into the mouth. However, a difference arose among the early Hanafi masters: does the tooth count as bone or nerve? And does its size affect the validity of prayer? This lesson clarifies the position of the school and the resolution of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the matn statement: 'And his bone is pure. And the prayer of one who reinserted his tooth is permissible even if it exceeds the size of a dirham.' This establishes a general principle and a specific application.
2
Understand the reason for explicit mention
The commentary explains that the tooth case was singled out because of a difference of opinion (ikhtilaf). Without this, the ruling would be inferred from the general purity of bone.
The commentator rebuts the implied question: why mention the tooth separately?
3
Trace the dispute to its root
The difference of opinion originates from two sub-issues: (1) Is the tooth bone or nerve? (2) Does bone have sensation? Some held that the tooth is nerve and thus impure, or that it has sensation and thereby becomes impure when detached.
The term 'al-makan' (cause) refers to the existence of this disagreement.
4
Identify the positions of the major Hanafi imams
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani considered the tooth to possibly be nerve or have sensation, so he ruled that if the reinserted part exceeds a dirham, the prayer is invalid. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf held that the tooth is bone without sensation, making it pure regardless of size.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between the Sahibayn? Actually Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa; only Imam Muhammad differs.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The commentary states: 'What the people of the madhhab corrected is that it is a bone without sensation.' Thus the relied-upon position is that prayer is valid even if the reinserted tooth exceeds a dirham.
This tarjih is based on the authoritative view of the later Hanafi masters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعظمه طاهر، وتجوز صلاة من أعاد سنه إلى فيه وإن جاوز قدر الدرهم.
[1] قوله: وتجوز؛ يعني من سقطت أسنانه كلها أو بعضها فأعادها إلى مكانها وصلى جازت صلاته، وإن كان المعاد أزيد من قدر الدرهم.
[2] قوله: أفرد... الخ؛ دفع لسؤال مقدر، وهو أن إفراد مسألة جواز الصلاة بإعادة سنّه بالذكر غير محتاج إليه؛ لأنها فهمت مما مرّ أن العظم طاهر، فإن السن أيضاً عظم، وحاصله: أنه إنما أفردها بالذكر لوجود الاختلاف فيها، فلرفعه والإشارة إلى عدم اعتباره صرح به (1).
[3] قوله: لمكان؛ علة للإفراد والمكان مصدر ميمي بمعنى الكون، ومنشأ الاختلاف في هذه المسألة اختلافهم في أن السن هل هو عظم أم عصب؟ وعلى الأول هل له حس أم لا؟ فإن منهم من ذهب إلى أن العظم لا حس فيه إلا السن، وعلى التقدير الثاني هل العصب يكون نجساً أم لا؟ والذي صححه أهل المذهب أنه عظم لا حس له.
And his bone is pure. And the prayer of one who reinserted his tooth into his mouth is permissible even if it exceeds the size of a dirham. [1] His statement: 'and it is permissible' means: the one whose teeth all or some fell and he reinserted them into their place and prayed, his prayer is permissible, even if the reinserted amount is more than a dirham. [2] His statement: 'he singled out... etc' is a rebuttal of an implied question: that singling out the issue of the permissibility of prayer by reinserting a tooth for mention is unnecessary because it is understood from what has passed that bone is pure, for the tooth is also bone. The summary is: he singled it out for mention because of the difference of opinion about it, so to remove it and indicate that it is not considered, he explicitly stated it. [3] His statement: 'because of' is the cause for singling out; 'al-makan' is a verbal noun meaning existence. The origin of the difference in this issue is their difference over whether the tooth is bone or nerve? And on the first view, does it have sensation or not? For among them is one who held that bone has no sensation except the tooth. And on the second estimation, is the nerve impure or not? And what the people of the madhhab corrected is that it is a bone without sensation.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage addresses the purity of bones and the specific case of a tooth that falls out and is reinserted into the mouth. The matn states that bone is pure (tahir) and that prayer is valid even if the reinserted tooth exceeds the size of a dirham. The commentary explains that this ruling is explicitly mentioned despite being understood from the general principle that bone is pure, because of a difference of opinion. The difference stems from whether the tooth is bone or nerve tissue, and whether it has sensation. Some scholars held that the tooth is nerve and thus impure, or that it has sensation and thus could be impure. The relied-upon position (mu'tamad) of the Hanafi school is that the tooth is bone without sensation, hence pure. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani had a differing view that if the reinserted tooth exceeds a dirham, prayer is not valid. However, the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) is the view of Abu Hanifa that it is valid. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute where the school has settled on the position that bone is pure and prayer with it is valid.

Key Terms
عظم (adhm): Bone of a human or animal; ruled as pure (tahir) by the Hanafi school.
سن (sinn): Tooth; subject to dispute whether it is bone or nerve tissue; the relied-upon Hanafi view is that it is bone without sensation, thus pure.
عصب (asab): Nerve; some scholars considered the tooth to be nerve tissue, which would be impure when detached from a living body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Prayer is invalid because the tooth is considered impure.
B) Prayer is valid because bone is pure, and the tooth is bone.
C) Prayer is valid only if the tooth is smaller than a dirham.
D) Prayer is invalid according to all Hanafi scholars.

Key Takeaway
Bone is pure in the Hanafi school, and a reinserted tooth is treated as bone, making prayer valid even if the tooth exceeds a dirham.

Well Water Purification: Nuzuh al-Kull When an Animal Dies
Rulings on a well when najasah falls or an animal dies and decays.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on well water purification when a dead animal swells or decomposes, and identify the exceptions for minor impurities and necessary excused cases.

Core Idea
A dead animal that swells or decomposes requires drawing all well water, even if the impurity is light.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the detailed Hanafi rulings for wells when impurity falls in or an animal dies. The central principle is that any impurity, even a drop, requires emptying the entire well, except for excused small amounts like camel dung in desert wells. We will unpack the ruling and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering event and ruling
Determine what happens (animal dies and swells or decomposes) and the required action: nuzuh al-kull (drawing all water).
The ruling applies regardless of the animal being edible (like a sheep).
2
Note the exception for minor impurities
Even a single drop of urine, blood, or wine triggers full nuzuh, but camel and sheep dung in desert wells is excused if the amount is small (not considered much by an observer).
This exception is based on necessity and custom.
3
Understand the condition of the animal
The animal must be blood-bearing and non-aquatic (e.g., not a fish). If it is removed alive, nuzuh is only required if there is certain impurity on its body or it is impure in itself (like a pig).
If the animal dies outside and then falls in, the ruling is the same.
4
Recognize the terminology: swelling vs decomposition
Swelling (intifakh) and decomposition (tafakhkhukh) are both triggers. Decomposition is more severe but no extra steps (like washing walls) are needed beyond nuzuh al-kull.
The commentator mentions decomposition to dispel any misconception about additional purification.

Or an animal dies in it and swells or decomposes. A sheep and other edible animals: drawing all the water is obligatory despite the impurity being light, as stated by Qazi Khan. And that there is no difference between a small amount and a large amount, such that even if a drop of urine, blood, or wine falls, drawing all the water is obligatory. But it should be restricted to what is not excused due to necessity: like camel and sheep dung, for it does not spoil the water because desert wells have no barrier, livestock come to them, defecate around them, and the wind throws it into them, so the small amount, which is what the observer does not consider much, is excused. This is stated in Al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'or dies' – if the animal is taken out alive, then if there is certain impurity on its body or it is impure in itself, drawing all the water is obligatory, otherwise not. [2] His statement: 'in it' – this qualifier is agreed upon; for the ruling is the same if it dies outside or falls into it, as in Al-Bahr. [3] His statement: 'animal' – meaning if it is a blood-bearing animal not of aquatic origin, otherwise its death does not spoil the water as mentioned earlier. [4] His statement: 'and swells... etc' – swelling is the enlargement of something by inflation; it is said: the stomach swelled, i.e., it became large with wind and the like. Decomposition is the spreading and separation of parts. He explicitly mentioned decomposition although there is no need for it, for when the ruling of swelling is known, the ruling of decomposition is known by primary inference because it is more severe, to repel the misconception that in decomposition something extra is required in drawing water, like washing the walls of the well and the like, because it is more severe. In al-Tabyin: reliance is upon it. There is disagreement on the definition of 'a large amount': it is said a quarter of the water's surface, a third, most of it, all of it, or that every bucket contains one or two dung pellets. See Hidayat al-Sa'luk.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage deals with the purification of well water when impurity falls in or an animal dies inside. The matn states: 'Section: A well into which impurity falls. Or an animal dies in it and swells or decomposes.' The commentary explains that the word 'section' is used to set apart the rulings of wells from previous topics, though some copies omit it. The central ruling is that if a dead animal (e.g., a sheep or any edible animal) swells or decomposes in the well, all the water must be drawn (nuzuh al-kull). This is despite the impurity being considered 'light' (since the animal's meat is halal). The commentator adds that there is no distinction between a small and large quantity of impurity; even a single drop of urine, blood, or wine requires emptying the entire well. However, an exception is made for camel and sheep dung in desert wells, where small amounts are excused due to necessity (the animals frequent the area and dung blows in). This exception is cited from Al-Hidayah. The commentary also provides details: if the animal is removed alive, nuzuh is only required if there is certain impurity on its body or it is naturally impure (e.g., pig). The qualifier 'in it' is agreed upon, meaning the same ruling applies if the animal dies outside and then falls in. The animal must be blood-bearing and non-aquatic; otherwise, its death does not spoil the water (e.g., fish). Finally, swelling (intifakh) and decomposition (tafakhkhukh) are defined, and it is noted that mentioning decomposition is redundant since it is more severe, but it is done to dispel the notion that additional steps (like washing the well walls) are required.

Key Terms
بئر (bi'r): A well; subject to specific rulings when impurity falls in, requiring nuzuh (drawing water) based on quantity and type of impurity.
انتفاخ (intifakh): Swelling of a dead animal's body due to gases; indicates decomposition has begun, triggering full nuzuh of the well.
تفسخ (tafakhkhukh): Decomposition and spreading of body parts; even stronger indication than swelling, also triggers full nuzuh. No additional purification needed beyond drawing water.

Key Takeaway
A dead animal that swells or decomposes in a well requires the entire well water to be drawn, and this obligation overrides the quantity of impurity.

When Must All Well Water Be Drained?
Explains when draining all well water is obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios that require draining all well water and explain the proof from the hadith of Zamzam and the qiyas for similar-sized animals.

Core Idea
Draining all well water is required for human, sheep, dog, or any animal that swells or disintegrates.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a common practical issue: how much water must be removed from a well when a dead creature falls into it. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between large-bodied creatures (human, sheep, dog) and others, and also considers whether the animal has begun to decompose. The proof is a well-known incident in Zamzam, extended by analogy to similar-sized animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories triggering full draining
The text presents two categories: (1) any animal (land-dwelling, blooded) that swells or disintegrates, and (2) a human, sheep, or dog regardless of swelling.
Other animals like chicken or cat require full draining only if they swell or disintegrate.
2
Examine the textual proof (dalil) for the human case
A hadith: an Ethiopian fell into Zamzam, died, and Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr ordered draining all its water. The command 'anzi' (drain) indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
The hadith is ahad but considered qati' al-dalalah due to the clear command.
3
Understand the qiyas for sheep and dog
Sheep and dog are joined to human by similarity in body mass (mugarabat al-insan fi al-jussa). The illa is their large size, making their impurity spread like a human's.
This is qiyas shabah (similarity analogy), explicitly mentioned in the commentary.
4
Apply the condition of swelling for other animals
For animals not human/sheep/dog, full draining is wajib only if they swell (intifakh) or disintegrate (tafsakh). Otherwise, a specified partial amount is drained (e.g., 20 buckets for a chicken).
Swelling or disintegration releases liquid impurity that mixes with all water.
5
Recognize the estimation ruling when full draining is impossible
If full draining is impossible (e.g., well too deep), the amount of water originally present is estimated and removed.
This ensures purification despite practical constraints.

Or a human, or a sheep, or a dog dies – all its water is drained if possible, otherwise what is in it is estimated. [1] His saying: 'or it dies' – the conclusion is that if a human or something similar to him in body like a sheep and a dog and the like dies, the whole is drained even if it did not swell, and other than these, the whole is only drained if it swells, otherwise it has a specified limit. (2) His saying: 'all its water' – i.e. what was in it at the time of falling and death. As for the case of impurity falling, because the well is like a small basin; impurity spoils it, unless it is 10x10 cubits; when impurity falls, all water becomes impure, so draining is obligatory. As for the case of swelling and disintegration, because then a wetness comes out of it, and it is impure and liquid, so all water becomes impure by mixing with it, unlike if it is taken out before swelling. As for the case of a human dying, because it is narrated that an Ethiopian fell into the well of Zamzam in Mecca and died, so Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr ordered draining all its water. Reported by Ibn Abi Shayba, al-Bayhaqi, al-Daraqutni, al-Tahawi, Abd al-Razzaq and others, and some of its chains are authentic, as detailed by Ibn al-Humam and al-Zayla'i. And by way of indication, everything similar to a human in body, like a dog and the like, is joined to the human. [3] His saying: 'what is in it' – i.e. what was in it at the time of impurity falling, as in al-Inaya and al-Binaya and other commentaries of al-Hidaya.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary expanding the matn of al-Wiqaya. It addresses two scenarios that require draining all water from a well. First, if an animal (a land creature with blood) dies in the well and then swells or disintegrates, then all water must be drained. Swelling means becoming enlarged due to gases; disintegration means the parts separate. Even if the animal is edible and its impurity is considered light (like sheep), the entire well must be drained. The author mentions that there is no distinction between a small or large amount of impurity; even a drop of urine, blood, or wine necessitates full draining, except for what is excused by necessity, such as camel or sheep dung that naturally falls into desert wells. The second scenario: if a human, sheep, or dog dies in the well, all water must be drained even if the body does not swell, because these are large in size and their impurity is severe. For the human case, the proof is the hadith that when an Ethiopian fell into Zamzam and died, Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr ordered all water to be drained. This ruling is extended by qiyas to anything similar in body mass, like a dog. If full draining is impossible (e.g., the well is too deep), then the amount of water originally present is estimated and removed. For other animals (not human/sheep/dog), full draining is only required if the animal swells or disintegrates; otherwise, a specified amount of water is drained. The commentary also refines the ruling: if the animal is removed alive, draining depends on whether it had definite impurity on its body or was itself impure. The restriction 'in it' is agreed to cover also dying outside or falling in.

Key Terms
انتفاخ (intifākh): Swelling of the dead animal's body due to gases or decomposition
تفسخ (tafakhkhukh/taksakh): Disintegration or separation of the animal's body parts
نزح الكل (nazḥ al-kulli): Draining all water from the well

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Drain all water because it is an animal
B) Drain a specified amount of water (partial)
C) No action needed because its impurity is light
D) Drain all water only if it is eaten

Key Takeaway
Draining all well water is wajib for humans, sheep, dogs, or any animal that swells/disintegrates; other animals require partial draining unless they swell.

Well Water Purification After Death of Humans and Animals
Ruling and proof for total removal and estimation methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on removing well water when a human, sheep, or dog dies, explain the proof from the Zamzam hadith, and apply the estimation methods for water quantity.

Core Idea
Death of a human or animal of similar size in a well requires total water removal; quantity estimated by two experts.

When a creature dies in a well, the water must be purified. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the size of the creature and whether it has swollen. This passage explains the ruling for humans, sheep, and dogs, and discusses how to estimate the water quantity when total removal is impractical. You will examine the hadith evidence and the preferred method of estimation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
Determine which creatures trigger total water removal: humans and anything similar in size (sheep, dog) even without swelling; smaller animals only if swollen.
The text explicitly includes humans, sheep, and dogs, and extends to similar-sized animals by way of indication (dalālah).
2
Understand the proof
The primary evidence is the hadith of the Ethiopian who fell into Zamzam; the command to drain all water indicates wujub per Hanafi usul (amr implies obligation).
The hadith is reported with multiple chains, some authentic, as detailed by Ibn al-Humam and al-Zayla'i.
3
Distinguish swelling cases
For animals smaller than human size, total removal is only required if the body swells and decomposes; otherwise a specified lesser amount is removed.
Swelling releases a liquid impurity that contaminates all water, unlike removal before swelling.
4
Apply estimation methods
When total removal is impossible, estimate water quantity. The most correct method is to accept the word of two expert men, analogous to testimony and the Quranic command to ask experts (16:43).
Abu Yusuf proposed two methods, but the later preference is the two-expert method per al-'Ayni and Mulla Khusraw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو مات آدمي، أو شاة ، أو كلب ، يُنْزَحُ كلُّ مائها إن أمكن وإلا قُدِّرَ ما فيها
[١] قوله : أو مات ؛ الحاصل أنه إذا مات آدمي أو ما يقاربه في الجنة كالشاة والكلب ونحوهما يُنزح الكلّ وإن لم ينتفخ، وفيما عداها إنما يجب نزح الكل إذا انتفخ وإلا فله حد معين.

Or if a human, sheep, or dog dies, all of its water is removed if possible, otherwise an estimate is made of what is in it. [1] His saying: or died; the summary is that if a human or something similar to it in size like a sheep, dog, and the like dies, all water is removed even if it has not swollen. For other than these, removal of all water is obligatory only if it has swollen, otherwise it has a specified limit. [2] His saying: all of its water; meaning that which was in the well at the time of the fall and death. As for the case of impure substance falling, the well is like a small basin, it becomes spoiled by it, unless it is ten by ten cubits. So by the fall of impurity, all water becomes impure, so removal is obligatory. As for the case of swelling and decomposition, because then moisture comes out of it, and it is liquid impurity, so all water becomes impure by its mixing, unlike if it is removed before swelling. As for the case of a human's death, because of the narration: 'An Ethiopian fell into the well of Zamzam in Mecca and died, so Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr ordered the removal of all its water.' Reported by Ibn Abi Shayba, al-Bayhaqi, al-Daraqutni, al-Tahawi, 'Abd al-Razzaq, and others; some of its chains are authentic, as detailed by Ibn al-Humam and al-Zayla'i. And by way of indication, everything similar to a human in size, like a dog and the like, is annexed to a human. [3] His saying: what is in it; meaning that which was there at the time of the impurity's fall, as in al-'Inaya, al-Binaya, and other commentaries of al-Hidaya. (1) From 'Ata': 'An Ethiopian fell into Zamzam and died, so Ibn al-Zubayr ordered that Zamzam water be drained, but the water did not stop; they looked and saw a spring flowing from the direction of the Black Stone, so Ibn al-Zubayr said: That is enough for you.' In Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar (1:17), Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba (1:150), etc. Ibn Daqiq said in al-Imam: Its chain is authentic as in I'la' al-Sunan (1:264). [11] His saying: and the most correct... etc. Mentioned in al-Hidayah from Abu Yusuf: two aspects: one is to dig a pit similar to the water area of the well and pour into it what is removed until it fills; the second is to send a reed into the well, make the water level a marker, then remove ten buckets, then return the reed, and every time the well water decreases, remove ten buckets for each measure. And al-'Ayni mentioned in Minhat al-Suluk Sharh Tuhfat al-Muluk, and Muhammad ibn Faramuz known as Mulla Khusraw in al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, and others: the most correct and most in accordance with fiqh is to consider the statement of two men who have insight regarding water matters—i.e., they have intuition and intelligence by which they know the quantities of well waters. Because two is the requirement for binding testimony, and because the principle is to refer to people of knowledge, by His saying: 'So ask the people of the reminder if you do not know' (Quran 16:43). And it is known that every field has knowledgeable men. So if their statement establishes that the water in this well is one hundred (for example), that amount is removed. [1] His saying: and Muhammad estimated 200 buckets to 300; meaning he ruled to remove this amount; the 200 is by way of obligation, and what exceeds it up to 300 by way of recommendation. And he chose this...
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on al-Wiqaya, addresses the ruling on well water when creatures die in it. The matn states: 'If a human, sheep, or dog dies, all its water is removed if possible, otherwise an estimate is made.' The commentator explains that for humans and similarly-sized creatures (like sheep and dogs), all water must be removed regardless of whether the body has swollen. For smaller animals, total removal is only required if the body swells and decomposes; otherwise, a specified lesser amount suffices. The reasoning is that a human’s death is analogous to these animals in size, and the proof is the hadith of the Ethiopian who fell into Zamzam, where Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr ordered all water removed—this hadith is cited with multiple chains, some deemed authentic by Ibn al-Humam and al-Zayla'i. The passage then moves to the issue of estimating water quantity when total removal is impossible. The text reports Abu Yusuf’s two methods: (1) digging a pit equal to the water volume and filling it, or (2) using a reed to mark the water level, removing ten buckets each time. However, the preferred view (al-asahh) according to later commentators is to rely on two experts in water estimation, analogizing to the Quranic command to ask the people of knowledge (Quran 16:43) and the requirement of two witnesses. Imam Muhammad provided a fixed estimate of 200 to 300 buckets, where 200 is obligatory and the additional up to 300 is recommended. The passage ends with a note that Muhammad’s estimation was chosen by some, but the text is cut off before completion. The overall ruling: death of a human or creature of similar bulk in a well necessitates removal of all water, with the quantity determined by either expert opinion (two men) or Imam Muhammad’s numerical approximation.

Key Terms
نزح (nazh): Removal or bailing out of water from a well for purification.
بصارة (basārah): Expertise or insight in estimating water quantities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Dig a pit similar to the water area and fill it.
B) Use a reed to mark the water level and remove ten buckets each time.
C) Ask two expert men who have insight into water estimation.
D) Always remove 200 buckets as obligatorily estimated by Imam Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
When a human or animal of similar size dies in a well, all water is wajib to be removed, and the quantity is determined by two trustworthy experts.

Determining Well Water Quantity: The Preferred Hanafi Method
Discussion of the correct method to measure well water and Imam Muhammad's bucket estimate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on determining well water quantity using expert testimony and explain the intra-madhhab differences.

Core Idea
Two expert men determine well water quantity, not fixed bucket numbers.

When an impurity falls into a well, the quantity of water must be known to determine the number of buckets to remove. This passage presents the most correct Hanafi method: relying on two expert witnesses. It also examines other views within the school, including Imam Muhammad's fixed estimate of 200–300 buckets, and grounds the preferred method in Quranic authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the preferred method
According to the text, the most correct method (al-ashbah bi al-fiqh) is to accept the statement of two men who have insight (basara) in water matters. Identify who these experts are and why their testimony is binding.
Two men form the nisab al-shahada (minimum for binding testimony).
2
Contrast with other Hanafi opinions
Examine the two methods transmitted from Abu Yusuf (digging a pit or using a reed) and Imam Muhammad's estimate (200–300 buckets). Understand why the two-expert method was preferred by later scholars.
Imam Muhammad's 200 buckets are wajib; 300 is mustahabb.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof
The preferred method is based on the verse 'Ask the people of remembrance if you do not know' (16:43). Explain how this general command is applied to water experts.
This is a dalil naqli (textual proof) with qati thubut but zanni dalala.

The most correct is to accept the statement of two men who have insight regarding water, and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) estimated two hundred buckets to three hundred. His statement: 'and the most correct...' etc. Mentioned in al-Hidayah from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) two ways: One: that a pit be dug like the place of water in the well, and into it is poured what is removed until it fills. The second: that a reed be inserted into the well, and the water level be marked, then ten buckets are removed from it, then the reed is returned; whenever the water of the well decreases, ten buckets are removed for each measure of it. And al-Ayni (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned in Manh al-Suluk Sharh Tuhfat al-Muluk, and Muhammad ibn Faramuz known as Mulla Khusraw in al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar and others: that the most correct and most similar to fiqh is to consider regarding the quantity of water in that well the statement of two men who have insight in the matter of water; that is, they have intuition and intelligence by which they know the quantities of well waters. For two is the minimum number for binding testimony, and because the principle is to refer to the people of knowledge, by His statement: 'Ask the people of remembrance if you do not know,' and it is known that every field has men knowledgeable in it. So if it is confirmed by their statement: 'The water in this well is one hundred,' for example, then that quantity is removed. His statement: 'And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) estimated two hundred buckets to three hundred'; meaning he ruled to remove this quantity. So the two hundred are obligatory, and what is beyond it up to three hundred is recommended. And al-Nasafi chose this statement in al-Kanz, and in Khizanat al-Muftin: 'Upon it is the fatwa.'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It discusses the correct method to determine the quantity of water in a well when an impurity falls in. The text first states that the most correct view is to accept the statement of two men who have expertise in water. This is the position preferred by later scholars. The passage then mentions two methods transmitted from Abu Yusuf: digging a pit and measuring, or using a reed to mark the water level and removing ten buckets per measure. However, the preferred view according to al-Ayni, Mulla Khusraw, and others is to rely on two expert witnesses, because two is the minimum for binding testimony, and the Quran commands asking the people of knowledge. Imam Muhammad's opinion that a standard well has 200-300 buckets is also mentioned, with 200 being obligatory and up to 300 recommended. Al-Nasafi and the Khizanat al-Muftin adopt this view. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi reliance on expert testimony and the range of opinions within the school.

Key Terms
بصارة (basara): Insight, expertise, or intuitive knowledge about water quantities in wells.
نصاب الشهادة (nisab al-shahada): The minimum number of witnesses required for binding testimony, which is two men.
نزح (nazh): To remove water from a well by drawing buckets.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers relying on two expert witnesses (basara) to determine well water quantity, with Imam Muhammad's fixed estimate as a fallback.

Removal Quantity for Small Birds in a Well
Ruling on removing water after a small bird dies in a well

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the quantity of water to remove when a small bird dies in a well, and evaluate the basis of Imam Muhammad's estimate versus the precautionary view.

Core Idea
When a dove dies in a well, 40-60 buckets may suffice, but total removal is the safer default.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling on purifying a well when a small bird dies inside. The passage presents Imam Muhammad's estimate of 40-60 buckets and contrasts it with the more precautionary view of total removal or expert consultation. You will learn why later Hanafi scholars considered Muhammad's view weak and how to apply these rulings in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
The act is the death of a small bird (e.g., dove or hen) in a well. The default ruling in Hanafi fiqh is that all water must be removed (nazh jami' al-ma') because impurity has been confirmed.
This default is based on the principle that once impurity enters, full purification is required unless a specific exemption is proven.
2
State Imam Muhammad's opinion and its basis
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani estimated 40-60 buckets for such cases. His basis was empirical: he observed that wells in his region (Kufa) typically contained that amount of water.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli), not a transmitted text.
3
Identify the criticism of Muhammad's opinion
Later scholars like the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and al-Mukhtar criticized the estimate for lacking a transmitted proof (dalil sam'i). Since the default is total removal, any limitation to a specific number must come from revelation, which is absent here.
The criticism is methodological: the ruling should not rely on local custom alone.
4
Note the safer alternative views
The alternative is either to remove all water or to delegate the assessment to two expert men (as reported from Nasir ibn Salam). This is considered more precautionary (ahwat) and aligns with the reports from Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubair.
This view is preferred by later Hanafi authorities despite being more burdensome.
5
Understand the epistemological status
Imam Muhammad's estimate is his personal ijtihad (ijtihad al-ra'y), not a transmitted ruling from the Prophet or earlier imams. Therefore, it has lower authority than the default principle of full removal.
This illustrates the hierarchy: textual evidence > rational inference; when textual evidence is lacking, the safer path is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نحو حمامة أو دجاجة ماتت فيها أربعون إلى ستين. قوله : وفي نحو حمامة ؛ هو بالفتح وتخفيف الميم، والتاء فيه للواحدة لا للتأنيث ؛ لأنّ الحمام يطلق على الذكر والأنثى، وكذا تاء الدجاجة، وهو مثلث الدلل ، ذكره الدماميني في عين الحياة»، والدميري في «حياة الحيوان). وقال في «البحر الرائق»: «قد اختلف التصحيحُ في المسألة، والإفتاء بما عن محمد أسهل، والعمل بما عن نصير بن سلام الله له من التفويض إلى رجلين أحوط ؛ ولذا قال في «المختار» : ما عن محمد الله اليسر ، لكن لا يخفى ضعفه، فإنّه إذا كان الحكم الشرعي نزح جميع الماء للحكم بالنجاسة، فالقولُ بالاقتصار على نزح عدد مخصوص من الدلاء يتوقف على دليل سمعي يفيده، وأنى ذلك، بل المأثور عن ابن عباس وابن الزبير خلافه». انتهى وفي «الهداية»، وغيره : «إنّ محمداً لله إنما قدر بهذا لما شاهده في بلده من أنّ الماء في الآبار يكون هذا المقدار غالباً».
And regarding something like a dove or a hen that dies in it, forty to sixty. His statement: 'And regarding something like a dove': it is with fatha and tashdid of the mim, and the ta' is for the singular feminine not for feminization; because 'hamam' is used for both male and female, and likewise the ta' of 'dajaja', and it is like the triple of dal, mentioned by al-Damamin in 'Ayn al-Hayat and al-Dumayri in 'Hayat al-Hayawan. And it is said in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 'Verification has differed in this issue. Giving fatwa according to what is from Muhammad is easier, but acting according to what is from Nasir ibn Salam (may Allah have mercy on him) of delegating to two men is more precautionary. Hence it is said in al-Mukhtar: What is from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) is ease, but its weakness is not hidden, because if the legal ruling is to remove all the water due to the judgment of impurity, then the statement limiting to a specific number of buckets depends on a transmitted proof that indicates it – and where is that? Rather, what is reported from Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubair is contrary to it.' End quote. And in al-Hidayah and others: 'Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) only estimated this because he observed in his country that water in wells is usually this amount.'
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof regarding the number of buckets to remove when a small bird like a dove or hen dies in a well. Imam Muhammad estimated 40-60 buckets. The commentary first clarifies the linguistic form of the words 'hamama' and 'dajaja'. Then it quotes from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that there is disagreement on which position is sahih (correct). Giving fatwa by Muhammad's opinion is easier, but acting on the report of Nasir ibn Salam to delegate to two experts is more precautionary. Al-Mukhtar notes that Muhammad's opinion is ease but weak, because the default ruling is to remove all water once impurity is confirmed; limiting to a specific number requires a revealed proof, which is lacking. Moreover, Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubair opposed it. Finally, al-Hidayah explains that Muhammad's estimate was based on observation of common well sizes in his region, not on a textual proof. This passage shows the tension between ease and precaution in Hanafi fiqh, with the preferred view being the safer one of total removal or relying on experts.

Key Terms
حمامة (hamama): A dove or pigeon; in this context, a small bird that may fall into a well.
دجاجة (dajaja): A hen or chicken; another small bird that may die in a well.
نزح جميع الماء (nazh jami' al-ma'): Removing all the water from the well; the default ruling for purification after impurity falls in.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main criticism of Imam Muhammad's estimate of 40-60 buckets for a dove or hen?
A) It is based on a weak hadith.
B) It is too easy and encourages laziness.
C) It lacks a specific textual proof and relies on observation of local wells.
D) It contradicts the opinion of Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
Imam Muhammad's estimate of 40-60 buckets for a dove or hen in a well is based on observation and is considered weak; the more precautionary view is to remove all water or consult experts.

Pages p. 401 · Taharah — Purification
